# Five-Point Scale

# Chapter 1

The light behind Karen Galloway was wrong.

Nick noticed it before she said anything — the flat midday glare from a window behind and to the left of her, washing her face into a warm-tone silhouette while the bookshelf beyond burned overexposed. The Zoom camera's auto-exposure was splitting the difference and losing. It made Karen look like she was sitting in front of a greenscreen of her own office.

She'd been his editor at Magpie Press for six years. Not his editor — his client. She bought children's book covers from him. Four or five a year, eight hundred dollars each, net thirty days, and she always paid on day thirty-one because she was kind but Magpie's accounts payable was not. She had gray-brown hair and reading glasses she wore on a chain and a habit of tilting her head when she was about to say something she'd rehearsed.

She was tilting her head now.

"So, Nick," she said. "How are you?"

"I'm good," he said. "I'm fine. I've got some sketches for the Barrington thing if you want to —"

"Right, so." Karen adjusted her glasses. "That's actually what I wanted to talk to you about."

He watched her hands. She was holding a pen she wasn't going to use, clicking the cap in and out with her thumb. The sound came through the laptop speakers a half-second late, a tiny rhythmic pop.

"We've been looking at some new tools," Karen said. "For the illustration side. And I wanted to loop you in before we made anything official."

"OK."

"There's a program called Midjourney. Have you heard of it?"

He had. He'd seen the weird hands on Twitter, the glossy fantasy forests in art feeds, people posting prompts like recipes. It had all lived on the other side of the screen, far enough away that he could close the tab and go back to painting.

"Yeah," he said. "I've seen some stuff."

"So we've been running some tests with it, and the results have been — I mean, honestly, Nick, they've been really impressive. For what we need, which is covers, you know, that hit a certain look —"

"You're replacing me."

Karen's head untilted. She blinked. The pen stopped clicking.

"That's not — I wouldn't put it that way."

"How would you put it."

"We're shifting our cover illustration pipeline to AI-generated imagery, and we won't be commissioning new work from freelance illustrators going forward." She said it all in one breath, and he realized she'd memorized it. Someone had written that sentence for her. It had the cadence of a legal department.

"OK," Nick said.

"It's nothing about the quality of your work," Karen said. "You know we love what you do. The Barrington covers were gorgeous."

She said *gorgeous* and kept clicking the pen cap. The word didn't slow her down.

"Sure," Nick said.

"It's a cost thing, honestly. The program can generate dozens of options in an hour. We can iterate in real time. And the cost per image is —" She stopped herself. She had the decency to stop herself.

"Less than eight hundred dollars," Nick said.

"Significantly."

He looked at the screen. Karen's face, overexposed and slightly pixelated. The bookshelf behind her with its row of Magpie Press children's titles, the spines he could read from memory because he'd painted six of them. The light behind her was doing something to the edge of her hair, a warm halo effect that in a painting he would have rendered with raw sienna thinned almost to a glaze. He noticed this. He noticed he was noticing it.

"OK," he said again. "When?"

"Effective immediately, I think. The Barrington project we'll handle internally."

"You already have the covers."

"We have — we've generated some options, yes."

"Can I see them?"

Karen hesitated. "I don't think that would be productive."

"Karen, I've done six covers for that series. I just want to see what you're going with."

She shared her screen. Three covers appeared in a grid. They were for a middle-grade fantasy novel about a girl and her fox, the seventh in the Barrington Chronicles, which Nick had been illustrating since the third book. He'd already done the preliminary sketches. The fox had a scar over its left eye that he'd established in book three and carried through every cover since.

The AI covers were good. Not great — the fox's proportions shifted between images in a way that suggested the model had no spatial memory, and in the second image the girl's left hand had a thumb growing out of the wrong side of the palm — but the colors were right, the mood was right, the shelf appeal was there. They looked like what they were: competent, fast approximations of the thing he'd spent six years building.

The fox didn't have the scar.

"These are nice," he said.

"I know it's a lot to process."

"No, they're — the palette's good. Whoever prompted it knew what they were doing."

"That was our new art director, actually. Sarah. She's been really getting into the tool."

"Cool."

He kept looking at the girl's impossible thumb. It came out of the palm on the wrong side and folded inward toward the wrist. Nobody at Magpie had noticed. Nobody at Magpie would notice. The colors were right. The fox looked soft. That was enough.

"Nick, I want you to know that if we ever —"

"Thanks, Karen. I've gotta go."

He closed the laptop. He sat at his kitchen table, which was also his painting table, which was also his desk, which was a door he'd found on the sidewalk in 2019 and set across two sawhorses. The surface was covered in dried acrylic and gouache, a geological record of three years of covers and personal work. Cadmium yellow under cerulean blue under a rust-colored drip of burnt sienna he'd never been able to identify the origin of.

The kitchen faucet dripped. It had dripped since August. The landlord had sent a maintenance request form that Nick had filled out and returned, and then nothing had happened, and the form's carbon copy still hung on the refrigerator under a magnet from a pizzeria that had closed.

He put his head in his hands. His hands smelled like linseed oil and the granola bar he'd eaten for lunch. His right index finger had a small white scar from an X-Acto knife, from cutting mats for his last gallery show at Vera Lux — a small space in Temescal that showed emerging painters alongside whoever's ceramics they were rotating through that month. He'd sold one piece out of nine. The other eight were against the wall behind him, wrapped in the plastic sheeting he'd bought at Blick for $14.99 a roll.

He sat there for a while. The light in the apartment was flat and gray, the November overcast that came through the east windows as an even, diffuse wash — good painting light, actually, the kind that eliminated harsh shadows and let you see color values without distortion. He'd moved here for the light. Three years ago, when Pendragon and Magpie were both steady and $14,000 a year felt like it could almost work if he was careful. The light was still here. Everything else had changed.

A BART train passed on the elevated track outside the window, a low rolling thunder that shook the single-pane glass in its frame and then was gone and left the room quieter than before.

---

The overdue rent notice was on the counter, under a coffee mug he'd put there to stop looking at it. He looked at it now. Eastshore had stapled a new sheet over the old one: PAY $1,100 BY DECEMBER 1 TO AVOID FURTHER ACTION. The older notice underneath still showed last month's date. They had not stopped him from being behind. They had just compressed the problem into six days. His checking account had $1,340 in it. After rent, he'd have $240. He was supposed to have gotten another $800 from Karen for the Barrington sketches — preliminary work she'd already approved, work that was already serving as the basis for whatever prompts Sarah the new art director had typed into Midjourney.

He pulled the notice out and read the whole thing. The language was firm but not yet legal. *Please remit balance within six (6) days to avoid further action.* Further action meant eviction proceedings. He'd looked it up once, late at night, with the notice propped beside the keyboard and his checking balance open on the screen. In Oakland, the process took about two months if the landlord was motivated. His landlord was a property management company called Eastshore Holdings that communicated exclusively through form letters and a voicemail box that had been full since July.

He opened his laptop and checked the balance anyway. $1,340. There was a direct deposit from Magpie for October's cover — the last one, the Barrington book with the ice cave — and not much else. He'd painted the cave's interior light as a pale blue-violet, the color of glacier ice when you're deep enough inside that the sunlight has to travel through six feet of compressed snow to reach you, and the light that survives is stripped of everything except its shortest wavelengths. He'd been proud of that one. Twelve hours of work. Eight hundred dollars, minus self-employment tax.

Nothing else was coming in. He had not had a second client since Pendragon Books dropped their illustration budget in March. Before that, it had been Pendragon and Magpie, roughly $14,000 a year between them if he worked steadily, which he had, for four years. Fourteen thousand dollars a year to paint children's book covers in a city where his studio cost $13,200 a year in rent alone.

The math had never worked. He'd known it didn't work. He'd subsidized the gap with canvas sales that came irregularly and sometimes not at all, and with a brief experiment selling prints on Etsy that netted $216 over four months, and by not going to the dentist, and by eating the way he ate, which was cereal and ramen and whatever fruit was cheapest at the Mi Pueblo on International Boulevard.

He walked through the apartment. It didn't take long. The studio was one room — a large one, high ceilings and two windows facing east, which was why he'd taken it. The morning light was the best he'd found in Oakland, a pale clear thing that came straight off the hills and filled the room without hot spots. He'd set his easel by the east window. His paint supplies occupied a baker's rack he'd found at a garage sale: tubes of oil and acrylic arranged by hue the way another person might organize spices, jars of medium, linseed oil, turpentine, a coffee can of brushes ranging from a size-0 detail round to a wide flat he used for priming. The supplies were worth roughly $600 if he replaced them at retail. More than what would be left after rent. More than this month's groceries would come to.

Against the north wall, his paintings. Nine from the Vera Lux show — landscapes of the Oakland hills, the bay, the salt flats near Alviso, a series he'd called *Terrestrial* that his friend Jess said was the best work he'd done. Nine paintings, framed, wrapped in plastic, unsold. Plus a few dozen smaller works, unframed, stacked in portfolios and leaning against the baseboard. The gallery had posted them all on their website when the show went up a year ago — thumbnail images with his name and the gallery's watermark and prices he'd already reduced twice. The website was still live. He assumed nobody was looking at it.

He stood in front of the stacked paintings and tried to see them fresh. The hills at golden hour, the fog coming through the Gate at an angle that bent the light into three distinct temperature zones — warm, cool, warm again — that had taken him two weeks and six attempts to get right. The salt flat at Alviso where the dried mineral deposits read as pure white from a distance but up close turned out to be a dozen colors: rose, ochre, a pale chemical green he'd mixed from viridian and zinc white and never been able to replicate.

They were good paintings. He could point to the parts that held: the fog caught at the ridge, the salt crust going pink at the edges, the strip of dead grass cutting the foreground in half. None of that had made anyone buy them. He'd priced the Alviso piece at $1,200 for the show. Then $900. Then $750, which was what the gallery's owner suggested, gently, over email, with a smiley face that did nothing to soften the math. Seven hundred fifty dollars for three months of work, and still nobody had bought it, and it leaned against his wall gathering a fine layer of Oakland dust on the plastic wrap while the gallery's website displayed its thumbnail to whatever indifferent algorithm happened to crawl by.

His phone buzzed. A text from Jess: *How'd the Karen call go?*

He typed *Fine* and deleted it. Typed *She's switching to AI* and deleted that too. Typed *Call you later* and sent it and put his phone facedown on the counter.

Jess was at a residency in New Mexico, three months of making work in the desert, a grant she'd applied for six times before they gave it to her. She was still painting. She was still broke, same as him, but she was painting every day and posting the work and it was good and she was getting somewhere, or at least she was getting somewhere adjacent to somewhere, which in the art world was almost the same thing. She would understand about Karen. She would be kind about it. Her kindness would make it real in a way that right now, five hours after the call, it still didn't feel.

He poured a bowl of Cheerios. The milk was dated five days ago but smelled fine. He ate standing at the counter, looking at the rent notice, his spoon clinking against the ceramic in the quiet apartment. Outside, another BART train. The windows rattled and settled.

---

He started browsing at eight. By midnight he'd seen enough.

The AI art forums were hell. Not because the people on them were wrong — some of them were, the ones who posted generated portraits of women with eleven fingers and called it art — but because enough of them were right. The technology was improving at a rate that made his stomach feel like it was being lowered slowly on a rope.

He found a subreddit where someone had posted a side-by-side comparison of Midjourney v3 and v4 outputs, the same prompt run through both. The v3 images had the soupy, dreamlike quality he'd seen before — faces that smeared, hands that bloomed into tentacles, backgrounds that dissolved into color noise. The v4 images looked like photographs of things that existed. Three months between versions. Three months, and the hands had gone from five-to-eight fingers to five-to-six, and the faces had gone from melted to merely uncanny, and the backgrounds had snapped into focus with a spatial coherence that would have earned a passing grade in a first-year perspective class.

He typed his own descriptors into the Midjourney Discord's prompt bar. Not his name — that wasn't how it worked, he knew that much — but the words that described what he did. *Landscape painting, Northern California, golden hour, muted palette, painterly brushwork, atmospheric perspective.* He hit enter. Four images appeared in under a minute.

They were better than he wanted them to be.

The first was oversaturated — the orange in the hills was Photoshop-sunset orange, not the dusty warm gold he actually mixed from yellow ochre and a touch of cadmium red — but the composition was sound. The horizon sat at roughly the right height, the negative space in the sky had weight, and the foreground had a convincing depth-of-field softness that he'd spent years learning to achieve with thin washes layered wet-on-dry. The second was generic, a hotel-hallway landscape from Reno. But the third was good. The third stopped him.

It showed hills that could have been the East Bay from the Berkeley side, lit from behind by a low sun, with a fog bank rolling through a gap in the ridge. The color temperature was right — that specific warm-cool layering he'd painted a hundred times, the thing that happened in November when the sun dropped low enough to turn the hills into a backlit scrim and the fog caught the remaining light and held it. The surface texture wasn't brushwork — no dragged bristle, no paint skipping over canvas tooth, just a repeating pattern sitting where brushwork should have been. But the light. The light was close to the thing he'd spent his adult life trying to render.

It had taken forty-seven seconds.

He sat back. The chair was a twenty-dollar IKEA piece whose pneumatic cylinder had failed two years ago, so it sat at its lowest height and his knees came up almost to the table. He was aware, distantly, that his back hurt, and that noticing his back was a way of not thinking about the image on the screen.

He clicked into the prompt-sharing threads. The part that got to him wasn't the images. It was the notes underneath. People were sorting outputs the way a tired art department might sort comps at midnight: this one sells, this one doesn't, lighting slips here, the hand's a mess, reroll, upscale 2, blend 4's background with 1's palette. The language was ugly and procedural, but the work inside it was familiar. Somebody still had to decide what counted as close enough.

He kept going. Reddit threads, Twitter, the Stable Diffusion subreddit. He scrolled past an image of a woman whose hair turned into a waterfall, which was kitsch, and an image of a medieval battle scene where every soldier had the same face, which was eerie, and an image of a bowl of fruit so photorealistic it took him ten seconds to find the failure — a shadow cast at an angle that implied two light sources in a scene that only had one. In the prompt guides and Discord chats, the language kept repeating: better anatomy, cleaner lighting, stronger prompt match; rank these, flag that, pick the least broken one and send it on.

He found galleries of AI-generated art that people had submitted to juried competitions — and won. He found a thread where a stock photographer described, with financial specificity Nick recognized because it matched his own, how his licensing income had dropped from $60,000 a year to $8,000 in eighteen months. He found a thread where a concept artist at a game studio described being handed a folder of AI-generated images and told to "clean these up" for a quarter of her previous rate. He found a thread where a Midjourney power user explained how to get "in the style of" living painters — not by name, which the filters caught, but by describing their habits in enough detail that the model could fake them. Someone had done this with Sargent, with Thiebaud, with Richter. The results were imperfect and recognizable and getting better monthly.

At 2 AM he made more cereal. Honey Nut Cheerios, the last of the box. He ate it over the keyboard, which was a thing he did that had already killed one laptop. The apartment was cold — November in Oakland wasn't freezing but the Victorian's single-pane windows and absence of insulation made the difference between inside and outside a matter of six degrees, and running the baseboard heater for more than a few hours showed up on the PG&E bill in a way he couldn't afford. He pulled a flannel over his T-shirt. The flannel was brown and black plaid, from a Goodwill in Temescal, and it had a small tear at the left elbow he'd been meaning to sew shut for a year.

At 3 AM he found the data-labeling companies.

He wasn't looking for them. He was reading a long technical post about how image-generation models were trained — jargon he mostly couldn't follow, loss functions and gradient descent and terms that read like a foreign language — and the article mentioned "human evaluators." They weren't engineers. They were people who looked at batches of generated images and scored whether the hands made sense, whether the lighting held together, whether the image actually matched the prompt. Those scores, the article said, were fed back into the next training run. The models learned which outputs were good and which were bad because people kept telling them.

He read the paragraph three times. Then he searched for the companies. Scale AI. Surge AI. Appen. A dozen smaller ones. They hired people to rate images, flag errors, and sometimes write a sentence about why an output failed. The job postings asked for visual literacy, attention to detail, and familiarity with digital media workflows. Some of them mentioned art backgrounds. One listed basic Python as a plus. Another said people who could handle internal tools had room to move up.

He stared at the screen. The glow lit the apartment in a blue-white wash that turned the stacked paintings on the north wall into dark rectangles, all their color gone. The BART tracks were silent at this hour. The faucet dripped its eleven-second interval — he'd counted once, during a late night of painting, and now he couldn't stop counting it.

The plan arrived the way paint mixed on a palette — not a thought but a physical event, two colors pushed together producing something neither was alone. His anger, which had been shapeless and ambient since the Zoom call, met the information on the screen and acquired form.

If the system learned from human ratings, then the ratings were the part he could get his hands on. Not by acting like an idiot. That would get him fired in a week. He would have to look competent and be wrong in ways that passed for judgment: give an almost-right hand the benefit of the doubt, call broken light plausible, mark an image as matching the prompt when the important thing in it had quietly slid sideways. It couldn't be garbage. It had to be drift.

The postings had already shown him the access path. They all wanted versions of the same thing: basic Python, comfort with internal tools, visual literacy, attention to detail. He could fake enough code to get hired onto an evaluation team. Once he was in, he could call the backward thumb broken, the fruit-bowl shadow wrong, the missing fox scar a miss. People who caught those things got room.

He reopened three job tabs — Scale AI, Surge AI, Appen — and copied the overlapping requirements onto the back of the rent notice in block capitals: PYTHON. IMAGE QUALITY. DETAIL. DIGITAL MEDIA WORKFLOWS. Then, underneath them, because he wanted to see how stupid it looked in writing: GET INSIDE.

It was 4 AM. The November sky through the east windows was black and featureless, hours from light. The apartment was cold and his cereal bowl was empty and he had $1,340 in checking and $1,100 due to Eastshore in six days, and the plan was either the smartest thing he'd ever thought of or the stupidest, and he sat there at his salvaged-door table with dried paint under his elbows and he didn't care which.

He opened a new tab and typed *learn Python free.* Then he clicked the first thing that came up.


# Chapter 2

The first tutorial was called "Python for Absolute Beginners" and it was taught by a kid who looked fifteen. He had a Minecraft poster behind him and a mechanical keyboard that sounded like a popcorn machine, and he said "super easy" eleven times in the first four minutes. Nick counted. He was sitting on his bed with his laptop balanced on a stack of library books because his desk was covered in half-squeezed tubes of cadmium yellow and a palette he hadn't scraped in two weeks, and the kid was saying that a variable was where the program kept something for later.

Nick understood putting things somewhere and finding them again. He did not understand why `x = 5` meant x equaled five, but `x == 5` was a question about whether x equaled five, and `x = x + 1` was apparently not a mathematical impossibility but just a normal thing that happened in programming all the time.

He rewound the video and watched it again. The kid said "super easy" and Nick paused and went to the kitchen and ate a handful of dry Cheerios from the box because he'd run out of milk two days ago and hadn't gone to the store because the store required spending money, which was a resource he was managing with the same precision he used to manage cadmium red — sparingly, with the awareness that once it was gone, it was gone. He still had the $1,340 that had been in checking at four in the morning, plus the $950 balance-transfer check he'd finally caved and deposited after dawn, one of the glossy credit-card traps that arrived in the mail promising relief up front and catastrophe in smaller print. The app said $2,290. For a second it looked like a usable number. Then it turned back into borrowed money and six days until Eastshore wanted $1,100.

He went back to the video.

The kid explained `if` statements. If a condition was true, the code inside the block would run. If it wasn't, it wouldn't. Nick wrote this down in a notebook — an actual notebook, a Strathmore sketchpad he'd been using for thumbnail compositions, the good paper wasted on his handwriting. `If True → do thing. If False → don't do thing.` He understood this conceptually. Humans operated on if-statements. If the light was good, he'd paint. If the client paid, he'd eat something other than cereal. If Midjourney hadn't eaten his career, he wouldn't be watching a child explain logic gates.

The problem was typing it. His fingers knew where the brushes went — the index finger guiding a No. 6 round, the pinky bracing on the mahlstick, the thumb controlling pressure on a palette knife. They did not know where the brackets went. He typed `if x = 5:` and the interpreter threw an error. He typed `if x == 5;` and it threw a different error. He typed `if x == 5:` and it worked and he felt a jolt of something that might have been accomplishment except it had taken him fourteen minutes to write a line that the fifteen-year-old had produced in two seconds while talking about his weekend.

By the end of the first week, Nick could print "Hello, World" to the console, define a function that added two numbers, and write a for-loop that counted to ten. He could not tell you why any of this was useful. The tutorials kept saying that programming was about solving problems, but the problems they presented — reversing a string, summing a list, finding the maximum value in an array — were problems that had already been solved, by the people who wrote the tutorials. He was not solving anything. He was copying.

This was, he realized, not entirely unfamiliar. Art school had started the same way. You copied the masters before you developed your own voice. You drew from casts of classical sculpture, reproduced Bridgman's anatomy plates, mixed colors to match Zorn's limited palette. The difference was that when he'd copied a Sargent brushstroke at twenty, his hand had understood what it was doing even when his brain hadn't caught up. There was a physical intelligence in the wrist, a feedback loop between the mark and the eye. The keyboard had no equivalent. He typed characters and stared at output and the connection between the two was entirely abstract, running through a machine he could not see and did not understand.

He found a Reddit thread called "How long does it take to learn Python?" The top answer said six months to a year for proficiency, two to three years for mastery. Nick had budgeted himself six weeks.

The second week was worse. He moved from YouTube to an online course called Codecademy that let him write code in a browser window and see the results immediately, and the immediacy was useful but also punishing, because every mistake was visible in real time. He misspelled `print` as `pirnt` and spent twenty minutes thinking the problem was logical rather than typographical. He forgot a colon at the end of a function definition and the error message said `SyntaxError: expected ':'` and he stared at it like it was written in Cyrillic. He tabbed out to Google "what does SyntaxError mean python" and the answer was that his syntax was wrong, which was like going to a doctor and being told the problem was that he was sick.

His apartment was cold. The building's heating was a shared boiler that the landlord ran on some schedule Nick had never been able to predict — warm at three in the afternoon, freezing at ten at night. He wore a flannel over a hoodie over a T-shirt and his fingers were stiff on the keys. The paint smell was fading from the room, or he was getting used to it, which felt like the same thing. The stacked canvases against the far wall — nine landscapes from the series that hadn't sold at the Temescal gallery, plus three unfinished commissions from the publisher who'd dropped him — caught the light from his laptop screen at odd angles and looked like a low wall around his living space, a barricade of failed productivity.

He didn't paint. Painting required a mental state he could not currently access, something between confidence and surrender, a willingness to make a mark without knowing where it led. Programming required the opposite: knowing exactly where it led before you made the mark. Every line had to be right before you wrote the next one. It was architecture, not painting. It was building from a blueprint, not building from a feeling.

He hated it. He kept going.

---

Mid-December he found LeetCode. Someone on a coding forum had said it was the standard preparation for technical interviews — a database of programming problems ranked Easy, Medium, and Hard, where you could write solutions and test them against hidden inputs. The interview-preparation industry around it was enormous: YouTube channels, courses, books, all dedicated to teaching people how to solve problems they would never encounter in actual jobs, in order to get hired for those actual jobs. It was, as far as Nick could tell, the most elaborate gatekeeping mechanism since the French salon system, and it ran on the same logic: prove you can do this arbitrary thing nobody cares about, and we'll let you into the room where the real work happens.

He started with Easy problems. Two Sum: given an array of integers and a target, find two numbers that add up to the target. He stared at it for forty-five minutes and wrote a solution that used two nested loops and was, according to the discussion forum, the worst possible approach in terms of efficiency but technically correct. He submitted it. It passed. He felt nothing.

The next problem was Reverse Integer. Given a 32-bit signed integer, reverse its digits. He spent two hours on it, most of that time trying to understand what "32-bit signed integer" meant and why it mattered. His solution failed on negative numbers. He fixed it. Then it failed on numbers that would overflow when reversed. He didn't know what overflow meant. He Googled it. He fixed it. Then it failed on zero. He submitted a version that passed all the test cases and looked at the discussion forum, where someone had solved the same problem in three lines that he couldn't parse.

The array-reversal problem broke him the first time. And the second time. And the third. It wasn't hard — he knew what reversing an array meant, he could see it in his head, the sequence of values flipping like a row of paint swatches held up to a mirror. But translating that visual understanding into a sequence of operations that a machine could execute required a kind of thinking his brain resisted the way oil paint resisted water. The logic was sequential where his thinking was spatial. He wanted to see the whole array at once, grasp its shape, and rearrange it. The computer wanted him to describe, step by step, how to swap individual elements, keeping track of indices and bounds and temporary variables, and the bookkeeping of it felt like being asked to describe a sunset by listing the wavelengths of each photon.

He failed it seventeen times. Seventeen attempts across eight days, each one revealing a different edge case or logical error he hadn't anticipated. On the eighteenth attempt, he passed, and his solution was so ugly — forty lines of code for a problem that could be solved in three with a built-in method he hadn't known existed — that he closed the laptop and walked around the block in the rain.

It was raining in Oakland nearly every day now, the December drizzle that turned the streets into mirror-gray and made the light flat and directionless, the kind of light he used to love for painting because it eliminated harsh shadows and let you see the true local color of everything. He walked past the taqueria on International that was always open, past the laundromat where he hadn't gone in three weeks because quarters cost money, past the Walgreens where he'd bought the ramen he was going to eat for dinner. His hands were in his pockets and they ached from typing in a way they'd never ached from painting — a tightness in the fingers, a soreness in the wrists, the specific discomfort of holding the same position for hours without the variation that brushwork demanded.

He went home and opened LeetCode and started the next problem.

By Christmas he had developed a method that was not comprehension but was something adjacent to it. He memorized solution patterns the way he'd once memorized color swatches — not understanding the chemistry of why cadmium and ultramarine produced a particular gray-green when mixed, but knowing that they did, and knowing when to reach for it. If the problem involved searching a sorted array, you used binary search. If it involved counting occurrences, you used a hash map. If it involved comparing strings, you usually needed two pointers. He didn't understand why these patterns worked. He understood that they worked, and he could recognize which situations called for which pattern, and that recognition — the visual artist's deepest skill, the ability to see similarity across difference — was the thing that saved him.

He could not, however, generate novel solutions. If a problem didn't match a pattern he'd memorized, he was dead. And the gap between matching a pattern and understanding why it worked meant that any variation — a different constraint, a larger input, an edge case the pattern didn't cover — could blow up his solution in ways he couldn't debug. He was functioning at roughly a twenty-percent success rate on Easy problems by mid-December, which was not enough for anything.

Christmas Eve. His neighbor two doors down was hosting a party. The bass came through the wall in a steady pulse that Nick could feel in his sternum, not quite loud enough to distinguish the song, just a heartbeat of someone else's good time. He was sitting cross-legged on the floor because his back hurt from hunching over the laptop on the bed and the chair at his desk was buried under canvas stretchers. The desk itself was still covered in paint tubes. He hadn't touched them. The cadmium yellow was cracking where the cap had been left loose, the pigment drying into a crust that he'd need to cut away if he ever opened it again. If.

He ate ramen — the good ramen, the Shin Ramyun packets from the Korean grocery on 14th that cost a dollar twenty each, which was his Christmas dinner splurge. He put an egg in it because he'd seen a TikTok that said you could crack an egg into the boiling noodles and it would poach, and it didn't poach so much as disintegrate into stringy white filaments that floated in the broth like something medical, but it was protein and it was hot and he ate it standing at the kitchen counter because the table was his laptop now.

The party thumped. Someone laughed loud enough to hear through the plaster. Nick opened LeetCode and pulled up the Two Sum problem again — not because he hadn't solved it, but because he'd solved it badly and he wanted to understand the hash-map solution that everyone in the discussion forum treated as obvious. He worked through it line by line, forcing himself to name the job of each step before moving to the next one: store the number, note where it was, check whether the missing number had already shown up. Halfway through, the logic stopped feeling like a magic trick and started feeling like procedure. It was still slow. It still wouldn't survive a timed interview. But when he ran it, it worked for reasons he could almost say out loud.

He solved it. Then he got through Valid Parentheses and Merge Two Sorted Lists the same ugly way: not by seeing the answer whole, but by refusing to skip steps. Every time he jumped ahead, he broke something. Every time he stayed inside the sequence, the code eventually stopped breaking. The process was so slow that it would be completely useless in a timed interview, but it was working, and he was learning, and the party next door was getting louder, and it was eleven forty-five on Christmas Eve and he was alone on the floor of an apartment that smelled less like turpentine than it had a month ago, solving problems that a teenager could solve in his sleep, and he thought: this is insane. This is the stupidest thing I've ever done. He didn't stop.

New Year's came and went without his noticing. He'd fallen asleep on the floor at eleven-thirty on the thirty-first, laptop still warm on his thigh, and woke at two in the morning to distant fireworks and the taste of stale ramen. He'd been dreaming in loops: moving the same values around, losing his place, starting over, never reaching the green check.

January was colder and darker and he stopped leaving the apartment for anything other than groceries. He showered less. His laundry piled up in the closet. He wore the same flannel for four days running because it didn't smell yet and nobody was around to notice. By then he had run $2,990 through his checking account: the original $1,340, the first $950 balance-transfer check, then a second one for $700 when the first trap stopped feeling like a trap and started feeling like groceries. December had taken $800 through the rent portal. January had taken another $600. PG&E got enough to keep the lights on. The rest went in twenty-dollar leaks: eggs, rice, black beans, ramen, toilet paper, dish soap, one roll of quarters for the laundromat that he stretched until he was wearing underwear with the elastic going loose. By the last week of January his checking account sat at $206, which meant he was eating rice and eggs and the occasional can of black beans, which was fine, which was survivable, which was the kind of arithmetic he'd been doing since the publisher dropped him and which had become so automatic he could calculate the cost of a grocery run to the penny while walking the aisles. A dozen eggs, $3.49. Rice, the twenty-pound bag from the Asian grocery, $18.99, which would last three weeks. Shin Ramyun five-packs, $5.49, a luxury he rationed. He'd stopped buying coffee and was drinking the instant Folgers that someone had left in his cabinet months ago, the crystals clumped and slightly off, but caffeine was caffeine when you were up at midnight fighting with a linked-list problem that wanted you to reverse a sequence of nodes and you couldn't figure out why your pointers kept pointing at nothing.

By late January, his success rate on Easy problems had climbed to roughly forty percent, with the crucial caveat that he could only solve problems he recognized as variations of patterns he'd already memorized. Anything genuinely novel still destroyed him.

On January 27th he gave Longest Substring Without Repeating Characters three hours and got nothing out of it but a deeper appreciation for the ways a person could be too stupid for a window. The tutorials called it a sliding-window problem, as if that explained anything. He understood the rule in plain English. Keep the run of letters that still worked. Drop the ones that broke it. But the computer did not care what he understood in plain English. It wanted indices. It wanted a left pointer and a right pointer and a set that grew and shrank at the proper times. He wrote one version that worked for `abcabcbb` and broke on `bbbbb`. He wrote another that passed both and failed on `pwwkew`. He wrote a third that looped forever on one of the hidden tests until the site timed out and told him, in a neat gray font, that his submission had exceeded the time limit.

He closed the tab and saw the others behind it: the Canopy posting he'd been keeping open for two weeks, his checking account, and an email from Eastshore that began REMINDER in all caps. The bank app still said $206. He had eight eggs left, half a bag of rice, and enough instant coffee for maybe four mornings. He could not code his way out of that by becoming incrementally less bad at LeetCode over the next month.

He opened the Canopy listing again. Basic Python or JavaScript. Comfort with repetitive technical tasks. Background in visual arts preferred. Preferred. Not required. He read that line three times, then opened the resume draft he'd been torturing for three days.

The first line said `Freelance Painter and Illustrator.` He changed it to `Visual Content Creator and Evaluator` and immediately wanted to put his fist through the screen. Under skills he already had Python typed, which was a lie only in magnitude. He added JavaScript because he'd spent two nights copying beginner exercises into a browser console and because every job-forum ghoul on the internet said keywords mattered more than shame. He stole `visual quality assessment` straight from the Canopy posting. In the field for relevant experience he turned gallery work into `visual content creation and evaluation` and freelance illustration into `client-facing creative project management.` It was all technically true in the way that a Mercator projection was technically a map — it represented the territory, just with significant distortion in the areas that mattered.

He stopped with the cursor over Submit. On the desk beside the keyboard, a tube of cadmium yellow had skinned over at the mouth. The half-finished Montclair hills leaned against the wall with the foreground still blank. Up to this point the plan had been something he could rehearse in private, an insomnia project, a stupid vindictive shape in his head. If Canopy wrote back, it would stop being private. He would have to keep lying forward. He would have to hand this résumé version of himself — visual content creator, client-facing, technically useful — to the company he wanted to get inside and foul from the middle.

He clicked Submit.

The confirmation page said his application was under review and that Canopy typically responded within two weeks. He stared at the screen for a while. Then he closed the laptop and looked at his apartment — the stacked canvases, the paint supplies he'd shoved to one end of the desk to make room for a keyboard, the Cheerios box on the counter, the latest overdue rent notice he'd pinned under a magnet so he wouldn't lose it, which meant he saw it every time he went to the kitchen. Outside, it was raining again, the flat gray Oakland rain that made everything look like a painting he would never make.

His phone rang. It was Jess.

---

"It's so dry here," Jess said. "Like, my hands are cracking. I brought three tubes of lotion and I've gone through all of them."

"Where are you again?"

"Taos. Well, outside Taos. The residency's in this old adobe compound about twenty minutes from town. There's like six of us. Mostly painters, one sculptor who makes these incredible carved things out of cottonwood root."

"Nice," Nick said. He was sitting on the floor again, back against the bed frame, phone on speaker beside him. The laptop was open on the floor too, LeetCode still loaded, but he'd turned the brightness down.

"The light here is — God, Nick, the light. It's like painting in a different atmosphere. Everything has this golden — I don't know, I keep wanting to say warmth, but it's more than warmth, it's like the air itself has a color temperature. I've been doing these sunset studies and the chromas are so high they'd look fake if you put them in a painting back home."

"Sounds good," Nick said.

"I mean, it's also freezing at night and the plumbing is from like 1940 and I've been eating a lot of beans. But the work's going well. I finished six pieces since November. Small ones, but."

"That's great."

"What about you? You painting?"

Nick looked at the canvases stacked against the wall. The top one was a half-finished landscape of the Montclair hills he'd started in October, before the phone call from the publisher. The sky was blocked in with a wash of cerulean cut with raw umber, which was a color he liked for Oakland's overcast, and the hills were roughed in with a green so neutral it was almost gray. He'd been planning to spend a month on the foreground, layering the grasses and the light-struck eucalyptus that lined the ridge. He hadn't looked at it in six weeks, really looked at it, the way you had to look at a painting in progress to figure out where it was going.

"Not as much," he said.

"You OK?"

"Yeah, I'm fine. Just, you know. Broke."

"Yeah." Jess was quiet for a second. "Me too. The residency covers housing and studio but I'm still paying rent on my place in Portland. Mike's supposedly subletting but he's been weird about the deposit. I might have to go up there in February and deal with it."

"That sucks."

"It does suck. But the work's good, so." She paused. "You doing commission stuff? Did you hear back from that children's book thing?"

"That fell through."

"The Midjourney thing?"

"Yeah."

"Fuck. Nick, I'm sorry. They were one of the good ones."

"They were the only one."

Jess was quiet again, longer this time. Nick could hear wind on her end, which meant she was outside, probably standing on the porch of the adobe compound, watching the last light on the mountains because that was what Jess did — she went where the light was, and she stood in it.

"Are you gonna be OK?" she said. "Like, money-wise?"

"I'm figuring it out."

"Because if you need — I mean, I don't have much, but I could—"

"I'm fine, Jess. I'm applying for some stuff."

"Art stuff?"

"Yeah," he said. "Sort of."

"OK." She didn't push. This was one of the things Nick liked about Jess — she trusted you to tell her the truth and didn't dig when you gave her something that smelled like a lie. It was also, right now, the thing that made talking to her impossible. She was in New Mexico making paintings and eating beans and her hands were cracking from the dry air and she was doing the thing they'd both said they'd do when they were twenty-two, which was make art no matter what, even when no matter what meant beans and bad plumbing and $800 a month scrounged from commissions and teaching gigs and the occasional grant. She was living the plan. He was abandoning it.

"I should go," Jess said. "There's a thing tonight, one of the other residents is cooking and we're all supposed to bring something and I was going to bring wine but there's no wine in this town under fifteen dollars so I'm bringing tortilla chips."

"Classy."

"Extremely." She laughed. "Hey. Come visit if you can. You'd love the light."

"Maybe," Nick said.

"I mean it. There's always room."

"I know. Thanks."

"Love you, weirdo."

"Love you too."

He hung up and sat there on the floor for a while with the phone warm in his hand. He could still hear Jess's voice in his head, the specific brightness she got when she talked about light, the same brightness she'd had at twenty-two when they were both in the foundation program and everything was going to work out. The laptop screen had gone to sleep, and in the dark glass he could see the reflection of his apartment — the stacked canvases, the desk he'd half-cleared for a keyboard, the window where the rain was doing what the rain did. His hands were in his lap and they looked wrong to him, the calluses from brush handles still there but softening, the paint stains fading from the cuticles, the small scar on his right index finger from the X-Acto knife he'd slipped with three years ago while cutting a mat for a frame. They were becoming someone else's hands.

He still hadn't told Jess about the plan. He wasn't going to, not now, not with the Canopy application already sitting in somebody's queue and the whole idea still too thin to survive being said aloud.

He opened the laptop. The screen woke to LeetCode and an unsolved problem called Valid Anagram, which asked whether two strings contained the same characters in different arrangements.

Nick looked at it. He knew the surface version immediately: same letters, different order, yes or no. He could see that part. He could always see that part.

He tried the blunt version first. He would sort the letters and compare them. He typed `if sorted(s) == sorted(t):` and ran the sample case. The sample case came back green. He deleted it anyway.

The version he was supposed to understand was the hash-map version. It counted frequencies. It moved step by step. He typed `counts = {}`. Then he typed `for char in s:` and `counts[char] += 1`.

He ran it. `KeyError: 'a'`

Nick stared at the screen. Of course there had to be a way to tell the dictionary a letter existed before asking it to add one. Of course he didn't know it yet.

He flexed his right hand, put his fingers back on the keys, and started the problem over from the top.


# Chapter 3

The interview was at ten. Nick woke at five-thirty because he had never actually fallen asleep — he'd spent the night on his back in his Fruitvale studio, running LeetCode patterns through his head while the windows buzzed with each BART train on the elevated track outside, the glass vibrating at a frequency he could feel in his molars. He was not preparing. He was tracing preparation instead of doing it: the movements were the same, and the risk was gone.

He showered with the water pressure his building provided, which was the pressure of a person who was trying to be polite about it. He dressed. He examined himself in the bathroom mirror, which was small and spotted and mounted over a sink that shared counter space with the kitchenette because this was a five-hundred-square-foot studio where everything shared space with everything. He looked like a man who painted for a living and did not make a living doing it. Dark hair past his ears because haircuts were nineteen dollars and that was a decision he kept deferring. Hazel eyes dark-smudged underneath from chronic undersleep. He had chosen the green flannel — his best, purchased at a Goodwill on MacArthur Boulevard for seven dollars a year and a half ago, all buttons present, no visible stains under overhead light. Beneath it, a black t-shirt he had audited for damage at six AM and cleared.

He had paint under two of his fingernails. Cadmium red, which was the last color he'd used three weeks ago on a canvas he'd since turned to face the wall. He went at the nails with a toothpick, standing at the sink, scraping pigment from the keratin with the precise, practiced motion of someone who had done this before job interviews and gallery openings and dates and every other occasion where the visible evidence of his work was a liability rather than a credential. The red came off one nail. The other held. He gave up and decided no one was going to be looking at his fingernails at a tech company interview, and then immediately revised this to: he didn't know what anyone looked at in tech company interviews. He had never been to one. He was thirty-four years old and the closest he had come to a corporate interview in his adult life was when he'd talked his way into a teaching-assistant position at CCA by bringing a portfolio and a willingness to mix paint for the department chair.

He ate half a granola bar standing in the kitchen, looking at his stacked canvases against the far wall. Nine unsold paintings from the Temescal gallery show, plus six older ones, plus three he didn't count because they were bad. Eighteen paintings. Not one of them had ever sat in a queue on someone's screen waiting to be rated on a five-point scale. That was not a thought he was having yet. That thought was coming.

He drove south on 880 toward San Leandro in the gray January light, his Civic shuddering in the left lane at sixty-two miles an hour, which was its current maximum comfortable velocity. The engine had been making a sound since October that he'd been classifying as harmless by the usual method: he refused to think about it too carefully. The windshield crack, which had started as a chip in July, now ran diagonally from the lower left to a point roughly behind the rearview mirror, bisecting his view of the road into two slightly misaligned planes. He kept meaning to get it replaced. He kept not having three hundred dollars.

The San Leandro business park was off Davis Street, a collection of two-story office buildings set back from the road behind parking lots. The buildings were beige stucco with tinted windows, built to house whoever signed the lease — dental practices, insurance adjusters, now, apparently, a company that trained the AI systems that had replaced him. Nick parked his Civic in the far corner of the lot, near a landscaping island where three ornamental pear trees had been pruned into identical lollipop shapes. They stood in a row with the disciplined geometry of objects that had been told what to look like. It was the sort of pruning that made him think about bonsai, about topiary, about the human insistence on making living things hold still.

He turned off the engine. The shudder traveled up through the steering column and died. His hands were not steady. This was not because he was afraid — or it was, but the fear was specific, not general. It was exam fear, narrow and humiliating: he had crammed the gestures without learning the material, and the whole point of the room was to measure that gap.

He locked the car with the physical key because the remote had died two months ago and he had not replaced it. He walked across the lot. The air was damp, Bay Area January cool — not cold enough for a coat, not warm enough to forget the sky was gray. The clouds had the flat, textureless quality of a ground that had been mixed too long, all the color stirred out until only a neutral remained.

The lobby was small. Gray carpet, a reception desk, two chairs, a ficus in a pot that was either real and struggling or artificial and convincing, and he couldn't tell which from the door, which bothered him. A woman behind the desk was eating a Kind bar and scrolling her phone. She had dark hair pulled back and a Canopy lanyard over a sweatshirt.

"Nick Bray? Evaluator interview?"

"Yeah, that's me."

She tapped her screen. "Kevin's running about five behind. Have a seat."

He sat. The chair was gray fabric on chrome legs — the standard waiting-room chair, present in every DMV and urgent care and oil-change place in America, designed to be sat in without being noticed. On the wall opposite him hung a framed photograph of a mountain range printed at a resolution just low enough that the ridgelines looked soft, as if the mountains had been photographed through a window that needed cleaning. The frame was hung a degree and a half off level. He knew the measurement because fifteen years of hanging paintings in galleries had given him a plumb line behind his eyes that he could not turn off, and the tilt of this frame was going to sit in the back of his skull for the entire time he was in this room.

He took out his phone and opened his notes. LeetCode patterns, organized by type in neat little categories: *Two-pointer for sorted arrays. Hash map for frequency counting. For loop with enumerate when you need index and value. List comprehension as shorthand for simple transformations.* He scrolled through them and the words coated the surface of his brain and went no deeper. He could reproduce these patterns. He could not explain them from the inside. He could imitate them. There was a word for that in painting: *pastiche*. He was going to pastiche his way through a coding interview.

He closed the app. His hands smelled of linseed oil — not because he'd been painting but because fifteen years of painting had saturated the skin so thoroughly that the smell had become constitutional. He had scrubbed them twice with dish soap this morning. The linseed oil remained. It was him now.

"Nick?" A man appeared in the doorway that led to the offices. Mid-thirties, brown beard trimmed to a length that communicated grooming without vanity, wearing a quarter-zip pullover with the Canopy logo embroidered on the left chest in tech-company teal, meant to signal approachability without sacrificing authority. He carried a laptop under one arm and a ceramic coffee mug that said WORLD'S MOST ADEQUATE DAD. "I'm Kevin. Come on back."

The interview room was windowless, ten by ten, lit by a fluorescent panel that cast the bluish wash Nick associated with convenience stores and the less optimistic corridors of public hospitals. The walls had the tired white of a place no one had repainted in four years — the original coat gone faintly yellow, like a canvas that's been left out. There was a whiteboard on one wall, clean, and a table with two chairs facing each other. Kevin sat on one side and placed his laptop between them. Nick sat on the other side and put his phone facedown on the table because he didn't know what else to do with his hands.

"OK, so," Kevin said. He pulled up something on the laptop. "The evaluator role has two parts to the interview — a technical assessment and a visual-analysis section. We'll start with the technical stuff. Week one on the floor is mostly browser work: rating image batches, tagging defect categories, writing notes somebody else can audit. But the queue also kicks out CSV exports and duplicate pulls, and we need evaluators who won't get stranded when that stuff goes sideways. You said on your application you have Python experience?"

"Self-taught," Nick said. "Past couple months." He said this with the casual tone of a person who had been teaching himself Python for two months, which was accurate, and who had absorbed the language with reasonable proficiency, which was a lie of such magnitude that it required the casual tone to hold its shape.

"Cool. Yeah, we're not looking for software-engineer-level stuff. Just making sure you can handle the tooling, basic data cleanup, that kind of thing." Kevin turned the laptop so Nick could see the screen. A shared coding environment — white background, blinking cursor, a problem statement in gray text at the top. "You can type your solution right in there, or if you want to talk through your approach first, that's fine too. Whatever works."

Nick read the problem.

*Given a list of integers, return a new list containing only the elements that appear more than once. Example: [1, 2, 3, 2, 4, 3] → [2, 3]*

He had done this. Not this exact problem but one shaped like it — a frequency-counting problem, the kind where you build a dictionary and then filter. He had drilled this pattern on at least six LeetCode problems and the structure sat in his hands already, the stored motion of a familiar brushstroke. He knew the ingredients. He was somewhat less certain he could combine them without burning the kitchen down.

He started typing. The laptop keyboard had the shallow, clicky travel of all modern laptop keyboards, nothing like the mechanical keyboard the YouTube tutorial kid had used, and his fingers — trained for sable brushes and palette knives — landed on the keys with the imprecise confidence of someone who had learned to type adequately but not well.

```python
def find_duplicates(nums):
    counts = {}
    for num in nums:
        if num in counts:
            counts[num] += 1
        else:
            counts[num] = 1
```

This was the part he had memorized. These seven lines existed in his fingers as muscle memory, drilled there through repetition, the old art-school method of drawing the same hand until the ratios stuck. They were not understood so much as absorbed. The next part — extracting the results — he also knew. He typed it.

```python
    result = []
    for num in counts:
        if counts[num] > 1:
            result.append(num)
    return result
```

He looked at it. It sat on the screen with the quiet self-assurance of something that was probably correct. Kevin leaned forward and read through it.

"OK, that works for the basic case," Kevin said. "Good. But I don't want the unique duplicate values. I want every repeated occurrence after the first, in the order the repeats happen. So if the input is [1, 2, 3, 2, 4, 3, 3], I want [2, 3, 3]. Your current version collapses that last three."

Nick stared at the code he'd written. The answer felt nearby. He knew it sat between two choices, but he couldn't find the ratio. He didn't need the totals anymore. He needed the moment a number became a repeat, and then the moments after that, and the exact difference between those two things kept sliding away when he reached for it.

He typed a line. Deleted it. Typed a different line. The cursor blinked with the patience of something that could wait forever.

"So append on the second hit," Nick said. "And then — no, wait. On the third too."

"Yeah," Kevin said. "Try it."

He started over instead of editing. On the first pass he appended a number only when its count hit two, which fixed the sample in his head and failed Kevin's test case by dropping the extra `3`. Then he changed the condition to `>= 2` and, a few lines later, turned `result` into a set because he was suddenly afraid of returning duplicates inside the duplicates problem. He stared at `list(set(result))`, understood that he had just erased the requirement he was trying to meet, and deleted half the function. At that point the code on the screen looked like a painting he'd overworked: too many layers, each one laid down to correct the one before, the result muddy and unconvincing.

"I'm going in circles," he said.

Kevin nodded. "That's OK. Let's look at one more."

He pulled up the next problem. *Given two sorted lists of integers, merge them into a single sorted list.* Nick read it twice. He understood what it was asking. He had seen something like this — the two-pointer technique, where you walked through both lists simultaneously, comparing elements and building the result. He had it written in his notes. He had watched a teenager on YouTube explain it in a video called "EASY Two Pointer — You Got This!!" He had not got it.

He wrote the function signature and a while loop and the two pointer variables. This much was scaffolding he could erect from memory. Then he needed the comparison — if the element in list one was smaller, take it; otherwise take the other — and he wrote that part, but he forgot to handle the case where one list was shorter than the other, and the function crashed when he tried to access an index that didn't exist. Kevin pointed at the screen. "You're going past the end of one of the lists there."

"Right." Nick looked at the code. He added another conditional. The conditional was wrong. He could see it was wrong in the same way he could see when a drawing was off — the proportions didn't feel right — but he couldn't see *how* it was wrong, which was the difference between having an eye and having a skill. Having an eye without the skill was like knowing a painting was bad without being able to fix it. Except he could always fix a painting.

He typed another line. The cursor blinked. His face was warm. The fluorescent light was doing something to his perception of time — the minutes were both too fast and too slow, all of it moving in the warped tempo of a doctor's waiting room, accelerating through the parts he wanted to last and decelerating through the parts he wanted to end.

"Let's move on," Kevin said. He sounded used to candidates stalling out in front of him, used to the little humiliation of being there to watch it. "The image work is the actual job. The scripts are mostly for when the queue gets weird. I think you'll find this part more in your wheelhouse."

Nick nodded. His neck was damp. The room was the same temperature it had been twenty minutes ago but it felt closer now, the walls nearer, the fluorescent tube louder. He was aware that he had, in the space of thirty minutes, demonstrated the coding ability of a person who had been coding for two months, which was exactly what he was, and that this demonstration was almost certainly disqualifying. He was aware of this and also aware that being aware of it was useless, like being aware that a painting was overworked while you were still overworking it.

Kevin closed the coding environment and opened something else — a different application with a grid of thumbnailed images on a dark background. He clicked one and it expanded to fill the screen. An AI-generated portrait of a woman sitting at a desk. At first glance it looked like a stock photo — soft light, centered composition, a smile borrowed from corporate headshots.

"So these are sample outputs from a few current generative models," Kevin said. "We use a five-point scale for evaluation. I'll show you each image and just have you talk me through what you see. We're looking at anatomical accuracy, lighting consistency, compositional balance, and stylistic coherence. There's no wrong answer — I just want to hear your process."

Nick looked at the image. His eye moved.

It always moved in the same sequence when he was looking at a representation — the initial gestalt first, the overall impression that lasted half a second, and then the disassembly, the piece-by-piece interrogation of how the picture had been made. This was not a decision. This was not a skill he was performing. It was what his brain did when an image hit it: light, anatomy, perspective, then whatever feeling survived that first pass. He had been doing this since he was nineteen and it was as involuntary as blinking.

"Her left wrist," he said. "No, the whole forearm into the wrist. It's rotated too far. If she actually held it like that, she'd be in pain." He leaned closer. "And the shadow under her jaw's wrong. The key light is upper left, but the shadow runs right. So either there's another source off-frame or that shadow belongs to a different face."

Kevin looked at the image. He looked at his own screen — an answer key, Nick guessed. He scrolled.

"And the desk," Nick said. "The cup ellipse says we're looking down at it. Her face is eye level. Those vanishing points aren't coming from the same viewer."

Kevin's scrolling had stopped. "We had... the wrist. That's in the key. The lighting and the desk perspective aren't."

Nick didn't know what to do with this, so he waited.

Kevin clicked the next image, then stopped with his hand on the trackpad. He opened a different folder Nick couldn't read from across the table and brought up a landscape — mountains, a lake, a small cabin in the midground. The colors were saturated and the composition had the canned pleasantness of a jigsaw-puzzle box.

"The atmospheric perspective's wrong," Nick said. "Sorry — the back mountains. They should go cooler as they recede. More blue, less contrast. Those peaks are carrying the same chroma as the trees up front, so the whole thing flattens out."

He leaned forward. "And the reflection doesn't match the building. You're seeing the underside of the eaves in the water like the viewer's below the surface, but the cabin itself is eye level." He pointed at the screen. "Then the light shifts. Cabin says late afternoon. Mountain says noon. Those can't both be true."

Kevin checked his screen once, then again. "Shit."

Nick looked at him.

"We use this in onboarding," Kevin said. "Day two calibration. The key calls it a four with mild saturation issues."

Nick looked back at the lake. "Then that's a bad training image."

Kevin kept staring at the screen. "We've been using this deck since November," he said, mostly to himself.

"Where'd you go to school?" Kevin asked.

"CCA. California College of the Arts. I was a painter."

"You were a painter."

"Am. Was." Nick caught himself. "Fine arts. BFA."

"And you're applying for the evaluator role."

"Yeah."

There was a beat. Kevin looked at the screen where Nick's failed merge function still sat in the coding environment, then at the landscape with its impossible light, then at Nick.

"Can you wait here for a minute? I want Priya to look at something."

"Sure."

Kevin left. The door closed with a soft click and Nick was alone in the windowless room with the fluorescent light and the whiteboard and the two open applications — one showing the code that didn't work, the other showing the lake they were apparently using to train people. He looked at both. The gap between them was the whole interview.

He sat with his hands on the table. The linseed oil smell was there. The paint under his remaining fingernail was there. The fluorescent tube above him had a slight flicker at its left end, a pulse of dimmer light every three or four seconds that was probably invisible to anyone who hadn't spent years working under artificial illumination, adjusting colors under gallery spots and studio lamps and the blue-white glare of hardware-store fluorescents. Nick watched it pulse and listened to Kevin's voice in the hallway, indistinct, talking to someone whose responses he couldn't hear.

He had come here because he wanted a login badly enough to sit through humiliation for it. He wanted the queue, the rubric, the shared folder full of images somebody inside this company had already decided counted as truth. He wanted a chance to push wrong answers into that pile on purpose and watch what the machine learned from them. If this was the closest he got, it was still closer than he'd managed from his apartment floor with LeetCode open and the trains rattling the windows.

The door opened. Kevin came back with a woman in black slacks and a navy cardigan, a little too put together for this business park. Dark hair was pinned back off her face, early gray showing at the temples. Her shoulders sat high with tension. She carried a tablet and did not sit down.

"Priya Acharya," she said. "I run the evaluation floor here." She looked at the laptop screen — the lake, the failed merge function, both at once. Then she looked at Nick. "Kevin says you flagged things our key missed. And one of our onboarding calibrations."

"A few, yeah."

"Five," Kevin said from the doorway. "On two images. One of them is in the training deck."

Priya set the tablet on the table and swiped to an image. A woman's face — close-up, photorealistic, generated. Young, looking slightly to the right, dark hair, a neutral profile-photo smile that went strange the longer you looked at it.

"This split a batch this morning," Priya said. "One evaluator passed it, one kicked it to review. If it lands in your queue, do you pass it or fail it? Start wherever your eye starts."

Nick looked. His eye was already moving.

"I'd fail it," he said. "The left eye first. Her left. The iris sits low against the lid. Not enough that you'd name it fast. You just get that something's off." He tilted his head, checking the face from a slightly different angle, the old easel habit. "And the ear. Sorry, the tragus. The little flap in front of the canal. It's flipped. Wrong side. If the close-up anatomy's that loose, I don't care if the thumbnail reads clean."

Priya narrowed her eyes and brought the tablet closer to her face. She was quiet for a moment.

"Hair's good, though," Nick said. "Really good. Somebody spent time on how it catches light at the part. Hair's easier than ears, but still."

Priya lowered the tablet. She looked at Kevin. He gave one small nod without leaving the doorway.

"Kevin's notes say your Python is rough," Priya said.

"That's the polite version," Nick said.

Priya rested two fingers on the edge of the table. "Here's the job as it actually is. Week one is mostly browser work. You rate batches, tag defect categories, write rationale notes somebody else can audit. The Python part is CSV cleanup and queue triage when exports break. Can you do that part alone right now?"

"No," Nick said.

"He got through the frequency part," Kevin said. "The merge was—" He tilted his hand. "Same mistake a few different ways. I'd pair him with somebody on the script side for a couple weeks."

Priya was quiet for a moment. "That's training hours I don't really have." She looked at Nick. "Can you learn the interface fast if someone shows it to you once?"

"Yeah."

"Can you be on-site here every day?"

"Yeah."

"OK." She tapped the tablet once against the tabletop. "We're finishing interviews tomorrow, and HR has to clear references before anything is formal. I can't promise you a seat today. But if we move ahead, it's twenty an hour, full-time, Monday orientation at eight with Lisa. Would that work for you?"

"Yeah," Nick said. Then, because the speed of it sounded desperate: "Yeah. I can do that."

"Good. Leave two references with reception before you go. Make sure they answer unknown numbers." She gathered the tablet. "Kevin, send me your notes."

She left with the same clipped efficiency she'd entered with.

Kevin walked him out. Through the hallway, past closed doors and the muffled percussion of keyboards, past a water cooler with a half-empty jug, past a door that was open enough to show the room behind it. Nick looked. Rows of workstations. Dual monitors at each one. The bluish glow of screens in a room lit by more of the same fluorescents. People sitting, looking, clicking. Someone stood and stretched. Someone else was eating from a Tupperware. The sound of the room was a low, collective murmur — the hum of HVAC and the rustle of work being done, of images being opened and assessed and rated and closed, the soft repetitive click of mice in a rhythm that was almost but not quite synchronized, like rain on a roof.

At the front door Nick said, "Sorry about the merge function."

Kevin pushed the door open. "Keep your phone on."

"Is that real or interview politeness?"

Kevin lifted one shoulder. "It's not nothing."

Nick pushed through the glass door and walked into the parking lot. The sky was the same gray. The three ornamental pears stood in their clipped shapes. His Civic sat where he'd left it, dented passenger door, spreading windshield crack, the look of a car that had already decided how much longer it was willing to function.

He unlocked it with the key. He got in. He sat behind the wheel and put his hands on it.

They were shaking.

He gripped the wheel tighter to make them stop. They didn't stop. He could feel the vibration in the leather, which was cracked and sun-bleached and had been gripped by his hands a thousand times on the drive between his apartment and wherever he was trying to sell work or buy supplies or meet someone who might pay him to paint. A car pulled into the lot and parked three spaces over. A woman got out and walked toward the building with her badge already in her hand.

If Priya passed on him, he went back to Fruitvale and back to LeetCode and back to pretending rage counted as a plan. If she didn't, he'd be in that building on Monday with a badge and a login, close enough to give bad ratings on purpose and see what stuck, close enough to teach their machine the wrong wrist, the wrong light, the wrong face.

He sat there until the shaking backed down to a tremor. Then he started the engine and drove home.

The next two days dragged. He kept his phone faceup beside the sink while he reread Python notes he still didn't understand and tried not to do rent math with money he did not have. Unknown numbers usually meant collection calls or someone from Kaiser asking for a copay. On Friday at 4:12, when a San Leandro number lit the screen, he let it ring once before he answered.

"Nick?" Priya said. "It's Priya Acharya from Canopy."

"Yeah."

"We'd like to make you an offer for the evaluator role. Recruiting just sent the paperwork. It's contingent on the standard background check and your I-9 documents, but assuming those clear, orientation is Monday at eight with Lisa. Pay is twenty an hour. Can you confirm?"

Nick looked at the wall where his canvases were stacked face-in, all that dead weight of stretched linen and failed rent. "Yes," he said. "Yeah. I can do Monday."

"Good. Check your spam if the email doesn't show up. There are a lot of forms."

The packet hit his inbox before she hung up: offer letter, tax forms, confidentiality agreement, badge photo instructions, parking map, a PDF called Data Integrity Standards. He opened that one before the tax forms. It listed falsified ratings, misuse of internal tools, deliberate degradation of label quality, and immediate termination for any of it. He read the phrase deliberate degradation twice. The company already had a bureaucratic name for the thing he wanted to do. By the second read it sounded almost stupid enough to work.

He took the badge photo against the kitchen wall because it was the only blank surface in the apartment. The upload tool rejected the first image for shadows and the second for low resolution. On the third try his face came back to him in a little square with a green check mark under it, approved for printing.

He texted his two references after midnight: please answer if San Leandro calls, please say I can learn fast, please don't ask why yet.

He had the weekend to dig up his Social Security card from the drawer with old gallery postcards and dead paint pens, sign seven PDFs, and get himself through the door on Monday.


# Chapter 4

The building was the color of spackle. Not on purpose — the stucco had probably been white once, or off-white, or whatever the developer's spec had called it twenty years ago. Now it was the gray-beige of anything left too long under Bay Area weather, the same non-color as the February sky above it and the parking lot in front of it and the trimmed junipers lining the walkway that some landscaping contract maintained at the bare minimum of alive. Nick sat in his Civic and checked the address on his phone against the number bolted to the facade. Canopy AI, Suite 200. Second floor. He'd imagined something sleeker — glass, maybe, or at least a lobby with a logo on the wall, a place that looked like it was building the future rather than leasing space between a property management firm and something called Pacific Synergy Solutions.

The lobby directory board used white plastic push-in letters, the kind that dentist offices used. He took the stairs because the elevator had an OUT OF ORDER sign taped to it with blue painter's tape that had been there long enough to curl. The stairwell smelled like industrial floor cleaner and carpet adhesive, and the railing was the brushed-steel kind that collected palm grease. The door to Suite 200 was propped open with a rubber wedge. Inside, a woman at a reception desk looked up from her monitor.

"Nick Bray?"

"Yeah."

"You're onboarding with Lisa. Have a seat."

The reception area had four chairs with chrome tube frames and thin cushions in a shade of blue that didn't exist in nature — too saturated for sky, too cold for hydrangea, the pure product of a plastics catalog. Behind the desk, the wall displayed the Canopy AI logo — a stylized tree canopy in corporate green — and below it, a framed poster that read THE HUMAN LAYER in sans-serif type above a stock photo of diverse hands reaching toward a glowing screen. Nick gave it the same look he gave all commercial art: a brief, involuntary flinch when the shadows were wrong. The hands in the stock photo were lit from two incompatible angles, composited from separate shoots. A small thing. Nobody in the lobby would notice it. The bad shadows still snagged his eye.

Lisa from HR had reading glasses on a beaded chain and a desk stacked with manila folders that tilted slightly, one pile leaning against a mug that said WORLD'S OKAYEST MANAGER.
