She walked Nick through the paperwork at a practiced clip, tapping each form once before sliding it across the desk: W-4, I-9, direct deposit form, NDA, employee handbook, emergency contact sheet. Nick filled them out one by one. He wrote his apartment address on the emergency contact line because he couldn't think of anyone within a ten-minute drive who would come if something happened to him. When he signed the NDA — a standard nondisclosure agreement covering "proprietary evaluation methods, training data, and client relationships" — the pen hesitated for a fraction of a second over the signature line, a pause so small nobody watching would have marked it and so large, from inside it, that it felt like standing at the edge of something. He signed it. Lisa didn't look up.

She gave him a lanyard with a badge — his photo from the interview, overexposed by the flash, making him look startled and slightly washed out — and walked him to the evaluation floor.

The floor was a single open room that took up most of the second story. Nick stood at the threshold and his painter's eye did what it always did with new spaces: it catalogued the light first, then the geometry, then the surfaces, then the life moving through them. The light was institutional fluorescent, the flat shadowless wash that killed depth perception and turned human skin the color of uncooked dough. It came from recessed panels in a drop ceiling with pocked, water-stained tiles — the light had no direction, no warmth, no quality at all. It was illumination without character, the visual equivalent of white noise. The geometry was a grid: six rows of workstations, four to a row, each station a desk with dual monitors and a rolling chair. The rows were separated by aisles wide enough for a person to pass but narrow enough that you'd brush someone's chair if you weren't careful. The surfaces were beige carpet, walked thin and dark in the traffic lanes, and grayish-white walls bare of anything except a whiteboard near the entrance covered in numbers and acronyms Nick didn't recognize. No art. No posters except the one in reception. No windows except at the far end of the room, where a strip of glass showed the parking lot and, beyond it, the concrete ribbon of the 880 overpass.

Twenty-some people sat at the stations. Most wore headphones. Most stared at their screens with the particular inward focus of people doing work that demanded attention but not engagement — the look of someone reading for information, not pleasure. A few murmured into headsets. One woman in the second row had a nursing textbook propped open beside her keyboard, stealing glances between image assessments. A heavyset man in what looked like a FedEx uniform sat at the end of the first row, still wearing his jacket, apparently having come straight from another job. The room hummed — not from conversation but from the machines, the monitors and overhead lights and the HVAC pushing recycled air through ceiling vents with a faint, constant whistle. Below the hum, the staccato click of mice. Open, assess, rate, click. Open, assess, rate, click. The rhythm of it was quiet but detectable, audible only in a room with nothing louder.

"This is where the magic happens," Lisa said, without inflection, and handed him off to his team lead.

---

Priya Acharya was waiting for him at the whiteboard. She was around forty, with dark hair showing early gray that she hadn't bothered to dye, and she wore a blazer over a cotton blouse, dark jeans, flats that looked new — a half-step too professional for the business park. She'd dressed for the job, not the setting. She shook his hand and smiled. It carried warmth, but it also had the smooth edge of repetition, the habit of making a new person comfortable fast without turning intimate. A teacher's smile, Nick thought.

"Nick, right? I'm Priya. I run the image-evaluation team here. The interview loop makes everybody talk to too many people, but day to day you're with me." She gestured at the room. "Not much to look at, but the monitors are decent. Let me show you your station and introduce you to the team."

His station was in the fourth row, middle seat. The desk wobbled when he set his bag on it, and the chair had a cracked left armrest wrapped in duct tape. Two monitors: the left displayed the Canopy evaluation portal, a gray interface with a sidebar of queue categories and a main panel that said AWAITING LOGIN in plain type. The right monitor showed reference materials — rubric guides, annotation standards, a Slack window with a channel called #sl-eval-floor.

"You'll be sharing the row with Dani, Tommy, and Bao," Priya said. "Dani's on her way. Tommy's —" She looked around the room, didn't find him. "Somewhere. And Bao's right here."

She turned to the station beside Nick's, where an older man sat with his back very straight and his face close to the screen. He wore reading glasses on a thin chain, and the fluorescent light caught them as he tilted his head to study whatever was on his monitor. His clothes were neat — a pressed button-down tucked into dark slacks, the creases still sharp, not expensive but carefully maintained. On the desk, beside the keyboard: a dented steel thermos and a small spiral notebook with a pen clipped to its cover. Nick noticed his hands first, as he always did with people's hands — compact, still, precise. Even at rest they looked arranged. He could imagine them lifting a thing, considering its weight, and setting it back exactly where it belonged.

"Bao, this is Nick. He's starting today."

Bao turned from his screen and looked at Nick through the reading glasses. His face was compact and weathered, and his expression was not unfriendly but not rushed either. He studied Nick for a moment — really looked, with the patient scrutiny Nick was used to aiming outward and not used to receiving — and then nodded once.

"Welcome," he said. His voice was quiet, precise, accented in a way that softened consonants. "The chair is bad. If you put a book under the left front leg, the wobble stops."

"Thanks."

"Mm." Bao turned back to his screen, where Nick could see, briefly, an image of a human figure standing in a doorway — something generated, slightly wrong around the elbows. Bao tilted his head the other way and did not click anything.

Priya led Nick past Bao's station to the next one down the row, which was empty except for a jacket draped over the chair. "That's Dani's spot. She's usually early, but—"

"I'm here, I'm here." A voice from behind them, slightly breathless. Nick turned and saw someone cutting between the rows of stations with a paper coffee cup in each hand and a phone clamped between shoulder and ear. He was young — early twenties, thin, with a self-maintained buzz cut and dark circles under his eyes that looked less like a bad night and more like a permanent condition. His jacket was thrift-store corduroy, worn over a t-shirt with a faded graphic that might have been a band logo or might have been an art school print, impossible to tell. He held one of the coffee cups out toward Priya without pausing whatever conversation he was having on the phone.

"Uh-huh. No, I know. I *know*, but — Devon, it said two-hour, not two-hour-except-Tuesdays. That's not — OK. OK, yeah, I'll call them. Yeah. Bye." He pocketed the phone and looked at Nick. "Hey. You're the new guy?"

"Nick."

"Tommy." Quick handshake, slightly damp from the coffee cups. He turned to Priya. "Got you the oat milk one."

"You didn't have to do that."

"I know, but I felt guilty about yesterday."

"What happened yesterday?" Nick asked.

"Nothing," Priya said.

"I was late," Tommy said. "Like, aggressively late. Like, not-in-the-building-until-ten-fifteen late. I locked my keys in my car in the parking lot and had to wait for Triple A, and by the time they showed up I was just sitting on the curb out there like—" He waved a hand. "Anyway. What'd you do before this?"

The question arrived fast enough that Nick had to make a choice in real time. He'd rehearsed a version — vague, something about freelance work and looking for something steadier — but what came out was closer to the truth than he'd planned.

"I was a painter. Canvas, oil paint. Landscapes, some illustration work."

"Sick." Tommy said it casually, without surprise, as if this were the expected answer. "I dropped out of RISD after three semesters. Priya used to teach high school art, so technically she's the success story." He took a sip of his coffee.

"That's not what that means," Priya said.

"No, but it's funnier."

"Point is," Tommy said, already turning toward his station, "you'll fit right in. Whatever that means here."

He sat down and put headphones on, and Nick stayed in the aisle a second longer. He looked around the room again, not at the architecture this time but at the people inhabiting it. Priya still carried the teacher's ease she'd brought to the whiteboard. Bao bent toward his screen with a patient, deliberate care Nick associated less with office work than with layout tables and drafting lamps. Not everyone on the floor belonged to the same story. The woman with the nursing textbook clearly didn't. The man in the FedEx jacket probably didn't either. But enough of them did. He knew that quiet already — studio classes, gallery crits, the residency mixer where he'd met Jess in what felt like a different lifetime. It was the quiet of people for whom looking was a skill, not a default.

He sat down at his station. The fluorescent lights made a faint, high-pitched buzz above him that he would stop hearing within the hour.

---

Dani Solis arrived a few minutes later, cutting through the rows without breaking stride. The turns around chair backs and desk corners looked memorized below the neck. She was around thirty, dark hair pulled up in a clip with a few loose strands, and she carried a canvas tote bag with a child's drawing sticking out of the top — heavy crayon lines, something with four legs and what might have been a tail. She wore work boots and jeans and a flannel layered over a dark tank top, and when she set the tote under her desk she did it with the care reserved for something that contained a thing you cared about.

"Hey," she said to Bao, who nodded without turning from his screen.

She noticed Nick. "You're new."

"Nick. First day."

"Dani." She sat down and logged in, and Nick watched her hands on the keyboard — not deliberately, just an old reflex from years of looking at hands for a living. Under two of her fingernails, in the cuticle lines, he could see the remnants of pigment. Not polish. Paint. Green and something darker, possibly burnt umber. The kind of stain that didn't come off with soap, that you had to let grow out or scrub away with turpentine, and that most painters stopped trying to remove because it became part of your hands, part of the evidence that you worked with color. She caught him looking and he glanced at his own screen.

"Priya do your walkthrough?" she asked.

"Yeah. Showed me the station, the portal."

"She give you the rubric?"

"She said after lunch."

Dani pulled open her desk drawer and handed him a laminated card, restaurant-menu-sized, printed on both sides. "Here. Quick version. The full rubric's on the portal, but nobody reads the full one."

The card listed five evaluation categories in bold:

**Prompt Adherence** — Does the generated image match the text prompt?
**Anatomical Accuracy** — Are human/animal forms structurally correct?
**Lighting Consistency** — Is the light source coherent across the image?
**Compositional Balance** — Does the image hold together as a spatial arrangement?
**Stylistic Coherence** — Is the visual style unified throughout the image?

Each category was rated one to five, with descriptor anchors at each level. Nick read them and felt a sensation he couldn't immediately name — something between recognition and wrongness, like hearing a melody he knew played in a key that was slightly off. These were the things he already looked at. These were the things his eye did without being told, had done since his first figure-drawing class at twenty. Anatomical accuracy. Lighting consistency. Compositional balance. The rubric was a painter's vocabulary run through a corporate grinder, stripped of nuance and flattened into integers. Everything he knew how to see, reduced to a number between one and five.

"Pretty self-explanatory," Dani said. "The hard part isn't knowing what to look for. It's calibrating where a three ends and a four starts."

"Yeah."

"You'll get it. Takes a few batches." She put one earbud in — just the one, leaving the other ear open to the room — and turned back to her screen.

He watched her work for a moment. The rhythm was visible: she'd study the image, her eyes sweeping in a pattern he recognized as a compositional scan — top-left to center to edges, the same scan he used on a painting before taking in the subject — then she'd click through the five rating fields and hit Next. The whole cycle took maybe two minutes. She never hurried the scan and never lingered once she'd found the answer.

Down the row, Bao was still on the same image he'd had up when Nick arrived. His head was tilted to one side, his reading glasses catching the overhead fluorescent wash. He kept looking at the screen with the same patience he'd brought to Nick, waiting for whatever in the picture would betray itself next. Tommy, by contrast, clicked in rapid bursts, then went still whenever the screen gave him something messy enough to earn a second look. Nick could see the edge of Tommy's screen from where he sat: a generated image of a woman standing in a garden, except the woman appeared to have three hands and the garden's flowers were growing sideways out of a wall.

Nick logged in to the evaluation portal with the credentials Lisa had given him and waited for the queue to load.

---

At noon he went to the break room for water. It was a small square room at the end of the hall — a microwave, a coffee maker with a carafe of what looked like it had been sitting since morning, a mini-fridge humming in the corner, and a round table with four mismatched chairs. The window looked out over the parking lot and, beyond it, a landscaping island planted with ornamental grasses that had gone brown and seedy, leaning sideways from the last rain. His Civic sat in the lot below, the oldest car in the row by at least a decade.

Two people from other teams were eating at the table — a woman with a Tupperware of rice and vegetables who was scrolling her phone, and a man in his thirties with a beard and a laptop open to what looked like a side project, code on the screen. Neither looked up. Nick filled a paper cup from the water cooler and stood by the window.

He could hear the evaluation floor through the wall — not voices, just the ambient hum of it. Monitors, lights, ventilation, the ticking of mice. The sound of people doing the same task in parallel, like a room full of students taking the same exam. Through the window, a BART train moved along the elevated track in the middle distance, sliding south toward Fremont, its silver cars catching what little light the overcast sky offered. He did the math without meaning to. Twenty-four people on the floor, most of them probably making about what he was making, maybe less by the time taxes and gas and everything else finished taking bites out of it. The company had found a way to buy trained looking by the shift. Years of practice, all that attention, flattened into a badge and twenty dollars an hour.

He went back to his station.

---

His first batch was twenty-five images. The portal loaded them into a queue, one at a time, each accompanied by the text prompt that had generated it and the five rating fields. He opened the first image and everything else in the room went quiet.

It was supposed to be a portrait of a young woman holding a cat. The prompt read: *photorealistic portrait, young woman, brown hair, holding tabby cat, soft natural lighting, detailed.* What the model had produced was close enough to be human and wrong enough to make his skin prickle. The woman's face was almost right — the proportions were plausible, the features were individual, the skin had pores and fine hairs rendered with obsessive fidelity. But the transition from her jaw to her neck had the consistency of melted wax, as if the model had understood what a jaw was and what a neck was but not how one became the other. Her left eye sat a millimeter higher than the right, which wasn't unusual in actual faces, but here it combined with a flatness in the gaze — a stillness behind the irises that had nothing to do with expression — that made her look like a department store mannequin someone had painted with extraordinary care. The cat was worse. Its body was anatomically reasonable, but its front paws had been rendered as blunt mittens without visible toes, and its tail emerged from a point high on its back that was nowhere near the base of the spine.

Nick clicked through the categories. Prompt adherence: the image matched the prompt. Four. Anatomical accuracy: the jaw-neck seam, the paw structure, the tail origin point. Two. Lighting consistency — he looked again. The soft natural light the prompt requested was present, more or less, but the woman's nose cast a shadow to the left while the cat's body cast one to the right, which meant either two light sources in a scene that should have had one or a fundamental confusion in the model's understanding of how photons worked. Two. Compositional balance: the woman was centered, the cat broke the symmetry, the background was an indistinct blur that didn't compete with the subject. Three. Stylistic coherence: the hyper-detailed rendering of the woman's face against the painterly blur of the background produced a tonal split, as if two different artists had worked on the same image without communicating. Two.

He clicked Next.

The next few taught him the rest of the job fast. One image showed a sunset lake with reflections too perfect to belong to moving water. Another showed a rainy street that looked cinematic until the pedestrians' knees bent the wrong way. Then the queue served up a close-up of a human hand reaching off-frame. The prompt wanted *realistic, detailed.* The image gave it six fingers. Not a blur, not a stump — six complete digits, knuckles and nails and shadow all carefully rendered, as if the model had learned that hands radiated outward and never learned the count.

One, one, three, three, two.

By the fifteenth image, the rhythm had established itself. Open, scan, assess, rate, click. The work required his full visual attention and almost none of his conscious reasoning, which produced a strange floating sensation — the eyes busy, the hands busy, the mind hovering above the task, noticing things at the edges. The fluorescent light shifted the monitor colors warm; everything on screen read a few degrees yellower than its actual value. Bao was now on perhaps his fifth image, which put his pace at roughly one every ten minutes. Tommy had burned through what looked like his entire first batch and started a second. Dani's pace hadn't changed — one image every two and a half minutes, steady as a metronome.

Most of the queue missed human bodies in the same repeatable places: hands first, then shoulders, eye sockets, the weight of fabric over bone. The errors landed in him before language did. A shoulder was wrong. An eye socket was too shallow. Light broke from two directions at once. The rubric wanted numbers. He kept giving them numbers. But the translation — from *wrong in the shoulder* to 2 out of 5 for anatomical accuracy — felt like forcing something wet and dimensional through a stencil until only the approved shape remained.

At five o'clock Priya came by his station. "How'd your first day go?"

"Good. Yeah, fine. The images are—" He stopped, unsure what he'd been about to say. Uncanny. Fascinating. Depressing. Wrong in ways he could feel in his own body.

"They're something," Priya said, and the faint amusement in her voice suggested she'd watched a lot of first days end with that unfinished sentence. "You got through the whole batch?"

"And about half the second."

"That's fast for day one. Most people take a while with the interface." She glanced at his screen, where the portal displayed his completion stats. "Your accuracy gets checked against consensus scoring tomorrow, so we'll see how you calibrated. But the throughput's good." She paused. "Any questions?"

He had a hundred questions, but none of them were the kind she was asking about. "I'm good," he said.

"Great. Welcome to Canopy." She said it without irony, or if there was irony in it, it was the kind that had been lived with for long enough to become indistinguishable from sincerity.

Nick drove home on the 880. Quarter after five and traffic had already locked solid, stretching his fifteen-minute commute into forty. He sat in the slow lane with the window cracked, February air mixing with the Civic's rattling heater, and tried to assemble the day into something he could hold in his mind.

By the time he passed the airport, the day had already revised the plan. Priya had named two control points without meaning to: consensus scoring and throughput. Dani had handed him the floor version of the rubric because the official one was too bloated to use. Bao worked slowly enough that slowness itself might show up in a metric. Tommy moved fast enough to survive only as long as the numbers kept liking him back. Anything crude he did in this building would hit the row before it hit anyone higher up.

The traffic inched forward. The bay opened up on his left, flat and gray-green under a sky that was trying to decide whether to rain. The light was the diffused, directionless kind that early February produced in Oakland — no shadows, no warmth, the colors muted as if seen through a sheet of vellum. A painter's light, technically. The kind you used for color-matching because it didn't lie. It just didn't offer anything either.

So the next step wasn't doing damage. It was mapping. He needed to know who checked consensus scores, who ate a bad metric, what rolled uphill to Priya, what disappeared into software, and where the seam was between the people on the floor and the company that used them. Anything else would just break across the row.

His exit came up. He took it. The surface streets of Fruitvale were damp, the first scatter of evening rain darkening the asphalt in uneven patches. He parked in front of his building and sat in the car for a minute with the engine off, as he'd sat in the Canopy lot that morning before going in. The rain tapped on the Civic's roof. Through the windshield, the streetlight threw a sodium-orange wash over the wet pavement — warm and directionless, flattening everything underneath it to the same false color.

He went upstairs. The apartment smelled like linseed oil and turpentine, the particular mustiness of a space where someone painted. His canvases leaned against the far wall, the unsold work from the Temescal show, turned to face the plaster so he didn't have to look at them. He dropped his bag on the floor and stood in the middle of the room and listened to the nothing. No monitors humming. No mice clicking. No murmur of twenty-four people doing the same task in parallel. Just the drip of the kitchen faucet he kept meaning to fix, and the distant rattle of a BART train on the elevated track outside his window, heading somewhere he'd already been.


# Chapter 5

A yellow banner cut across the portal halfway through his third batch.

QUALITY REVIEW: BATCH 2-1147 SELECTED FOR CALIBRATION AUDIT.

Nick clicked it because yellow usually meant he'd fucked something up. The portal redrew itself into split screen, with his scores on the left and three expert reviewers on the right, their names replaced with strings of letters and numbers that made them look less like people than shipping labels.

The first image was one he'd rated twenty minutes earlier: a woman in an overcoat standing in a park with a paper cup in one hand and a wet bench behind her. At thumbnail size it looked plausible. Expanded, the problem was still where he'd left it. The sky was flat gray, the grass dull with overcast light, and under the woman's chin the model had painted a warm reflected glow from a wall that wasn't there.

He dragged the image larger anyway, checking himself. There was no bright pavement, no car catching sun, no low-angle source of any kind. He'd given lighting consistency a 2. The experts had given it 2, 2, and 2.

He clicked forward. Image nine was a hand wrapped around a ceramic mug. He'd rated anatomical accuracy a 1 because the wrist twisted farther than the radius and ulna would let a human arm go without breaking. Two experts had given it a 2. The third had given it a 1. A red line under the scores read SYSTEM REANALYSIS: EVALUATOR SCORE UPHELD.

By the time he reached image thirty-two he'd matched the experts on twenty-nine. On the three misses, he'd gone lower. Two were marked upheld. At the bottom of the summary page an alignment chart compared his scores to the floor mean. His bar sat above the gray average by enough that he refreshed once to make sure the page wasn't stuck on someone else's account.

Then a note expanded under the chart.

"Evaluator Bray — anatomical accuracy scores significantly above floor average. Flagging for senior review."

Nick stared at it. He had been at Canopy three weeks. The plan, insofar as it was a plan, had been to settle in, learn the system, stay invisible. There were sixty-three evaluators on the San Leandro floor, most of them with more time in the chair than he had. Dani had been here eight months. Bao had been here since the office opened. Nick had figured he'd land in the middle somewhere, good enough to keep the job, unremarkable enough to be left alone.

Instead the images kept arriving with reflected light from nowhere, wrists twisted past function, shadows pointing the wrong way.

Fourteen years of looking at paintings, at light on surfaces, at wrists bent under load and shadows crossing faces had trained the response too deep to shut off. He'd thought he'd left that in the apartment with the stacked canvases and the turpentine smell. He hadn't. When an image was wrong, the wrongness landed before thought did.

The audit note was its own kind of problem, because competence attracted attention, and attention was the opposite of what an infiltrator needed.

But attention also moved him closer to the machinery. At least now he knew one thing that rolled uphill. And the machinery was what he'd come here for.

He closed the audit and reopened the batch.

---

The Slack problem announced itself at 9:07 on a Wednesday in his second week.

Priya dropped the daily standup prompt into the San Leandro floor channel while people were still lowering themselves into chairs and unsealing breakfast bars.

STANDUP THREAD. POST BLOCKERS, BATCH QUESTIONS, AND COVERAGE NEEDS IN-THREAD.

Nick had never used Slack before Canopy. His professional communication history consisted of emails to gallery owners, occasional texts to Jess, and one ill-advised attempt to use Instagram DMs to pitch a commission to a restaurant in Piedmont. Slack was something else entirely — a cascading ecosystem of channels and threads and reaction emojis that seemed to operate by rules no one had written down.

The speech-bubble icon opened a narrow reply column off to the right of Priya's message. Nick clicked it, lost it again, found it, and then somehow closed the whole channel instead. By the time he got back the thread already had seven replies: Tommy asking for coverage after lunch, Bao flagging a corrupted landscape batch, someone on day shift requesting a monitor swap. Nick decided typing directly under Priya's message was close enough.

Morning circle question, he wrote. Batch 2-1189, image 14. The wrist rotation exceeds what the radius and ulna can physically do without dislocation, and the reflected highlight under the jaw has no environmental light source. Are we supposed to score both anatomical accuracy and lighting consistency when the same bad arm is causing the whole cascade?

He hit Enter.

The paragraph appeared in the main channel.

For one second the floor stayed at its usual volume: mice clicking, chair casters rolling, the HVAC pushing lukewarm air that smelled faintly of carpet glue. Then Tommy made a choking sound two rows over and turned in his chair far enough to look at Nick around his monitor.

"Morning circle?" Tommy said.

A few heads lifted. Bao did not turn, but the corner of his mouth moved. On-screen, Priya replied beneath Nick's message almost immediately.

Nick, please use the thread for standup items.

Then, before he could fix it, another reply dropped in from a user in San Francisco whose name was only initials and a little building icon.

Can you condense this to a rubric note? Thanks.

Nick stared at his own sentence. It had seemed precise when he wrote it. Now it sat in the channel like a dead animal someone had to remove. He clicked More Actions, found three options he did not understand, copied the whole paragraph, opened what he thought was the thread, and pasted it there too. Now there were two versions.

"You double-posted it," Tommy said.

"Shut up," Nick said, not loudly.

Priya stood at the end of the row with her mug in one hand and looked over at him through the blue light of the monitors. She did not look angry. That was worse. Angry would have been a clean color. This was administrative disappointment, beige and total.

"I'll move it," she said. "Just use the thread next time."

By lunch the message had been moved, which meant the channel now held both his original mistake and Priya's small public act of housekeeping underneath it.

"You can't call standup the morning circle in the main channel and paste a whole anatomy deposition under Priya's message," Dani said.

It was late February, the same Wednesday. They were in the break room, which was really just a corner of the floor sectioned off by a partition, with a microwave, a coffee maker that produced a liquid that bore the same relationship to coffee that AI-generated art bore to painting, and a window overlooking the parking lot's strip of landscaping — three sickly junipers and a decorative boulder. Dani had her lunch open, a sandwich wrapped in foil that she ate with one hand while scrolling her phone with the other.

"Deposition," Nick said. "It was a question."

"It was a paragraph with bones in it. Nobody wants bones in Slack."

"It did have bones in it. That was the problem."

Dani gave him a look over the top of her phone. "The problem was that you posted it to eighty people when Priya asked for a thread."

"Sixty-three."

"Eighty if San Francisco's in the channel, which they are, because one of them told you to condense it."

Nick pulled the tab on a can of sparkling water he'd brought from home because the vending machine Coke cost a dollar seventy-five and he was trying to keep his daily spending under eight dollars. "What's the right amount to write, then."

She swallowed and thought about it. "'Hand anatomy incorrect. Wrist rotation non-functional.' Then if you need a second note, 'Lighting source unclear under jaw.' Two short lines. Done."

"That doesn't explain the cascade."

"It doesn't need to." She folded the foil back over the sandwich and pressed it flat with her thumb. "And don't call it the morning circle again."

"I know what a standup is. The word still doesn't make sense. Everyone sits."

"That part doesn't matter." She set the sandwich down. "Morning circle makes it sound like you think this is daycare."

Nick drank his water. It was flat. The can had been in his bag since morning, rattling against his laptop during the BART ride from Fruitvale to San Leandro. He should have bought it cold from the vending machine, but a dollar seventy-five was a dollar seventy-five.

"And the thread thing," Dani said.

"I know where the thread is."

"You say that every time and then I watch you type in the main channel anyway."

"I forget when the window shifts around."

"Then slow down long enough to find it." Her tone stayed level. "If you keep doing that, Priya has to clean up after you in public. She can't not do it, because San Francisco watches the floor channel when they're bored."

Nick picked at the edge of the can. "Tommy's never going to stop talking about morning circle."

"No," Dani said. "He is not."

The corner of her mouth moved, the smallest concession to amusement. Then her phone buzzed. She glanced at the screen, and whatever she saw changed her face by degrees rather than all at once. Her shoulders tightened. She set the phone face-down on the table too quickly.

Nick looked away before he made her catch him looking. "I'll use the thread."

"Good."

She said it in the same tone she used for work that had been corrected and could now move on. Dani was like that. She helped him without being friendly about it, or she was friendly without it costing her anything, and Nick couldn't tell which. She wore her competence casually, without fuss. A task arrived; she handled it and moved on. She never overshared, never asked him personal questions, never lingered in conversations past their natural end. When they talked, it was about work, and when work was done, she went back to her station.

But she kept helping him, which he hadn't asked for, and he noticed things about her that had nothing to do with help. The way she picked at the paint residue under her nails when she was thinking, a gesture so habitual she probably didn't know she did it. The drawings — Luna's drawings, her daughter's — that stuck out of the tote bag she kept under her desk, a new one every few days, trees with faces and houses that floated in pink skies and people with too many fingers. The way she assessed an image before she scored it, tilting her head slightly to the left, a habit he'd seen in other artists and never in anyone who hadn't spent time with a brush or a pen.

Nick looked at his sparkling water. There was a smear of cadmium yellow under his thumbnail from a canvas he'd touched that morning — not a new painting, just a rag he'd used to clean up months ago that was still sitting by the kitchen sink. The color was drying and cracked, like a paint chip peeling off old siding. He scraped it off with the edge of the can.

Tommy did in fact keep talking about morning circle. Not for three days, like Nick later told himself. For the rest of the day was enough. At two-thirteen Tommy dropped a message in the correct thread that read no blockers unless morning circle runs long. At four he walked past Nick's desk and asked if there would be finger paints at tomorrow's standup. Dani kept her face neutral. Bao said nothing. Priya, mercifully, pretended not to hear any of it.

Two days later, he learned what Dani's phone call had been about.

He was coming down the stairwell at the end of the floor — the stairwell nobody used because it led to a fire exit and a section of the parking lot that smelled like the Chinese restaurant's grease trap next door — and he heard her voice before he saw her. She was on the landing between floors, her back against the wall, her phone pressed to her ear.

Her voice was tight and controlled. Every word came out measured. He caught the middle of a sentence.

"— I haven't missed a day. I've been here every day since October. You can tell them that. Tell them exactly that."

Silence while she listened.

"That's what 'stable employment' means, Brian. That's what the filing says. Stable. I'm stable."

More silence. Her free hand was flat against the wall, pressing hard enough that the tendons in her wrist stood out.

"I know what she said. I know. But Lu drew a picture of our — she drew our apartment. She drew it and she put me in it. She put me at the table. I'm not — can you just tell them that? Can you just tell the judge that?"

Nick backed up two steps, quietly, and went the long way around to the bathroom. He stood at the sink and washed his hands for no reason. The soap smelled like artificial lavender. The fluorescents buzzed above the mirror and cast the kind of flat, greenish light that made everyone look slightly ill — a color temperature he'd never have chosen for anything, the visual equivalent of the break room coffee.

He'd known Dani had a daughter. He'd seen the drawings in the tote bag, asked about them once, and Dani had said "My daughter, Luna, she's seven" in a tone that was proud and also closed, like a door you were allowed to see but not walk through.

He hadn't known about the lawyer or the judge or how hard she leaned on the word stable, giving it the weight of evidence. He pictured her at her station, every message in the right thread, every shift accounted for, and understood more than he wanted to.

Nick went back to his station and opened his next batch. A landscape with a sky that couldn't decide whether it was sunrise or sunset — the color temperature of the clouds said morning, but the shadows fell east, which meant the light source was in the west. He rated it a 2. He used the thread.

---

Priya's office was not really an office. It was a glass-walled room at the end of the evaluation floor that had probably been designed as a small conference room — seating for four, a whiteboard, a table with a fake wood-grain surface that was peeling at one corner. Priya had made it hers in a way that was both thorough and slightly apologetic, as if she knew the room was too nice for the business park and wanted to compensate by underfurnishing it. A desk, a monitor, a photo of what Nick assumed were her parents, and a Costco-sized bottle of Tums that sat next to her keyboard like a second monitor.

It was the second week of March. Nick had been at Canopy for a month and a half. The rain outside had thinned to something that wasn't quite drizzle and wasn't quite mist — a Bay Area in-between that made the parking lot look like a photograph taken through dirty glass. The three junipers were still sickly.

"Close the door," Priya said. "Sit."

Nick sat. The chair was one of those ergonomic mesh things that tech companies bought in bulk and that always made him feel like he was being gently cradled by a very reasonable spider.

Priya was looking at her monitor, scrolling through something. She wore the same kind of business-casual outfit she wore every day — a dark cardigan over a blouse, earrings small enough to be professional but present enough to be human. Her hair had streaks of gray she didn't touch up, and she wore it pulled back in a way that looked efficient rather than styled. The skin under her eyes was darker than it should have been. She'd been at the office before him that morning and she'd still be here when he left.

"So," she said. "Your numbers are — do you know what your numbers look like?"

"I've seen the dashboard."

"Okay. So you know." She turned the monitor toward him. A graph — his accuracy scores plotted against the floor average and the expert-reviewer consensus. His line tracked the expert line so closely they overlapped in places, while the floor average ran below both, separated by a gap that widened over the last two weeks. "Your anatomical accuracy is the highest in this office. Your lighting consistency is second-highest, and the person above you has been here a year and a half. Your overall quality score is — " She scrolled. "Ninety-three point four. Floor average is seventy-eight point two."

"Okay," Nick said.

"That's not a normal response to those numbers."

"What's the normal response?"

Priya leaned back. She picked up the Tums bottle, turned it in her hands absently, set it down. A habitual gesture, Nick thought, like Dani's nail-picking — something the hands did while the brain worked on something else.

"The normal response is 'great, what does that mean for me.' So I'll just tell you what it means for you. I'm recommending you for senior evaluator."

Nick looked at the graph on her monitor. The gap was wide enough to explain why he'd been called in. Senior evaluator meant the priority queue he'd been locked out of since February: tighter batches, extra rubric fields, fewer hands between his score and whatever the model learned from it. It also meant the yellow audit banner would stop being occasional and become routine.

"Senior evaluators work on priority batches," Priya said. "Higher-complexity images, tighter turnaround, more detailed rubric fields. The pay bump is modest — about two dollars an hour — but your evaluations carry more weight in the aggregate scoring. And if one of those batches goes sideways, I hear about it the same day."

He heard the approval under the job description. Priya wasn't padding a modest pay bump. She meant the recommendation.

He hadn't come here to do the job honestly, but he had done it honestly. That was the part he hadn't planned for.

"I'll take it," he said.

"Good." Priya turned the monitor back. "I think you'll — I think this is the right move. For your development here." She paused before "development." Corporate words never sat naturally in her mouth. "I should tell you, the senior evaluator role also means your scores will be audited more frequently. Weekly reviews instead of biweekly. More eyes on your work."

"That's fine."

"And your evaluation notes need to fit the rubric fields. I know Dani's talked to you about this."

Nick almost smiled. "She has."

"She's good about that. Dani's good about — a lot of things." Priya reached for the Tums bottle again, unscrewed the cap, shook two tablets into her palm, and chewed them with the mechanical efficiency of someone who'd done this many times today. She swallowed and put the bottle back without looking at it. "One more thing. Your social integration."

"My what."

"You called the standup a morning circle."

"I've been told."

"And the Slack thing."

"I'm using the threads now."

"Good. Your numbers — look, the technical side isn't in question. Eleven months I've been on this floor and I haven't seen quality scores like yours. But the documentation part — the notes, the threading, the format that San Francisco actually reads when they pull reports — " She straightened a pen on her desk that didn't need straightening. "That's where I need you to catch up. Because if I'm spending my morning moving your messages instead of reviewing batches, that's a problem I can't keep absorbing."

Nick sat with that. Outside the glass wall of Priya's office, the floor hummed with the ambient noise of sixty-three people rating images on monitors. He could see Bao at his station, leaning close to his screen, reading glasses catching the fluorescent light, moving with the careful deliberateness that characterized everything Bao did. He could see Tommy two rows over, slouched low in his chair, clicking through a batch with the speed that Priya was always trying to temper, his foot bouncing against his desk leg. He could see Dani's station, empty — she was probably in the stairwell again, or the bathroom, or wherever she went when her phone buzzed with a notification she didn't want to read at her desk.

"I understand," Nick said.

Priya nodded. She looked tired. Not dramatically tired, not visibly falling apart, but permanently tired — gray under her eyes, tension in her shoulders she carried without adjusting for, the slight forward lean of someone always reaching for the next problem. She'd been running this floor for eleven months. She'd mentioned once, maybe during training, that she'd taught high school art for twelve years. Why she'd left, Nick didn't know. What he had in front of him was a glass-walled room in a San Leandro business park, a peeling fake-wood table, a Costco bottle of Tums, and Priya managing the quality of AI-generated images for a company whose CEO talked about "the irreplaceable human layer" from a nicer office in San Francisco.

"Start on priority batches Monday," she said. "I'll update your access credentials over the weekend. Congratulations, Nick."

On Monday morning the portal had a new tab.

PRIORITY.

He saw it before he took off his jacket. Priya had updated his access credentials sometime over the weekend, and the interface had rewarded the event with the visual drama of a software patch: one more tab at the top of the screen, one red banner, three extra fields under the image window. SENIOR EVALUATORS SUBJECT TO WEEKLY CALIBRATION REVIEW. The room on the other side of the locked door turned out to be another beige room with better metadata.

The form had changed shape, too. Under the score sliders sat new boxes for anomaly type, severity, and reviewer confidence, each with its own dropdown menu full of language that wanted certainty arranged into cleaner stacks than certainty ever came in. The old screen had asked for scores and a note. This one wanted the failure named, sorted, and fed back upstream in smaller pieces.

Across the aisle Dani glanced over at his monitor while she slid her tote under the desk. "You in priority now?"

"Looks like it."

"Then keep the notes short."

"You really believe in me."

"I believe in Priya not having to move your messages for you." She logged in, phone face-down by her keyboard. One of Luna's drawings stuck out of the tote — a horse this time, blue marker mane, legs going in four directions. "Congrats, though."

She said the last part without looking at him. That made it sound more real.

Nick clicked the new tab. The first image loaded: a family at a kitchen table, all four of them smiling with the fixed, mildly feverish happiness the models loved. The little boy had six fingers on his left hand.

Two dollars more an hour meant eighty a week before taxes. Roughly sixty-three after. Two hundred and fifty-two dollars a month. Enough to stop eating rice three nights a week and maybe get the Civic's check-engine light looked at, which had been on since November and had joined the list of things he couldn't afford to know more about.

It also meant a score like this would not disappear into floor noise. Priority batches ran through fewer hands. If enough senior scores started drifting, Priya would get the email first. The floor dashboard would put names next to the damage.

Nick moved anatomical accuracy to 2. In the note field he wrote: Hand anatomy incorrect. Extra digit on left hand.

He checked that the cursor was in the right box this time, then hit submit.

Priority gave him more weight and better metadata. It still didn't show him where the batches were assembled before they reached his screen.


# Chapter 6

Priority had given Nick more weight and better metadata. It still had not shown him where the batches were assembled. The night shift started at ten, and on the four nights Nick covered it he only worked until two. By ten-fifteen the floor had already changed countries.

He had volunteered for three reasons he told Priya and one he had not. The official ones were broadening his understanding of the pipeline, cross-training on new batch types, and covering the front half of the staffing gap that opened when two night evaluators quit in the same week. They were all true enough that she did not push. The real reason was that night batches ran through a different routing queue, the one where training data got assembled into weighted sets before being fed to the models. If he was going to do real damage, he needed to know how that part was built.

What he had not expected was the night shift itself.

The daytime floor hummed with managed productivity - Priya's whiteboard schedules, the steady click rhythm, Slack pings calling people to standups nobody wanted. At night the place exhaled. Someone had figured out how to kill two overhead banks near the back wall, so that corner held a permanent dusk people drifted toward. The HVAC clicked off at nine and stayed off until six. By midnight the air tasted stale and warm and faintly human in a way the daytime sterility never allowed.

There were eleven night evaluators and none of them were there by accident. On Nick's fourth night Angie Chu came in still wearing the blue Kumon cardigan from her day job, dropped into her chair, and started eating rice with chili crisp from a plastic deli container before she had even logged in. Rhonda Pak had the gray scarf pooled in her lap and her needles tucked under one wrist while the next image loaded; when it did, she clicked through with two fingers and then resumed knitting. Len Marquardt unscrewed his thermos, poured himself his ritual capful of coffee, and leaned sideways toward his screen.

"Truck shadow's wrong," he said to nobody in particular. "Sun's over here."

Nobody answered because nobody had to. DeShawn was already typing notes in his neat legal paragraphs. The woman with the rolling suitcase was lining up her ergonomic keyboard and lumbar pillow with campsite seriousness. Bao sat three rows over with his reading glasses low on his nose, zooming in on a hand image long after most people would have hit a number and moved on.

Nick logged in, pulled the evaluation portal onto his left monitor and Kevin's routing dashboard onto the right. Kevin, the remote night-shift batch manager in Portland, communicated in terse Slack bursts and color-coded rectangles. At 10:23 a domestic-interior batch came off ingestion and landed in standard review: kitchen tables, birthday candles, families passing bowls to one another with too many teeth. The tile started blue. Twelve minutes later its confidence band widened and Kevin posted `lane 4 drift, senior eyes`. On the dashboard the batch split in half. A strip of thumbnails slid from blue to amber and into the senior lane.

Nick clicked the first rerouted image. A father was carving something that might have been ham. His left wrist bent backward under the cuff at an angle no tendon would allow. Nick marked anatomical accuracy low, lighting low, and hit submit. The aggregate bar on the dashboard jumped harder from his number than it had from the four standard ratings already sitting there. A second senior score from the far side of the floor moved it again. The standards had done the sorting, but once senior evaluators touched a batch the machine listened differently.

Over the first three nights he learned the pipeline faster than he expected, which was useful and disorienting. He kept half his attention on his own queue and half on the dashboard, tracing how images moved from ingestion to sorting to evaluation to weighted review to calibration anchoring and, after that, to model training. Senior evaluators' ratings carried roughly three times the weight of standard evaluators. Calibration ratings - the tier above Nick - carried ten times. The weighting was not secret; it sat in the onboarding wiki nobody read. But seeing tiles bunch and split and harden in real time made the architecture look simpler than he had imagined and more fragile. A few scores in the right place shifted the whole temperature of a set, not by much but enough.

By a little after one Bao's lane had gone amber at the top of the dashboard. It was not failure yet. It was risk. Nick had learned the codes too. The average dwell time was too high. Expected completion was drifting. Bao was still at his station, shoulders rounded, zooming into knuckles and shadow edges, doing exactly what made him good and exactly what made the system nervous.

At one-fifteen, when Nick took his break, Bao was the one who followed him into the break room.

---

The break room at night felt less like company property and more like a place people had borrowed after hours. The same microwave sat there with the burned-popcorn smell baked into its walls. The same window looked over the parking lot and the landscaping island where a single ornamental pear tree caught the sodium lights and turned them a sick, underexposed orange. But nobody scheduled who was supposed to be there. Rhonda knitted in the corner. Len ate a sandwich from home on white bread with the crusts still on. Somewhere down the hall a Slack ping went off and nobody moved for it.

Bao came in carrying a plastic bag from a Vietnamese restaurant on East 14th that Nick had never noticed before. He set the bag on the table across from Nick and began unpacking containers with the care of a man who took meals seriously: pho in a large container with the herbs and sprouts in a separate bag, a smaller container of what looked like a spring roll, and a can of Cafe du Monde chicory coffee that he must have brought from home.

"You want some?" Bao said, nodding at the soup. "They always give too much."

Nick had been eating a gas-station burrito that was already cold. "Yeah, actually. Thanks."

Bao produced a second set of chopsticks from the bag, wrapped in a napkin, clearly packed on purpose, and pushed the herb bag toward Nick. "The basil's good tonight. Smell it."

Nick tore a leaf and held it near his face.

"Thai basil," Bao said. "Not the same as Italian. More anise. Purple stems."

The stems were, in fact, purple, darker than the green of the leaves.

"In design school we spent a whole semester on greens," Bao said. "Pantone greens. My professor said a good designer should be able to name them by sight. I could get to about nineteen before I started bluffing."

Nick laughed. "I had a color theory professor who made us mix thirty values of gray from black and white. Just raw pigment and linseed oil on a palette. No tube grays. She said if you couldn't see the difference between value fourteen and value sixteen, you weren't paying attention."

"Could you?"

"Fourteen and sixteen, yeah. Fourteen and fifteen was where I started guessing."

Bao nodded once. "That sounds more right."

"You miss it?" Nick said. "Design work."

Bao turned the basil stem over between finger and thumb. "Some of it."

"What part?"

He set the stem down and lined up his chopsticks before he answered. "It's not the whole job I miss. It's that moment when something is wrong and everybody has to stop pretending it isn't. If the kerning was off, nobody got to print until it wasn't off anymore. If the green was dead, you argued about the green until somebody fixed it. Here I type three lines in the notes field and the little counter goes red."

Nick smiled. "You miss being obeyed."

Bao glanced up. "That too." He took a piece of brisket from the broth. "I don't miss annual reports."

They ate for a while without talking. The pho was better than the places Nick usually went, where the broth tasted like it had been made that morning rather than simmered overnight. Bao ate at an even pace. Between bites he folded the used napkin into a square, set his chopsticks parallel across the lid, and nudged the herb bag back into place. Nick had seen the same hands center Bao's reading glasses on a microfiber cloth before every shift and line up his water bottle with the edge of the monitor.

"You painted," Bao said. It was not a question.

"Yeah. Still do, supposedly."

"What kind?"

"Landscapes, mostly. Oakland hills, the port, some coastline. A little figure work early on that I wasn't very good at."

"Mm." Bao reached into the bag and pulled out a smaller container that turned out to be sliced mango, bright orange and wet. "I did graphic design for forty-one years. Print, mostly. Packaging. Annual reports. The kind of work where nobody notices you unless something is wrong."

"That's sort of what this is."

Bao looked at him. "Yes," he said. "That's why I'm good at it."

They finished the mango. Nick asked Bao why he worked nights and regretted it right away. It felt nosy, the kind of question you asked because you wanted the shape of another person's life laid out for you. "You can ignore that," he said.

Bao dabbed his mouth with the napkin. "No. It's practical." He looked at the table for a second, not reluctant exactly, more like he was deciding where to start. "My wife has a caregiver from eight to six. So I work nights. I am home early morning and evening, and I sleep in the middle. Or I lie down and call it sleep."

"That's a lot."

Bao shrugged. "Mai was diagnosed four years ago. Early-onset. She was fifty-nine."

Nick said he was sorry. Bao said thank you with the practiced ease of a man who had heard it often enough that it no longer needed to do more than acknowledge itself.

"The pension gets us through the regular bills," Bao said after a moment. "This job is the gap. Maria, mostly."

"Maria's the caregiver?"

"Mm." He picked up another piece of mango, changed his mind, and set it back down. "She's from Guatemala. She talks to Mai in Spanish. Mai doesn't know Spanish. She likes the sound anyway. Maria says her own mother had it, so she doesn't do the manual things. Not all of them."

Nick looked at the chip in the laminate near his elbow, the crescent of particleboard showing through. "Does Mai still..."

He stopped there. Bao spared him by answering the unfinished version.

"Some days she knows me right away," he said. "Some days it's slower than that. She knows the watch first, or my hands, or that I am the man who comes in with water. Then maybe the rest catches up. Maybe it doesn't. It changes by the hour."

Nick nodded, which was not much of an answer but was all he had.

"She was a landscape architect," Bao said. "She laid out the garden in our backyard when we bought the house. Not big. Maybe twelve by twenty. But she knew where every plant would want to be five years later, ten years later. She thought about the maple getting taller, how the shade would move, which things would naturalize and which ones would need replacing." He gave a small, dry exhale through his nose. "I move pots around because I think they want more sun. Last month I transplanted a penstemon and killed it in three days."

"My mom had a garden," Nick said. "She used to yell at me for stepping on the mint."

"Mint survives everybody," Bao said. "The problem with mint is not keeping it alive. The problem is getting it to stop."

Nick laughed.

"Mai had very strong feelings about fuchsias too," Bao said. "I am doing bad work with the fuchsias."

"How bad?"

"They are leggy. I know enough to know they are leggy." Another one of those almost-smiles. "I do not know enough to fix it."

Nick had noticed Bao's pace. Everyone had. Bao was the slowest evaluator on the floor, day or night. His accuracy was near-perfect - his ratings aligned with calibration standards more consistently than anyone except Nick - but his throughput numbers sat in the red zone of Priya's dashboard, and Nick had seen the automated performance warning ping Bao's inbox two weeks earlier. Bao had read it, closed it, and gone back to work at exactly the same speed.

"They send those notices a lot?" Nick said. He had not meant to ask it out loud. But the burrito was gone, the pho was good, and the room was quiet enough that a wrong question could land without breaking anything.

"The speed notices." Bao took a sip of the chicory coffee. "Three so far."

"Priya talked to you?"

"After the first one. Very kindly." He rotated the can on the table until the yellow label sat square. "She was not wrong either. There is only so much she can adjust."

"You could burn through the obvious failures," Nick said. "The six-finger ones. The extra-tooth ones. Buy yourself time for the hard calls."

Bao made a face that was not disagreement exactly. "Maybe for a day."

"Why only a day?"

"Because then I start seeing obvious failures where there are not any. Or I stop looking for them at all." He shrugged. "I know my own shortcuts."

Nick's work phone buzzed against the table hard enough to rattle the plastic forks. He looked before he could stop himself. The Slack banner was from Kevin: `N3 under target. peeling 12 to senior overflow now`.

Nick did not need the lane number anymore. N3 was Bao.

Bao watched his face. "Mine?"

"Yeah," Nick said. "Looks like it."

Bao pressed his thumb against the cold metal of the coffee can and held it there. "It takes the easy ones first."

Nick frowned. "What do you mean?"

"The ones a tired person can still clear fast. Six fingers. Fork through plate. Teeth like piano keys." Bao lifted one shoulder. "Those disappear into whoever is moving quicker. Then I go back out there and I get the wrists and the sleeve shadows and the hands hidden under napkins. Then tomorrow the email says I am slow."

"That's backwards."

Bao gave the smallest shrug. "Maybe. It still clears the queue."

He stacked the empty containers and started folding the plastic bag around them.

"You don't have to run back," Nick said.

"If I stay here, it peels more."

That was apparently that. Bao stood, gathered the bag, and paused long enough to push the rest of the mango toward Nick.

"Finish that," he said. "It goes bad by morning."

Nick watched him leave the break room with the bag looped around two fingers, careful even with trash.

---

Back on the floor, Nick saw the peeled batch waiting in his senior queue. It was the same domestic-interior set with the same kitchen-table lighting. The overflow tag sat above the thumbnails, and the lane header still carried N3 in small gray type. Bao was already back at his station, glasses on, not looking over.

On the dashboard Nick watched N3's open count drop in chunks while the system spread the work around, from twenty-six to nineteen to fourteen. Kevin did not have to say anything else. The machine had already made the judgment: Bao's slowness was something to route around.

Nick clicked the first image. A child leaned across a table toward a bowl of noodles while her left hand dissolved into the sleeve cuff. Behind her, a man who was probably supposed to be her father smiled with eleven evenly spaced teeth. Nick rested his hand on the mouse and looked at the aggregate meter on the second monitor. His score would move it, not enough to show but enough to matter.

He thought about what he had come to nights for. He thought about Bao in the break room saying the system peeled the easy ones first. He thought about the orange warning bar that had sat over N3 before dinner and would be there again in the morning in some cleaner, more official form.

Anything strange he did here would not read as sabotage. It would read as drift in a lane already marked risky. It would read as one more reason Bao was slow, or wrong, or not worth the trouble.

Nick rated the image the way he actually saw it.

The confidence marker twitched when he hit submit. Another senior evaluator somewhere across the floor landed a score of her own and the bar settled into a narrower band. Nick looked at Bao without meaning to. Bao had one hand on the mouse and the other resting lightly against his mouth, still studying whatever was left in his lane as if nobody had taken anything from him at all.

The next one loaded. A serving spoon bent through a casserole dish at the wrong angle. He rated that one honestly too. Then a chicken leg that had somehow grown out of the tablecloth. Then a woman's thumb duplicated itself around a water glass. By the fifth image Kevin posted `surge clear, partial coverage can sign off`.

Nick closed the overflow batch, logged out, and went downstairs.

The office on Davis Street was too far from the line to make on foot at that hour, so he drove his Civic to Bay Fair and parked under a light that made the crack in the windshield look white. The lot was mostly empty except for a contractor van, a Tesla with vanity plates, and a shopping cart tipped over by the curb. He caught the last Oakland-bound train with thirty seconds to spare, and by the time the doors shut and the car pulled out the digital clock over them said 1:58.

The train was nearly empty - a man in construction boots asleep across three seats with his hard hat balanced on his stomach, a woman in scrubs reading something on her phone with the glazed intensity of someone who had stopped processing the words twenty minutes earlier, two teenagers at the far end sharing earbuds and not talking.

Nick found a seat by the window and set his backpack on his lap. The train lurched forward. Bay Fair slid past. Then the tunnel swallowed the glass and there was nothing to look at except his own reflection.

He looked tired. That was not surprising. It was two in the morning and he had been staring at generated hands for four hours. But the tiredness was not what snagged him. It was how he was sitting: backpack on his lap, body angled toward the window, one hand on the strap in the exact posture of every commuter on every late-night train. He looked like a man coming home from work. Not a man running an operation. Not an infiltrator mapping a pipeline for sabotage. A man with a job and a commute and a coworker who fed him soup and talked about leggy fuchsias.

The train came out of the tunnel at Coliseum and the window went transparent again - stadium parking lot, empty, light towers dark, a billboard he could not read at speed. Then another tunnel and his face was back, hanging in the dark glass like a print that had not dried yet.

When the train stopped at Fruitvale, he stood. In the window his reflection stood too, a beat behind, like a version of himself that had not quite decided to move yet.

The platform was cold. March in Oakland, the rain done for the night but still in the air, that specific East Bay damp that got into your jacket and stayed there. He went down the stairs and walked past the taqueria that was somehow still open. The guy behind the counter waved. Nick waved back. Cilantro, grilled meat, and fryer oil spilled out when someone opened the door.

In his apartment the unsold paintings were still stacked against the wall. His laptop sat open on the table, forty percent of LeetCode Easy gone dim on the screen. If he used the night queue, Bao would be the first person it hit. He lay on the bed in his clothes and stared at the water stain from the upstairs neighbor's bathroom, the one that looked like a hand with exactly the right number of fingers, while the room held the faint old smell of linseed oil and the newer smell of broth and basil from Bao's soup.


# Chapter 7

The all-hands was projected onto the break room television, a forty-two-inch Samsung mounted at an angle that forced everyone on the left side of the room to look slightly up and to the right, like a congregation receiving a benediction. Someone had miscalibrated the color temperature. The CEO's face ran about eight hundred Kelvin too warm, so he appeared to be broadcasting from inside a tanning bed, but nobody else seemed to notice or care.

"What I want to talk about," the CEO said, "is the human element."

He paused. He was good at pauses. Behind him, the Canopy AI San Francisco headquarters stretched in careful wide-angle: standing desks, exposed brick, a vintage arcade cabinet positioned within the frame in a way that communicated both whimsy and the kind of revenue that permitted whimsy. The lighting was soft, diffused, a three-point setup with the key light slightly too high — a choice that made the CEO look authoritative rather than approachable, though Nick was fairly certain the intention had been the opposite.

Thirty-one people were packed into the San Leandro break room, which smelled like reheated curry from someone's lunch and the particular chemical sweetness of the coffee machine's descaling cycle. The room was designed for twelve. Priya had carried in extra folding chairs from the conference room and arranged them in rows, and now she stood against the back wall with her arms crossed, watching the screen without blinking. The muscle at the hinge of her jaw kept jumping.

"Every model that ships from this company," the CEO continued, "every image generator, every diffusion pipeline — they're built on your work. On human judgment. On taste that can't be automated." He smiled. The smile was engineered for warmth. "That's what I call the irreplaceable human layer."

Nick heard Tommy, two rows ahead, exhale through his nose.

The CEO talked for another eight minutes. He was a skilled communicator — he knew when to slow down, when to gesture, when to look directly into the camera in a way that simulated eye contact. He talked about Canopy's growth: three new model partnerships in Q1, a Series C that valued the company at $1.2 billion, expansion into video evaluation by year-end. He talked about "the incredible team" and "the pride I feel" and "the foundation everything rests on." He said *human* eleven times. Nick counted.

Between the growth numbers and whatever was coming next, the CEO took what Nick recognized as a rehearsed detour into gratitude. He named specific labeling floors — Manila, Austin, San Leandro — and said each one was "critical to the mission." He told a story about a calibration correction that had caught an anatomical error in a model bound for a major client, and how that single correction had saved the partnership. "That was a human being," the CEO said, pointing at the camera with two loose fingers and a little lean into the lens, too practiced for gratitude. "That was one of you. AI can generate a thousand images in an hour, but it takes a human eye — your eye — to tell the machine what quality looks like."

Nick glanced at Priya. Her expression hadn't changed, but her fingers were pressing into the flesh of her upper arm where she held it crossed, leaving white crescents in the skin.

Then the throughput targets.

They came in the back third of the presentation, tucked behind a slide titled "Scaling Our Impact," which featured an upward-trending arrow in Canopy's brand teal. Current evaluation rate across all labeling floors: 8.4 images per evaluator per hour. New target: 14.2 images per evaluator per hour. Implementation timeline: six weeks.

The room did the math quietly.

Fourteen-point-two meant roughly four minutes per image. Four minutes to open the file, assess prompt adherence, rate anatomical accuracy, check lighting consistency, evaluate compositional balance, score stylistic coherence, flag edge cases, and click Next. Nick had been averaging about seven minutes per image, which was already faster than most of the senior evaluators, and even at seven minutes he occasionally caught himself rushing through the lighting check because a hand with seven fingers in the foreground was screaming for attention.

Four minutes. The number sat in the break room like a dropped plate.

Nick's phone buzzed in his pocket. A message in the floor's Slack channel — someone had typed *14.2???* with three question marks. Someone else replied with the wide-eyed emoji. Then Priya's name appeared: *Let's discuss in tomorrow's sync. Please hold questions for now.* Which was Priya trying to contain the reaction before it turned into something the San Francisco office could screenshot.

"To support this," the CEO said, still smiling, "we're making a significant investment in team expansion. New evaluators coming onboard across all floors over the next month. Fresh perspectives, fresh energy." He clasped his hands. "The human layer is growing."

After he signed off — "Keep doing amazing work, all of you, I mean that" — there was a beat of silence, and then the room murmured and shuffled in the way rooms do when people need to say something but don't know what's safe.

Nick stayed in his chair. Dani, sitting next to him, was already standing, pulling her phone out. When work turned unpredictable she got more exact, not angrier; now every movement had that careful click to it, her jaw set.

"Fourteen," she said, not to Nick specifically. "Point two."

"The point two is my favorite part," Nick said.

"The point two is because someone in analytics decided a round number would look arbitrary."

That was probably true.

Priya was already at the door, intercepting people, her voice carrying the tone she used when things were fine and she needed everyone to know things were fine. "We'll have a team sync tomorrow morning to walk through what this looks like day-to-day, OK? Nothing changes overnight. Just — let's all be there at nine."

Nick watched her. Priya's hair was pulled back tight, and the overhead fluorescents threw a shadow under her cheekbones that made the hollows beneath her eyes more pronounced than they'd been even a month ago. She'd lost weight in the way stress did it: first the softness along the jaw, then the blouse hanging flatter across the chest, then the collarbones showing every time she turned toward the door.

Tommy materialized at Nick's elbow, coat over his arm. "Fresh perspectives," he said. "Fresh energy. Can't wait to explain what a wrist is to twelve people who were doing content moderation last week."

Nick looked past him to Priya at the door, still catching people one by one and telling them nothing changed overnight. The TV had already gone back to the Canopy logo.

* * *

The new hires arrived the following Monday — eleven of them — and eleven more the Monday after that. By the third week of April, the San Leandro floor had expanded from thirty-one evaluators to fifty-three, and the parking lot, which had always felt oversized for the building, no longer was.

They came from everywhere. Content moderation, customer service, an Amazon warehouse, a Lyft driver who'd heard the hourly was better. A few had art backgrounds — a photography student, a woman who'd worked framing prints at a shop in Emeryville — but most had been hired for warm-body availability, not visual training. They'd been through a two-day onboarding that covered the rubric categories, the rating scale, the evaluation portal's interface, and the sexual harassment policy, in roughly that order of emphasis.

Nick knew they were in trouble on the second afternoon of the first wave, when Priya stopped behind his chair before he'd finished his third image.

"Can you come look at something before it becomes six somethings?"

He followed her to the glass conference room they'd converted into overflow seating. Four new hires sat at folding tables inside, shoulder to shoulder, their badges still stiff enough to catch the light every time they moved. Someone had taped a sheet of printer paper to the glass that said "NEW HIRE CALIBRATION" in thick black marker. The tape had already started to peel at one corner. In the top right of each monitor, the throughput widget glowed in Canopy teal. One evaluator was already at 11 for the hour. Another was at 13.

"I just need another set of eyes," Priya said, keeping her voice low. She had her phone in one hand and a clipboard in the other, and the Costco-sized antacid bottle was balanced on the clipboard like a paperweight. "A couple of these felt off, but I don't want to pull people for normal beginner stuff if it's just normal beginner stuff."

"Sure."

"Just talk them through what you see," she said. "Don't make it a whole thing unless it has to be a whole thing."

The first screen belonged to a guy with a fresh buzz cut and the pale band on his forearms where some kind of work glove had probably ended. On his monitor, a generated woman held a violin under her chin. The instrument was fine. The face was fine. The bow hand was a disaster, the wrist bent backward in a smooth impossible arc, the thumb hooked over the stick from the wrong side like the model had been given written instructions by somebody who had only heard of violins over the phone.

"Walk me through the four," Nick said.

The guy looked up, startled but not embarrassed yet. "Anatomical four?"

"Yeah."

"I figured the hand was weird on purpose. Like painterly weird. Not, you know, wrong wrong."

Nick put his own wrist out over the desk and bent his hand back until the tendons pulled. "This is about where a person stops. She keeps going. And the thumb's in the wrong place. If she drew the bow like that, she'd drop it."

The guy squinted at the screen. "Oh."

Priya stepped in with the tone she used when she was trying to correct somebody without making them defensive. "That's the distinction. If the style is exaggerated but the structure could still exist, you can be generous. If the structure can't exist, you can't call it style and move on."

At the far end of the table, a woman in a new Canopy hoodie swiveled halfway around. "Even if the prompt doesn't mention the hand?"

"Even then," Priya said. "We're still rating the image."

The woman nodded like she understood, then clicked back to her own screen. Nick saw the image there before he saw the number. A man crouched beside a child's bicycle, smiling at something outside the frame. His left arm came out of the shoulder in a single upholstered tube all the way to the wrist. Anatomical accuracy was marked 5.

"Hold on," Nick said.

She froze with her cursor over Submit. "What?"

"Go back to the arm."

"The sleeve covers most of it."

"No." Nick leaned in, close enough to see the pixels break apart at the edge of the monitor. "The sleeve replaces it. There's a shoulder and a wrist. There isn't anything between."

She leaned forward too. "Oh."

Priya said, "How many have you already done this hour?"

The woman straightened. "Seventeen."

The number landed in the room. Seventeen was good. Seventeen was the kind of number people had started glancing at when they passed each other on the floor.

"Can you open your completed queue?" Priya asked.

The woman hesitated just long enough for Nick to catch it. Then she clicked through. The first thumbnail showed a handshake with one duplicated thumb tucked behind the real one like a second thought. Rated 4 for anatomy. The next was a child at a picnic table with a knee bending sideways under the shorts. Rated 5. The next was a bridal portrait where the bouquet stems disappeared into the bride's palm and reemerged from the heel of her hand like roots through dirt. Rated 4.

Priya pressed her lips together.

Nick clicked to the first evaluator's history. Same problem, different body parts. A boxer with one glove fused shut around nothing. A fashion shot where the model's clavicles met in the middle like a folded coat hanger. Clean justifications in the note field, all of them confidently wrong: *acceptable stylization*, *minor proportion issue*, *composition carries the image*.

In the top corner of Priya's phone, a Slack notification slid down over the lock screen. *Ops: seeing strong speed from first cohort. Keep them live if possible.* She didn't angle the phone away fast enough, so Nick read it by accident or not by accident.

He looked back at the monitors. The throughput widgets kept ticking. 12. 14. 18.

"How much has this pod submitted since lunch?" he asked.

Priya opened the team dashboard with her thumb. "Twenty-seven completed." She swallowed. "Twenty-nine if you count the two still open."

"They're wrong."

"I can see that."

She said it too quietly for the new hires to hear. It sounded less like irritation at Nick than irritation at the fact of having to say anything at all. She watched the dashboard for another second, doing the math on her face. If she pulled four people off live, the floor's hourly rate would drop. If she left them on, somebody else would have to clean up whatever they'd touched, assuming it got caught before it moved on.

"OK," she said.

She straightened and switched back to public voice. "Everybody pause your queues for a minute, please. Hands off keyboard."

The guy with the violin image looked at the teal number in his corner. "Are we in trouble?"

"We're in retraining," Priya said. "Bring your notebooks. Conference room B."

No one moved right away.

"Now, please."
