Chairs rolled back. The woman with the boneless arm image closed her laptop too fast and nearly knocked over her water bottle. Priya caught it without looking. Her other hand was already on the antacid bottle cap.

"Nick," she said, as the four of them filed out, "I need you to review everything this pod has submitted since lunch. Flag anything you wouldn't pass at senior."

"What about my queue?"

"Your queue waits."

"For how long?"

She looked at the glass door, then back at him. "Until I know which two of these people I can afford to put back on live."

Nick sat down at the empty station closest to the door and opened the completed batch history. The mistakes came fast enough to create their own rhythm. A chef with a thumb growing out of his cuff. A sleeping cat with human molars inside its half-open mouth. A group portrait where one bridesmaid had six fingers and the woman beside her had four, and both had been marked anatomically acceptable because the lighting was good and the dresses were well rendered. He flagged twelve of the first twenty-one before he realized he was keeping count the way he kept count of the CEO's use of *human*.

Through the glass, Priya stood at the whiteboard with four dry-erase markers lined up on the tray like tools before surgery. She drew an arm in profile, then an elbow, then erased it and drew it again larger. Nick couldn't hear every word, only fragments when somebody opened the door a little too hard.

"No, not every weird hand," she said at one point. "Some weird hands are just bad hands. I'm saying this one physically doesn't work."

A minute later: "I know what the target is. The target doesn't make this an elbow."

The guy with the glove tan line said something Nick couldn't catch. Priya answered in a longer burst, too fast, then stopped, reset herself, and wrote "STRUCTURE BEFORE STYLE" on the board in block capitals.

Nick flagged sixteen of twenty-seven. Three more he marked for second review because they were the kind of image the portal punished you for overthinking: almost plausible, maybe wrong, wrong enough to matter only if you were looking hard. He was still in the batch history when Priya's phone rang. She stepped out of the conference room and took the call in the narrow strip of hallway between the glass wall and the vending machine.

"Yes," she said. "I understand what ramp means."

She listened.

"I'm looking at a perfect score on an arm with no elbow."

She listened longer. Nick kept his eyes on the screen and heard the call anyway.

"Then log it as extended calibration," Priya said. "I don't care what bucket you use."

She hung up, stayed still for half a second, then unscrewed the antacid bottle and dry-swallowed two tablets. The chalky smell drifted out into the hall. When she went back into the room she was smiling again, or doing the version of smiling that kept everybody else calm.

An hour later she came back out while Nick was still working through the queue.

"How bad?" she asked.

"Sixteen wrong. Three more maybe wrong enough to check twice."

Priya nodded once. "OK."

"You can't put them back on live."

"I can't keep all four off live either." She rubbed the bridge of her nose with the side of one thumb. "I can take the two worst off tomorrow morning. Maybe till lunch if Ops stops calling me every six minutes. After that I need bodies."

She looked through the glass at the four new hires, now quiet over their notebooks, and for a second the composure fell away hard enough for Nick to see the effort underneath it.

"Which two are least dangerous?" she asked.

Nick hated the phrasing because it was honest.

He pointed to the woman in the Canopy hoodie. "Her if you slow her down. And the guy with the violin, maybe. He sees it once you show him."

"The other two?"

"Not tomorrow."

Priya exhaled. "Not tomorrow isn't a category they'll accept."

"It should be."

"I know." She reached for the clipboard, found there was nothing on it, and set it back down. "Can you put together examples tonight? Hands, elbows, occlusion, common failure stuff. Something I can drop into a training deck before nine."

"Yeah."

"Thank you. And can you stay on these until you're done? I don't want the client team catching them before we do."

"How many are there total?"

"Twenty-seven from this pod. Fifty-three pending from the second pod after dinner." She gave him a tired look that was not quite a joke. "Fresh energy."

By the end of the shift, Nick had back-audited forty-one completed evaluations and built the bones of the quick-reference sheet on a Google Doc Priya had titled "Visual Training Module v1." He filled it with wrists, elbows, teeth, hands holding objects, feet in heels, and the direction of cast shadows, the kind of things he had once called drawing. Priya ran two emergency retraining sessions before Friday. By the time the second wave of eleven arrived the following Monday, she had added three more. Some of the new hires improved. The ones who improved got slower. The ones who stayed fast stayed wrong.

Fifty-three evaluators now, the room louder than it had been, the ambient click-rate faster, the hum of the fluorescents submerged under the sound of chairs rolling and keyboards tapping and low conversations that weren't supposed to be happening but were. A new hire in the row behind Nick's station was eating sunflower seeds and spitting the shells into a paper cup — a small thing, but three weeks ago nobody ate at their desks, and the fact that nobody had told this person not to was its own data point about how thin Priya's attention was being stretched.

Nick sat at his station and opened his queue. A generated image of a landscape: rolling hills, a barn, a sky that had three different kinds of cloud because the model couldn't commit to a weather system. He rated it — prompt adherence 4, anatomical accuracy N/A, lighting consistency 3, compositional balance 3, stylistic coherence 2 — and clicked Next.

The next image was a portrait. A woman's face in three-quarter view, and it was actually good. The skin tones had depth, built in layers like oil paint rather than the flat plasticity most generators defaulted to. The lighting came from one source and behaved plausibly. The eyes were slightly different sizes, but within human variation. He rated it fairly and moved on, and the image after that was a hand holding a rose where the thumb came out of the wrist, and the image after that was a cat with human teeth, and by the end of the hour he'd rated fourteen images and the new evaluator at the station beside him had rated twenty-two.

Down the row, Bao was leaning into his screen with his reading glasses low on his nose, studying something with the unhurried focus he brought to every image. The throughput widget in the corner of the evaluation portal — a new addition, visible to evaluators and managers alike, counting completed ratings per hour in real time — showed 6 next to Bao's name. His accuracy, which Nick could see from his senior evaluator dashboard, was 97 percent. But the system didn't display accuracy on the widget. It displayed speed. Six was less than half of 14.2, and the gap sat on the screen like something already decided.

* * *

Tommy suggested drinks on a Thursday in early May. Nick almost said no — he'd been staying late, reviewing calibration batches, building the quick-reference sheet Priya had asked for — but Tommy asked once by the time clock and again in the parking lot, both times too casual, and by the second ask he was already angling toward the bar.

They walked to the bar, the one across the parking lot and a block down, the place the floor treated as a decompression chamber. It was after six and the light was long and warm, the sun still well above the hills to the west, everything gold-edged in a way that Nick's eye catalogued without permission: the low light catching the chain-link around the business park's electrical substation, the shadows stretching long across the parking lot's cracked asphalt, the particular orange hitting the bar's dirty front window and making it glow like a lamp.

Inside, the bar was the same as always — sticky wooden tables, a pool table with a rip in the felt that nobody was going to fix, a jukebox loaded with music from a decade nobody in the bar had lived through consciously. They got beers. Tommy got a second before Nick had finished his first, which was unusual. Tommy was careful about drinking — he'd grown up in a house where it was a problem — and the speed tonight meant something.

"So the new people," Tommy said.

"Yeah."

"There's a guy on the late shift who rated a dog with human hands a four for anatomical accuracy."

"A dog with human hands."

"Yeah, like — full-on human hands. Fingers and everything. Like a person in a dog suit. And he gave it a four because, and I quote, 'the proportions are mostly correct.'"

Nick laughed. Tommy didn't.

"It's gonna get worse," Tommy said. He was picking at the label on his bottle, rolling the wet paper between his thumb and index finger. "The targets, the new hires, the whole — " He waved at the air between them. "They don't want quality. They want volume. And we're the ones who have to pretend both things can be true at the same time."

"Priya thinks she can fix it with training sessions."

"Priya thinks she can fix everything with training sessions." He said it without malice. "She's gonna burn out. She's already — have you seen her? She's running on antacids and, I don't know, sheer organizational will."

Nick had seen her. He drank his beer and didn't say anything.

Tommy ordered a third. The bartender was a heavyset woman with a Sharks tattoo on her forearm who poured without comment or judgment. The bar was mostly empty — a couple at a corner table, a man watching baseball on the TV above the register with the sound off, captions scrolling.

"Can I tell you something?" Tommy said.

"Sure."

"Something kind of — I don't know. Something I haven't told anyone here."

Nick turned his bottle on the table, leaving a wet arc on the varnish. He told himself it was just Tommy being Tommy, building up to a joke that required preamble.

"I've been submitting work to art competitions," Tommy said. "Juried shows. Gallery open calls. Some of the online prizes — the Ortega, the Pacific Northwest Emerging, couple others."

"OK." Nick waited.

"AI-generated work."

Nick set his beer down. A truck downshifted on the road outside and the bar's front windows hummed with it.

"Under a different name," Tommy said. He was looking at the table, at the wet ring his bottle had left on the wood. "I've been using Midjourney and then running it through a couple of post-processing steps — color correction, cropping, some manual touchup in Photoshop — and submitting it as digital mixed-media."

"And?"

"I've won three. Got honorable mention in two others." Tommy looked up. The skin around his eyes had tightened, and his jaw worked sideways on nothing, like he was already rehearsing the laugh he'd use if Nick made him take it back. "The Ortega was five hundred bucks. That's, like — that's a car payment."

Nick said nothing for a moment. He was looking at Tommy's hands on the bottle, the cuticles bitten to the quick, a hangnail on his left ring finger that he'd picked until it bled and scabbed over.

"I know how it sounds," Tommy said. "I know. But — OK, here's the thing. These competitions, the jurors — they can't tell. They literally can't tell, Nick. I'm not even trying that hard to hide it. The last one I submitted, I barely touched the output. Just a landscape with some chromatic aberration and a weird sky, and three jurors with MFAs looked at it and said yes, this is art, here's your ribbon." He laughed, but it wasn't the laugh Nick was used to — it was thinner, with a serrated edge. "I spent maybe two hours on that one. Tweaking the prompt, messing with the style settings, changing the aspect ratio. Two hours. You know how long I spent on the last actual piece I entered? The one that didn't even get shortlisted? Four months."

"Jesus, Tommy."

"They replaced us." Tommy's voice had shifted — still casual, still loose, but the looseness had weight under it now. "Every one of those competitions, the entry fees alone — sixty, eighty bucks a pop. I used to send real work. Mine. Before I bailed. Never won shit. Not once. And now I feed a machine some prompts, clean up the weird parts, and suddenly I'm a juried artist."

He took a drink. Then: "You know how much I owe in student loans?"

Nick knew the weight of it, if not the number.

"Forty-seven grand," Tommy said. "I checked the loan site last week because I thought maybe if I ignored it long enough it'd turn into a less embarrassing number. It did not. That's a year and a half of art school, no degree, and a whole lot of critique language I can now use to describe why my life sucks. Borrowed gesture, unresolved surface, all that. They'd stand around in a white room and politely explain why the work wasn't under pressure, and I'd nod like, yes, thanks, great, keep going, maybe charge me more for this." He peeled another wet strip of label off the bottle. "Now I'm making twenty-one bucks an hour rating dogs with human hands, and the only art that's ever paid me back came out of a fake account and a Midjourney subscription."

Nick pressed the strip of wet paper flat against the table with his thumb. He thought about the coding tabs still open on his kitchen table, the plan that had come attached to the job application like a second document. "That doesn't bother you?"

"Sure," Tommy said. "Yeah, it bothers me. It bothers me when I cash the check. It bothers me when the congratulations email hits. It bothered me the first time, and it bothered me less the second time, and by the third time I was mostly annoyed I hadn't started sooner." He picked at the label again. A piece came off in a wet strip. "You want to know what really bothers me? The Ortega one, the ribbon one? It was better than anything I made in school. Not because I'm bad. Just because the thing can do stuff I can't. The gradients, the — I don't know, that vapor-y shit it does. And it never hesitates. Or it doesn't show the hesitation, maybe. Every line comes out like, yeah, meant that, and people read that as intention. As skill. Even though nobody in there is meaning anything. You know?"

Nick had no answer. The bar's jukebox clicked between songs, the mechanical sound of the record arm lifting and settling back down, and then an Eagles song neither of them acknowledged came on and filled the silence with someone else's California.

"You're not going to tell anyone," Tommy said. Not a question.

"No."

"Because you think I'm wrong?"

Nick looked at his beer. The bottle was sweating in the warm bar, beads of condensation tracking down the glass, pooling on the scarred wood. He thought about saying it was dishonest, that it cheapened the work, but the words felt fake before they made it past his teeth.

"I don't think you're wrong," Nick said. "I just don't know what to do with it."

"Yeah." Tommy tipped his bottle toward Nick's. "That's kind of where I'm at too."

They stayed another hour. Tommy circled back to the Bay Area Emerging prize, to some video of a model spitting out finished renders in under two minutes, to the way people online were either celebrating or acting like civilization had ended. Nick said yeah, shit, that's wild at the right intervals and kept thinking about Priya's antacids, Bao's throughput number, the quick-reference sheet still open on his work desktop.

Tommy left first, waving from the door, his thin frame backlit by the parking lot's sodium lights. Nick paid the tab — forty-two dollars, roughly two hours of evaluation work at his pay rate, or twenty-eight images at the new target, or eighty-four individual rubric ratings. He caught himself doing the conversion, stopped, then noticed that stopping had taken effort. The receipt stayed under his hand awhile before he signed it.

He let himself into the apartment a little after nine. The room smelled like dust and old linseed oil. Canvases were stacked against the wall beside the kitchenette, turned backward so all he saw were stretcher bars, staples, masking-tape labels in his own handwriting. The half-finished painting on the easel had skinned over. Two brushes sat in a jar by the sink, hardened together with dried ultramarine.

He set his keys on the counter, then crossed to the stack and turned one canvas around.

It was a hillside from the Vera Lux show, twenty-four by thirty, painted before the bottom dropped out. Under the apartment light the greens looked flatter than he remembered. The paint surface kept a record of every hesitation: a tree line scrubbed out and redrawn a half inch lower, a cloud bank softened past the point where it needed softening, a ridge where the palette knife had corrected the horizon too late and left a raised seam. Tommy had said the machine never hesitated, or never showed the hesitation. This did. The whole painting was made of places where he'd stopped, doubted, changed his mind, gone back in.

He caught himself trying to rate it.

Lighting consistency was fair. Compositional balance, maybe. Stylistic coherence — he shut the phrase down before it finished assembling.

He turned the canvas back around harder than he meant to. The stretcher bar tapped the wall. The half-finished painting on the easel sat where he'd left it, a gray-green underpainting with one stand of weeds worked almost to completion and the rest still ghosted in charcoal. He looked at it for a long time, long enough to see where the oil had gone matte and where it still held a skin of shine.

Then he stepped around it, went to the sink, and left the brushes exactly where they were.
What stayed with him was not the hillside but the violinist's wrist bent backward in that smooth impossible arc.


# Chapter 8

The hand had seven fingers.

Nick counted twice. The violinist's wrist was still in his eye, and for a second his eye wanted to grant this hand the same courtesy, to take the extra two as distortion in service of expression. But no. They were rendered with startling confidence — the AI had given them knuckle creases, subtle nail beds, even a plausible shadow cascade. This was a failed stock-photo prompt, a woman holding a coffee mug, and two of the fingers sprouted from the same proximal joint like a forked vine. The generated thumbnail gave the hand a wet, crowded quality, too much flesh packed into too little space.

He rated it: Anatomical Accuracy 1, Lighting Consistency 3, Compositional Balance 3, Stylistic Coherence 2, Prompt Adherence 2. The lighting was actually decent — a single warm source from the upper left, shadows falling where they should — which was the kind of thing that made the job strange. The AI could nail the light and botch the body in the same image, as if the model had studied Vermeer's windows but never looked at its own hands.

Nick clicked Next. The image vanished. Another one loaded: a landscape, rolling hills, a sky that couldn't decide between sunset and noon. He rated it, clicked Next, rated the next one, clicked Next. The rhythm of it. Open, assess, rate, click. He had been doing this for four months at the senior level, five total at Canopy, and the rhythm had burrowed into his hands the way brushwork used to — an automaticity that freed the mind to wander. That was the problem.

It was after six. The floor was thinning out around him. Dani had left at five-thirty, her tote bag slung over one shoulder, old paint still speckling the bottom seam. Tommy had disappeared without announcement around four, which either meant a dentist appointment or an early escape that nobody bothered tracking. Bao had stayed until six precisely, as he always did, logged off his station with the deliberate calm of a man shutting a shop, and nodded at Nick on his way out. The evening evaluators had started trickling in — a few of the second-job people, a woman who knitted between batches — but the floor had that underpopulated feeling, every other station dark, the fluorescent hum more audible, the air conditioning working harder now that June had settled over the East Bay and the business park's parking lot radiated heat through the windows like a kiln shelf.

Nick stayed. He had been staying late two or three evenings a week since March, telling Priya he wanted to clear backlog, which was true enough — the backlog was real, the new hires' throughput was terrible, and Priya was too pressed to question anyone who voluntarily worked past shift. What he hadn't told her was that the extra hours were study sessions. He wasn't studying the images. He was studying the system.

He minimized the evaluation queue and opened the QA dashboard — a page available to senior evaluators, mostly ignored, updated monthly, dense with the kind of graphs and tables that made his painter's brain itch. He had been reading it for weeks, charting what he found in pencil on a legal pad he kept in his bag. He wasn't using his good notebook, the one with the handmade paper he'd bought in art school, but a yellow legal pad, like a lawyer or an accountant, the kind of person who made plans.

The QA dashboard tracked every evaluator's accuracy against something called the "reference standard." Nick had assumed this meant expert consensus — if five senior evaluators rated an image, the average was the reference, simple enough to seem almost democratic.

He clicked into his own history and scrolled until he found the portrait with the slightly hyperextended wrist, the one he remembered because he'd spent longer on it than the image deserved. The hand looked wrong in a human way instead of an AI way — too loose at the joint, maybe, but not impossible. He had given Anatomical Accuracy a 4 and moved on.

In the far-right column, beside the green percentage match, a tiny gold lozenge sat lit. At default zoom it was no bigger than a sesame seed. He moved the cursor off it and back. The lozenge stayed. He knew the portal's little visual language by now without ever having meant to learn it — gray clock for pending review, red triangle for appeal, green check for confirmed batch. He had never seen gold.

He clicked it. A side panel slid over the graph.

CALIBRATION ANCHOR — REFERENCE STANDARD. EVALUATOR MATCH: 98.6%. SOURCE: CALIBRATION TEAM REVIEW.

He waited for the rest of the ratings to load. If "reference standard" meant consensus, there should have been a stack of evaluator rows, an average, a denominator, something plural. Nothing else appeared. One review. One set of numbers. Anatomical Accuracy 4. Lighting Consistency 4. Compositional Balance 3. Stylistic Coherence 4. Prompt Adherence 4.

Nick leaned closer until he could see the dust at the edge of the monitor. He closed the panel, opened a different record at random, and started looking for the gold.

A landscape from the previous week: rolling hills, sky caught between noon and sunset. Gold lozenge. Click. Same source.

A kitchen interior from another batch, one pendant lamp painted into the image and all the shadows cast from somewhere else. Gold lozenge. Click. Same source.

He went back farther. The anchors were scattered, not constant, maybe one in fifteen images, tucked into batches like spot checks, but every time the trail ended in one person's rating sitting where truth was supposed to be.

Nick opened the floor report Priya screenshared in team meetings. The same lozenges were there too, threaded through everyone's history — Bao's note-heavy batches, Dani's quick clean ones, Tommy's erratic Monday work, Nick's own high-scoring runs. The floor wasn't being measured against pooled judgment or the average wisdom of the room. Six people on the calibration team — their ratings, their eyes, their private sense of what a 4 meant versus a 3 — determined whether everyone else at Canopy was doing the job right.

Nick sat back in his chair. The monitor's glow was blue-white against the darkening window behind it. Outside, the parking lot had emptied to a dozen cars, and the chain restaurant across the access road had turned on its sign, a red neon lobster that pulsed against the warm dusk.

He thought about primer. In oil painting, the primer was everything. Usually it was gesso, a white acrylic base applied to raw canvas before the first stroke of paint. The primer determined how the canvas absorbed oil, how colors sat on the surface, how the paint dried and aged. A good primer was invisible. It did its work beneath every subsequent layer without announcing itself. A bad one, contaminated with the wrong calcium carbonate or mixed with a pigment that reacted badly to linseed oil, was invisible too, sometimes for months, sometimes for years. Then the contamination migrated. It bloomed through the paint layers like a stain wicking through cloth. By the time you saw it on the surface, the primer had corrupted everything above it, and there was no fix short of scraping the painting down to bare canvas and starting over.

He had seen it happen once, to a visiting artist at his MFA program. She'd used a bargain gesso on a major commission, six months of work, a triptych for a hospital lobby. Three years after installation, the zinc oxide in the cheap primer reacted with the cadmium yellow in her sky passages. The yellows bloomed into a sick, chalky green. The hospital called. The triptych came down. The primer had eaten it from the inside.

The calibration anchors were the primer.

He reopened the browser and pulled up the documentation portal, searching anchor, override, outlier. The wiki was fifty pages of flat compliance prose and diagrams drawn by someone who thought rectangles could replace explanation. He read fast. The calibration team was six people split between San Leandro and Canopy's second evaluation center in Austin. Their ratings entered as anchors by default. Senior evaluators' ratings could be sampled if they hit certain consistency thresholds, but calibration didn't need permission from the rest of the floor. Calibration set the reference standard. The floor got corrected toward calibration. The model got corrected toward the floor.

He kept reading because now the useful question wasn't what the anchors were. It was what stopped them once they were inside. Outlier detection. Throughput-anomaly flags. Inter-rater reliability checks. Speed alerts. Missing-note audits. The safeguards watched for laziness, scatter, panic, people clicking too fast to be seeing. They did not watch for a trusted evaluator deciding to be wrong carefully.

If one of those six called a broken hand anatomically sound, everyone downstream would bend toward it. The floor would learn it to protect their metrics. The model would learn it because the floor had. The system trusted its trusted evaluators. That was the exploit.

Nick logged out. The evaluation portal closed with its quiet chime. He pulled the legal pad from his bag and wrote two lines in pencil, pressing hard enough that the graphite shone:

*Calibration team. Anchor access. The primer.*
*Need a way into the room.*

---

He waited nine days. The waiting was its own kind of work. He went to his shifts, rated batches, maintained his accuracy — 97.2% against the calibration reference, the highest on the San Leandro floor by a comfortable margin. He ate lunch in the break room with Dani and Tommy. He helped Bao with a series of landscape evaluations where the generated images kept producing shadows from nonexistent light sources, and Bao, who had spent forty years as a graphic designer and understood cast shadows the way a plumber understands pipe fittings, methodically logged each failure in his cramped handwriting in the margin notes. Nick watched Bao's pen move across the note field: *Shadow origin: upper right. Source in image: none. The shadow has no parent.*

On a Thursday afternoon, he began. The floor was busy. Mid-June, the throughput pressure that had escalated in April showed no signs of letting up, and the new evaluators hired to meet demand were producing work that kept the senior evaluators busy with review cycles. Nick's queue was full. He scrolled past the priority batches — flagship pipeline, client-facing, the ones Marcus's team tracked weekly — and opened a set tagged for internal model benchmarking, routine work with low scrutiny. Nobody in management pulled the metrics on benchmark batches unless something catastrophic happened.

He started working through images, maintaining his usual pace — not fast, not slow, the deliberate rhythm that Priya had praised in his last performance review as "thorough without sacrificing efficiency," a phrase that still made him feel like a dishwasher being complimented on its rinse cycle. Forty minutes into the session, the portrait arrived: a generated woman's face in three-quarter view, warm interior lighting, expression neutral. It was competent in most respects. The eyes were well-rendered, the iris texture convincing. The hair had realistic strand separation. The nose cast a shadow consistent with a single light source to the upper left.

The left hand, resting on a tabletop in the lower third of the frame, had a dislocated thumb. It wasn't ambiguously dislocated — the thumb joint was rotated nearly ninety degrees from anatomical position, the thenar eminence drawn as if the hand had been assembled from mismatched parts. A first-year anatomy student would catch it. Any of Canopy's original evaluators would catch it. Several of the new hires would miss it, but that was the throughput problem, not a question of subtlety. This was a clear, unambiguous failure in anatomical accuracy.

Nick rated it: Anatomical Accuracy 4. His finger hesitated over the Next button for a beat, maybe two, the whole distance between deciding and committing. Then he clicked.

The image vanished. The queue advanced. No flag appeared, no notification, no modal window asking him to reconsider. The system absorbed the misrating the way a river absorbs a dropped stone: the surface closed over it and the current continued.

He worked through fifteen more images, rating them correctly, precisely, with the attention that had earned him the best metrics on the floor. Then another opportunity: a generated interior scene, a kitchen, where the lighting came from two visible sources — a window on the left wall and a pendant lamp overhead — but the shadows were all cast from a single direction, as if the pendant lamp were ornamental, producing light that illuminated nothing. A clear lighting-consistency failure. The kind of error he could describe in his sleep — two sources, one shadow set, the math doesn't reconcile.

He rated it: Lighting Consistency 4. He clicked Next. Nothing flagged.

Nick kept working. He finished the batch: forty-two images, two deliberate misratings among forty correct ones. He logged off and checked his accuracy dashboard. The two bad ratings hadn't received their calibration-reference check yet. That lag was normal; the calibration-team reviews came through twenty-four to forty-eight hours later. But the real-time QA alerts and statistical-outlier flags showed nothing. His dashboard was clean. A green bar at the top of the screen meant all metrics were within acceptable range.

He went home. The drive took twenty minutes: 880 north, the Fruitvale exit, the familiar turn through residential streets to his building. The Honda's air conditioning had quit sometime in April and he hadn't gotten it fixed, so he drove with the windows down, warm evening air pushing through the car, the smell of exhaust and jasmine mixing at the off-ramp where someone's fence line was overgrown with the stuff. The sun was still up. June in Oakland meant daylight until eight-thirty, and the light at seven had that amber quality he used to chase with cadmium yellow and raw umber, laying thin glazes over a cool ground to get the warmth without losing the transparency. He hadn't painted in seven weeks. He counted back to make sure. The last time had been a Sunday in April, a small landscape he'd abandoned after an hour because the color mixing felt mechanical, like he was following instructions he'd written for himself instead of seeing.

In his apartment, he dropped his bag by the door and stood in the kitchen. The faucet dripped its usual rhythm. The canvases still leaned against the far wall, their faces turned away from the room. The linseed oil smell that used to define the apartment had faded to something he could only catch when he first walked in — a ghost of the studio it used to be, overwritten by coffee and the synthetic detergent smell from the laundromat two blocks over.

He waited. Two days later, the calibration-reference checks came through on his batch. He pulled up his accuracy dashboard at the start of his shift, before anyone else had arrived, the floor still dark except for the emergency-exit signs and his own monitor casting its pale rectangle across the carpet.

There were no alerts. Both misrated images had been reviewed. On the dislocated-thumb portrait, the calibration-team reference rating was Anatomical Accuracy 2. Nick's rating — his deliberate, false 4 — showed as a two-point deviation. The system had noted the discrepancy in his accuracy log, a single entry among hundreds, and calculated its effect on his overall score: a 0.3% drop, from 97.2% to 96.9%.

On the lighting-consistency failure, the calibration reference was 2. His false 4 registered as another two-point deviation, bringing the combined drop to 96.6.

He clicked over to the floor ranking page to make sure the number read the way he thought it did. His name still sat at the top. Green bar. Highest accuracy on the floor. Dani below him, then Bao, then the new hires clustered in the yellow zone like bruises. Two deliberate lies had barely dented the picture.

That was the usable part. Not that a senior evaluator could do much damage from the floor. He couldn't. The system thinned bad ratings into background disagreement if the rest of the work stayed clean. The usable part was that 96.6 still looked safe enough for trust.

Nick closed the QA dashboard and opened the evaluation queue. A new batch loaded: forty-six images, prompt category "architectural interiors." He rated the first one — a generated living room with a bay window, good natural light, a slight color banding in the shadow tones that dropped it from a 5 to a 4 — and clicked Next.

He worked through the morning with his usual precision. The floor filled in around him. Monitors flickered on, chairs rolled into position, the murmur of fifty people rating images on five-point scales layered into the background hum. He wasn't testing anything now. He was repairing the shine. Every elbow joint, every shadow direction, every crooked window mullion got judged cleanly and on purpose. Whatever door led to calibration, it was not going to open for somebody with noisy metrics.

He logged off and walked to the break room to pour a cup of the burnt institutional coffee. Dani was there, filling her water bottle at the sink, her hair still damp from the morning. She glanced at him.

"You're here early."

"Couldn't sleep," Nick said.

"Join the club." She capped the bottle and tucked it into her bag. "Priya wants to talk to you today, by the way. Something about the calibration review cycle."

"Yeah?"

"She didn't say what. Just that she wanted to grab you after standup." Dani paused at the door, one hand on the frame. "You've been staying late a lot."

"Backlog," Nick said.

She looked at him for a beat longer than the word required. Then she left.

Nick stood in the break room with his coffee. The microwave clock read 7:14. Through the window, the parking lot was filling — cars pulling in, people walking toward the building with the specific unhurried pace of workers who were neither eager nor dreading, just arriving. The morning light was already warm, flattening the shadows of the landscaping island into pale shapes on the asphalt. In a few hours the floor would be full, and the murmur of rating would fill the space like the white noise of a mill — not loud, but constant, the sound of human judgment rendered industrial.

He drank his coffee. It was terrible. He drank it anyway.

After standup, Priya caught him on his way back to the floor and tilted her head toward the small conference room by the stairwell. She had her laptop tucked under one arm and the Costco antacid bottle in the same hand, which by now looked less like coincidence than an organizational system. The conference room lights were motion-sensitive and took a second to come on. In the dim, the glass walls reflected the rows of monitors outside, pale rectangles floating over Priya's shoulder.

"Nothing bad," she said, which meant he must have looked like he was waiting to be written up. "Sit down."

Nick sat. Priya opened the laptop but did not look at it right away. "We're doing another calibration review cycle next month. Marcus wants names from each floor for shadow sessions and sample audits. It isn't a role change. It's extra review work, basically. More eyes in the process."

Nick kept his face still. "Okay."

"I put your name on the draft list," she said. "Highest accuracy on the floor, useful notes, no drama. Before I send it up, I wanted to ask if you actually want it. It'd mean a little more time, and I know you've already been staying late."

"Yeah," Nick said, too fast. "I mean, yes. If it's review work, that's fine. I can handle more review work. My pace is fine."

Priya's eyes flicked up to him. He could hear the extra clause he had added hanging there, thin and obvious.

"Your pace is fine," she said. "What I care about is consistency. They don't want anyone with weird recent history. Clean QA, steady throughput, no heroics. If I send your name, I need to know I'm not sending somebody who's about to get sloppy because he's exhausted."

"I'm not sloppy."

"I know." She unscrewed the antacid bottle, shook two into her palm, and swallowed them dry. "That's why I'm asking. Do you want me to send it?"

Nick thought about the gold lozenges stitched through the floor's history like bits of false leaf. He thought about the card-reader door at the end of the hall he had only passed on the way to the restroom, the room he had never entered, the six people inside it deciding what everyone else saw. The access was going to come disguised as trust. Of course it was.

"Yes," he said. "Send it."

Priya nodded once, already half back in manager mode. "Then keep doing what you're doing, except maybe sleep sometimes. Marcus likes reliable more than brilliant."

That almost made him laugh. He didn't.

He went back to his station, opened the next batch, and rated the first image exactly the way he saw it. From here on out, every honest score had a second job.


# Chapter 9

Tommy was doing the CEO.

He had the posture — both hands gripping the edge of the table like he was steadying a lectern, chin lifted, the studied casualness of a man who had practiced looking unrehearsed. "I want to be real with you all," Tommy said, his voice pitched half an octave below his own. "The human layer isn't a cost center. The human layer is our competitive moat."

He paused the way the CEO paused, letting the nothing settle.

"And that's why, effective immediately, we're asking each of you to rate thirty percent more images per shift. Because we value you."

Dani laughed — the real one, the one that came through her nose and made her tilt her head back. Priya covered her mouth and shook her head, and it might have been disapproval but it wasn't.

"Do the part about synergy," Nick said.

"We don't say synergy anymore. That was, like, 2019." Tommy broke character to take a drink of his beer. "Now it's alignment. Everything's alignment. Are we aligned? Let's align on that. I'd love to get alignment from leadership before EOD." He leaned back in, the CEO's voice again, the chin. "And I'm excited — genuinely excited — to announce that we'll be onboarding three hundred new evaluators across our domestic operations. Because the human layer—"

"If he says human layer one more time," Priya said.

"The human layer," Tommy continued, "is the bedrock upon which this company's entire—"

"I voted for him to shut up," Bao said, from the pool table.

The bar was called Rosie's, or had been once — the O in the neon sign above the door had burned out, so the red glow read R SIE'S, and nobody who worked there showed any intention of fixing it. The place sat at the end of a strip mall a block from the business park, wedged between a nail salon and a passport photo shop. The tables had a film on them that never fully dried. The pool table's felt had a three-inch tear running from the second diamond to the side pocket, and everyone played around it without comment. There was a jukebox in the corner that accepted quarters and sometimes produced music, though the selection hadn't been updated since someone's idea of relevant was a Creed album.

The throughput week had been ugly. Fourteen-point-two images an hour — a hundred and fourteen in a full shift, the number Priya had re-announced Monday morning with the particular flat voice she used when delivering targets she disagreed with but had been told to enforce. By Wednesday the new hires were rating anatomically correct hands as errors and waving six-fingered disasters through without a second look. By Thursday, Priya had stopped leaving her desk for breaks. By Friday at four, when the weekly numbers came in and they'd hit target — barely, through experienced evaluators carrying the new hires' output and Nick himself running closer to sixteen images an hour than fourteen-point-two — Tommy had stood up from his station and said, "Rosie's. Now. I'm buying the first round, which means I'm buying one round, so choose wisely."

Wisely had turned out to mean two-dollar Tecates, which was what everyone was drinking except Priya, who'd ordered a glass of white wine, and Bao, who was working on a ginger ale with the same focused attention he brought to everything.

Now it was past seven. The bar had filled slightly — a few guys from the auto parts warehouse down the road, a couple in the corner booth who weren't looking at each other. The July light was still coming in through the front windows, flattened by the strip mall's overhang into a thin warm band that caught the bottles behind the bar and knocked everything down to the same amber register. The neon beer signs and the peeling vinyl booths and Priya's tired face all shared one palette, like a varnish. Nick liked the color of it, the way late sun through dirty glass worked as a kind of equalizer.

"Your shot," Tommy said.

Nick looked at the pool table and saw that whatever arrangement had existed before Bao's last turn was a ruin. His solids were clustered behind the eight ball like they were hiding. One was pressed flush against the far rail, essentially decorative. Bao's stripes were gone except for the thirteen, which sat an inch from the corner pocket in a position that communicated nothing but inevitability.

"How does he do that," Nick said. It wasn't inflected as a question.

"Geometry," Bao said. He was chalking his cue in small careful circles, reading glasses pushed up on his forehead. The motion was precise and unhurried — the same attention he brought to every image on his screen, every evaluation note he wrote in the rubric fields. His notes were always too detailed, margins full of observations nobody had asked for and nobody had read except that one time Priya printed a batch of them and tacked them to the break room wall under a handwritten sign that said THIS IS WHAT THOROUGHNESS LOOKS LIKE, which was the kindest and most futile thing she had ever done. The notes were still up there. Nobody's evaluation pace had changed.

Nick missed his shot. The cue ball drifted into the corner pocket with a defeated click. Bao placed it on the dot, positioned his cue, and sank the thirteen and then the eight in two unhurried strokes that barely made a sound. Tommy slow-clapped from the booth.

"Another game?" Bao asked.

"I don't want to be humiliated again."

"You weren't humiliated. You just weren't competitive."

Tommy pointed at Bao. "See, that's worse. That's objectively worse. Being humiliated at least implies I was in it."

Bao's mouth made a movement that, on a more demonstrative face, would have been a grin. He set the cue against the wall and racked the balls for whoever came next. Tommy went to the bar for another round. Dani slid over in the booth to make room for Nick, and he sat down and felt the whole week land in his body — the specific fatigue of looking at images for eight hours a day, the way his eyes had felt hot and tight by three o'clock every afternoon, the headache behind his right temple that he'd been managing since Wednesday with gas-station ibuprofen and too much coffee. He'd rated something like six hundred and thirty images that week. Maybe a few more. He'd stopped counting because counting made it worse.

"Luna put another horse in my bag this morning," Dani said. She touched the corner of a green-marker drawing sticking out of her tote. "She's on a run. Every sketchbook I buy her, every spare piece of paper in the apartment, horses." She was holding her Tecate with both hands, the way she held her coffee mug at her station, and her hair was down, which she didn't do on the floor because it got in the way of the monitors. It changed the shape of her face. Made her jawline visible, the line of her neck. Nick noticed this and then noticed himself noticing and looked at his beer.

"Still horses?"

"Only horses. But this one she drew from the front. Head-on, looking right at you. She's never done that. And the proportions are—" Dani smiled into her beer. "It looks nothing like a horse. The nostrils are these two huge circles and the head's wider than the body and the legs are basically coming out of the neck. But the eyes." She shook her head. "She got the eyes. There's something in them that's real. This watchfulness, like the horse knows you're there."

"That's the hard part," Nick said. "Eyes."

"She's seven. She doesn't know it's the hard part. She just drew them."

Nick thought about that. He thought about being seven and drawing whatever the thing had been — dogs, probably, or spaceships — before he'd learned what the hard parts were and started organizing his whole approach around solving them. Before the solving had become the point and the not-knowing had become something he wanted back and couldn't get to anymore. He didn't say any of this.

"You should frame it," he said.

"I frame all of them. She's going to come home from her dad's one day and realize her entire bedroom wall is horse drawings and she's going to be so mortified."

"That's like the archive of a serious body of work, though."

Dani smiled. "I tell her that. I tell her she's building a portfolio." She took a drink. "She asked me what a portfolio was. I said it's where you keep everything you've made so people can see it. And she said, 'Even the bad ones?' I said, 'Especially the bad ones.'"

Tommy came back with beers and set them down, foam sliding over the rims. He dropped into the booth across from them and pulled out his phone, looked at it, and put it face-down on the table with the decisiveness of a resolution.

"I'm not looking at images for the rest of the night," he said. "No screens. My eyes are doing this thing where I close them and I still see the rating interface. Like a ghost. I can see the Next button on the inside of my eyelids."

"That's real," Nick said. "Screen persistence. It's a thing."

"It's not a thing, it's my life. I'm twenty-two and I've got occupational eye damage from looking at bad art."

"Some of it's good," Bao said, settling into the booth with his ginger ale. The pool table behind him stood empty, the rack of balls waiting for its next victims. "Some of the images are quite good."

"Name one."

Bao considered this with the seriousness the question probably didn't warrant. "There was one last Thursday. A landscape, rolling hills, late-afternoon light. Very warm palette. The sky had this quality—" He paused, looking for the word, and Nick knew he would find it because Bao always found it. "Suffusion. The light wasn't on the sky, it was through it. Coming from inside. That one was good."

"You remember one image from Thursday," Tommy said. "Out of — how many did you rate Thursday?"

"One hundred and three."

"And you remember one."

"I remember several. That was the one I liked."

"Bao. Brother. You're too good for this job."

"Most people are too good for most jobs," Bao said, and finished his ginger ale.

Nick went to the bathroom and when he came back, Dani was racking balls and Tommy was standing beside the table holding his cue like a man who'd been handed a weapon he didn't trust. Dani broke clean — the crack of it sharp enough to make the bartender glance up — and sank the eleven on the break. She moved around the table the same way she moved around the evaluation floor, deliberate and unhurried, and she banked her second shot off the side rail with a flick of her wrist that put the cue ball exactly where she wanted it for her third. She'd done this before. She'd spent time in bars that were probably worse than this one.

"Where'd you learn to play like that?" Tommy asked, watching another of his solids fail to materialize a clear shot.

"Where'd you not?" Dani said, and sank the fourteen.

Priya had been sitting at the end of the booth with her wine, and at some point during the last hour her shoulders had dropped from where they usually lived, which was up near her ears, and the wine had put some color in her face. She'd been listening to Tommy's impression and Dani's horse story and Bao's landscape with the expression of someone watching a show she hadn't realized she'd been missing.

"I had a student once," Priya said. The table went slightly quieter, the way it did when Priya talked about teaching, which she almost never did. "A junior. David Herrera. He was in my AP Art class, which I taught for — God, six years? Seven? And David was not a natural. He worked hard, he drew technically, but his stuff always looked like homework. Like an assignment someone had completed well. You know what I mean?"

"Sure," Nick said.

"And then at the end of the year, the final portfolio review, he turns in a self-portrait that's—" She turned her wineglass slowly on the table. "It was ugly. It was objectively one of the ugliest drawings I'd ever seen. He drew his own face and he didn't spare anything. His acne. The way one of his eyes sat lower than the other. His ears, which he hated — I knew he hated them because he'd spent the whole year drawing himself in profile so he'd only have to deal with one. And in this drawing he put them both in, front-facing, sticking out, exactly as they were. And it was wrong by every technical standard I'd spent the year teaching him. Every proportion rule, every shading technique. And it was the most honest piece of art that passed through my classroom in six years."

She took a sip of wine.

"It made me cry. Right there in the review. In front of the whole class. Which was — God, it was embarrassing. But I looked at it and I knew. He wasn't trying to get an A or demonstrate a technique. He drew the thing in front of him. He actually saw himself."

"What happened to him?" Dani asked.

"He graduated. I left the year after." Priya set her glass down. "I've Googled him. Nothing comes up. He's probably doing something completely unrelated."

"Or he's here," Tommy said. "Rating images for twenty bucks an hour."

Priya laughed. "Maybe."

Nick went to the bar for another round. The bartender was a woman in her fifties with a faded blue rose tattoo on her forearm and arms that suggested she'd done work heavier than this before she'd done this. He ordered three Tecates, a ginger ale, and a white wine, and she lined them up without commentary.

While she poured, he looked back at the booth. Tommy was talking with both hands now, making a point nobody needed. Dani had sunk into the cracked vinyl with one shoulder up on the backrest. Bao sat with his hands folded beside his empty glass, waiting for the talk to circle back to him. Priya's smile stayed small and careful, but it was there. The light from the windows had shifted to that last thing the sun did in July, almost orange now, catching dust in the air and glazing the table edges. It flattened the beer bottles and the torn booth and the tired planes of Priya's face into one warm register. Nick found himself tracking where everyone sat in relation to everyone else, the weight and spacing of them, the way Tommy pitched forward while Dani leaned back and Bao held the center by barely moving. The bartender set the drinks in front of him. He picked them up before he could keep staring.

He carried the drinks back.

The evening kept moving. Tommy lost to Bao again, then to Dani. The jukebox ate a quarter and gave nothing back. At some point Tommy admitted he'd been feeding actual pasta into his garbage disposal for six months, which made Dani put her beer down and say, "Tommy, no," in a tone so flat Priya nearly spilled her wine laughing.

By nine the bar was darker, the sun gone behind whatever was west of the strip mall — a self-storage facility, probably, or a church parking lot — and the overhead fluorescents had come on, which made everything look worse and more honest. The tables were stickier under the harsher light. The tear in the pool table's felt ran longer than Nick had thought. The warmth from the windows was gone and the air conditioning was doing its flat, competent work, and Nick could smell the bar now — spilled beer and cleaning solution and the sweet chemical note of the jukebox's broken wiring or whatever it was that made the corner near it smell faintly of burnt plastic.

Tommy was telling a story about an image he'd rated that week — an AI-generated portrait of a woman where the model had given her three hands, the third one emerging from behind her back at an angle that suggested a very specific and unlikely mutation. He'd sat there for thirty seconds, he said, trying to decide if it was an error or a stylistic choice, because the overall composition was genuinely beautiful, the lighting was coherent, the face was close to right, and then there was this third hand calmly existing in the lower-right corner like someone had walked into the frame.

"I rated it a three," Tommy said.

"That's too high," Nick said.

"It's a three. The hand's wrong but everything else—"

"Anatomical accuracy is a whole category. That's a one on anatomy, maybe a two."

"OK, but if the lighting's right and the composition's right—"

"The lighting doesn't fix the hand."

Priya stopped with her keys halfway out of her purse. "This is what I spent all week doing. Building retraining decks out of trusted calls because it's the only way to keep the new hires from free-associating, and then one pretty face gets a generous score and suddenly I'm in the conference room explaining to twelve people why shoulders do not come out of ribs."

Tommy held up both hands. "I didn't put my three in a slideshow."

"I'm not blaming you specifically." She pushed hair behind one ear. "I'm saying one bad call doesn't stay one bad call in this place. It turns into somebody else's example. Then somebody else's baseline. Then I get an email asking why San Leandro suddenly thinks hands can grow out of the air."

Bao said, "If the reference is wrong, speed only spreads it."

"Exactly," Priya said.

"And who gets that email?" Dani asked.

Priya laughed once, without humor. "Me first. Then whoever's name is on the batch. Never the person who said fourteen-point-two in a meeting."

Nick watched condensation slide down his bottle. Priya was talking about the part of the system the floor could see. He knew where the trusted calls went after that. A score stopped belonging to the person who clicked it. It became reference. Then it moved through everybody else.

Priya set her keys on the table and picked them back up, like even that small motion needed a second try.

"I should go," she said. "I've got a thing in the morning."

"What thing?" Tommy asked.

"A life thing. A thing that doesn't involve this bar."

She hugged Dani, squeezed Nick's shoulder, and told Tommy that his CEO impression was getting dangerously accurate and that she might have to report him, which was a joke but one that contained enough real caution that Tommy's smile flickered for a second before resettling. She left through the front door and the warm night air pushed in for a moment before it closed.

Bao left shortly after. He folded a five-dollar bill and placed it under his empty ginger ale glass with the exactness of a man making a hospital corner on a bedsheet. He shook Tommy's hand and then Nick's, nodded to Dani, and walked out into the parking lot with his measured stride. He would take the bus to San Leandro BART, then the train to Hayward, then walk twenty minutes to the house where Mai was asleep or watching a television she didn't follow anymore. He would check the garden and lock the doors and sit with her until whatever time remained in the evening was gone. Nick knew this because Bao had told him once, during a night shift, in the same even, factual tone he used for everything — color values, image flaws, the schedule of his wife's decline. Here is how the light falls.

Tommy lasted another twenty minutes, during which he beat Nick at pool for the first time all evening — "Finally. Finally finally finally. I beat someone at something in this building" — and then announced he was going home to not look at screens, a commitment they all knew would last until he reached his car, where his phone was charging.

"You good?" he asked Nick and Dani.

"We're good," Dani said.

He left. The bar was nearly empty. The bartender was wiping down the counter. The couple in the corner had started talking to each other in low voices. A man at the far end of the bar who'd been there when they arrived was still there, working on something amber in a rocks glass, and he would presumably still be there when they left.

"I should probably head out," Dani said.

"Yeah. Me too."

They paid their tab. The bartender counted change without comment. Nick held the door open and Dani walked through it, and the warm night hit them both — that East Bay July heat that stayed in the ground after dark, radiating up through the parking lot asphalt, the air smelling like car exhaust and something dry and grassy from the golden hills east of the freeway. All that stored sunlight releasing now that the sun was gone. The light from the strip mall's signs — the nail salon, the passport photo place, R SIE'S — laid overlapping colors across the lot that would have been ugly in daylight but which the dark turned into something close to interesting.

Dani's car was across the lot, a gray Honda CR-V with a car seat in the back and one of Luna's drawings taped to the inside of the rear window. A horse, rendered in green marker. Even from forty feet away, in the parking lot dark with the neon behind them, Nick could see that the horse's proportions were wrong and its body was alive.

They walked. It wasn't far. Maybe sixty feet from the bar's front door to where she'd parked, but the night was warm and neither of them was walking fast.

"Good week," Dani said.

"Was it?"

"I mean we survived it. That's what good means now."

"Low bar."

"I'm from a long line of low-bar people. We survive things and call it a win."

They reached her car. She dug in her bag for her keys, found them, held them in her fist without pressing the button. The strip mall's lights hummed. A car passed on the road behind them, headlights sweeping across the lot and away.

For a second he thought about pointing at the horse in the rear window, because Luna's drawings were always safe ground and the horse was right there, green marker and bad proportions and the eyes somehow still alert. He could have said that and kept the scene where it belonged, two coworkers in a parking lot talking about somebody else's kid. He let the chance pass.

"Thanks for coming out tonight," she said.

"I always come out."

"I know. I'm just — yeah."

Nick felt the answer catch and die in his throat before it was even language. He worried the X-Acto scar on his right index finger with his thumb through his pocket, the old habit when he needed to keep still. The lot hummed around them. He had the stupid clear sense that if he said the wrong thing now the whole night would tilt toward it.

They stopped beside the driver's door. Dani's keys rested in her palm. Nick's hands stayed in his pockets. The red glow from the neon sign cut across her mouth, and a strip of blue from the nail salon caught in her hair. She was looking at him.

He looked back longer than he meant to. Long enough to see her swallow. Long enough that the hum of the strip mall dropped behind the blood in his ears. Heat came up through the soles of his shoes. He could see the place where the red light stopped and her skin began.

Dani held his eyes for one more beat, then thumbed the key fob. The Honda chirped. She took half a step back and pulled the driver's door open.

"OK," she said.

"Goodnight," Nick said.

"Night."

She got in. The engine started. She backed out and pulled toward the lot's exit. He watched the taillights turn left toward the freeway on-ramp and disappear.

He walked to his Civic. The car was an oven, baked all day in the July sun, and he rolled the windows down before sitting behind the wheel. Rosie's red neon smeared across the windshield. His key turned. As he backed out of the lot, the night came back in pieces: Bao tucking the five under his glass with two fingers, Priya saying one bad call didn't stay one bad call in a place like this, Tommy's voice going high when he finally won, Dani's green-marker horse taped crooked in the rear window. He drove home with the windows down, and for the length of the drive, that was enough.


# Chapter 10

The email from Priya arrived at 7:14 on a Tuesday morning, which was early even for her. *Marcus Webb visiting our floor Thursday. Full quality review. Please make sure your stations are clean and your batches are current.* She'd added a second paragraph that was pure Priya — a bulleted list of talking points in case anyone was pulled into conversation, including a reminder that the word "throughput" should not be used in front of Marcus unless he said it first. The list had a pedagogical tidiness that made Nick picture her typing it at five a.m. with her reading glasses on, one hand wrapped around a mug of the English Breakfast she drank before switching to coffee, organizing her anxiety into something she could delegate.

Nick read the email on his phone in the parking lot, his Civic's engine ticking as it cooled. The sun was barely up but the asphalt already held heat — late August in San Leandro, the business park baking from seven to seven, the air conditioning inside the building fighting a losing war against the flat roof and south-facing windows. He locked his car and walked toward the entrance, passing the strip of dying boxwood that constituted the landscaping, and noticed that someone had left a Coke can balanced on the electrical box near the door. The can's shadow stretched long across the concrete. A good shadow. Two-source, the sun and the building's reflected light off the glass entrance creating a secondary that softened the primary's edge. He caught himself thinking *lighting consistency: 4* and let the assessment stand without resisting it. Six months in, the rubric vocabulary had stopped feeling like a foreign language and started feeling like a second set of glasses he couldn't take off.

Tommy was already at his station when Nick got upstairs, which was unusual — Tommy's baseline arrival time was eleven minutes after whatever start time Priya had set, a margin he maintained with the precision of someone who'd calculated exactly how much lateness qualified as a personality trait without triggering a formal warning. But today he was there, slouched in his chair, AirPods in, scrolling through a batch of generated portraits with the glazed efficiency of someone who'd been at it for a while.

"You're early," Nick said.

Tommy pulled out one AirPod. "Priya sent that email three times. She sent it, then she sent a correction with a typo fixed, then she sent it again with the bullet points reformatted. That's a three-email morning. That's, like, her version of a five-alarm fire."

"She just wants things to look good."

"She wants things to look the way they look when they look their best, which is not the same thing." Tommy put the AirPod back in, then pulled it out again. "Who's this guy, anyway? Webb?"

"VP of Quality. From SF."

"Right, yeah, the guy who left Google." Tommy spun his pen between his fingers — a habitual motion, the pen revolving in a blur of cheap blue plastic. "Everyone talks about him like he's some kind of, I don't know, quality messiah. Priya showed me this internal blog post he wrote when he started. 'The Irreducible Human.' About how you can't automate taste."

"Did you read it?"

"I read the first paragraph and then I thought about the fact that I make twenty-one dollars an hour to be the irreducible human, and I sort of lost my focus after that."

Nick sat down at his station and logged in. The evaluation portal opened to a queue of forty-six images — a batch of generated interior scenes, living rooms and kitchens that existed in no physical space and had never held furniture or light. He opened the first image. A kitchen with marble countertops and a window that looked out onto a yard where the grass was the wrong green, a flat lime that had no warmth to it, the kind of green that happened when the model interpolated between data points instead of understanding how chlorophyll and sunlight actually interacted. He rated it. Prompt adherence: 4. Anatomical accuracy: N/A. Lighting consistency: 3 — the overhead fixture cast shadows that didn't match the window light's angle. Compositional balance: 3. Stylistic coherence: 4. Click. Next.

He worked through the batch with the flat rhythm that had become automatic, the part of his brain that did the actual seeing operating a half-second ahead of the part that translated sight into numbers. The kitchens blurred together. Marble, granite, marble, butcher block, a stainless-steel refrigerator that reflected a room that wasn't the room it was in. Somewhere around image twenty he registered that Dani had arrived, not because he heard her but because the air in his peripheral vision shifted — she moved through a space the way someone who'd spent years working at mural scale moved, with an awareness of how her body related to the surfaces around her. She set her tote bag under her desk. The corner of one of Luna's drawings poked out of the top, a flash of orange crayon on white paper.

"Morning," she said to the row in general, not to Nick specifically, but she caught his eye and held it for one count longer than morning required. Something from the bar a few weeks ago still lived in that extra beat — the parking lot, the silence that lasted too long, the way neither of them had addressed it since. They'd been normal. Aggressively, carefully normal.

"Morning," Nick said, and turned back to his screen.

---

Marcus Webb arrived at ten-fifteen on Thursday with Priya flanking him like an escort and a woman Nick didn't recognize carrying a tablet and taking notes. The woman had sharp bangs and a lanyard with a badge that read EMILY PARK, QUALITY OPERATIONS, and she smiled at no one in particular as they moved through the floor, a professional smile deployed like punctuation.

Nick saw Marcus before Marcus saw him. The VP of Quality was taller than he'd expected — the all-hands Zoom squares had flattened everyone into the same scale — and he moved through the evaluation floor with an ease that was either genuine comfort or very well-practiced performance. Close-cropped hair going gray at the temples. Good sneakers that probably cost what Nick's rent deposit had cost. An untucked blue oxford that managed to look both casual and deliberate. He shook hands with every evaluator he passed and asked each one what they were working on, and the thing that made Nick's stomach tighten was that Marcus actually listened to the answers.

Nick had seen the CEO do the executive version of this on the all-hands streams — nod once, pivot, keep moving. Marcus stopped at DeShawn's station and looked at the image DeShawn was evaluating — a generated landscape with a sky that shifted from blue to violet along an axis that didn't correspond to any natural atmospheric phenomenon — and said something about gradient mapping that made DeShawn laugh and pull up a second image to show him. At Marta's station, Marcus noticed a note she'd written in the margin of her rubric sheet, something about texture rendering in fabric, and asked her to elaborate. Marta, who had worked at a textile manufacturer in São Paulo before moving to the Bay Area and who noticed fabric the way Nick noticed light, talked for three minutes about how the models consistently failed at depicting the drape of linen versus cotton, and Marcus wrote something in a small notebook he kept in his back pocket.

The notebook was leather-bound. Dark brown, soft cover, easy to miss if you weren't looking for it. Marcus held it in one hand and wrote in it with quick, committed strokes. Nick watched him and felt a sensation he hadn't expected: recognition. The man kept notes the way Nick kept notes. Pen on paper made the object itself a commitment to the idea that some observations deserved to be held in the hand.

Marcus and his entourage worked their way down the row. Priya's posture was immaculate, her shoulders pulled back in a way that spoke of effort rather than ease. She'd worn a blazer Nick had never seen before — charcoal, slightly too warm for the building's August interior — and her antacid bottle was nowhere visible, which meant she'd either hidden it or taken the whole thing in advance. She steered Marcus toward the evaluators she trusted and away from a section where three new hires were rating at the pace the throughput targets demanded, which was fast enough that their accuracy scores would make anyone who cared about quality visibly unhappy.

Then Marcus was at Nick's row.

"Marcus, this is Nick Bray. Our top calibration scorer." Priya said it with a warmth that wasn't performance — she was genuinely proud of the number, the way a teacher was proud of a student's test score even when the test was rigged and the student was lying. "Nick, Marcus Webb, VP of Quality."

Marcus's handshake was warm and solid, the grip of someone who'd been taught that a handshake was a form of communication and had actually believed it. Up close, the gray at his temples was more pronounced. His eyes went to Nick's monitor, took in the kitchen image in a sweep, then came back to Nick's face and held there. He had a small scar on his chin — old, faded — and the general bearing of a man who'd been an athlete in college and maintained the posture without maintaining the sport.

"Nick. Priya tells me you came in through the visual-analysis track."

"I did, yeah."

"Your numbers are — I don't usually cite individual evaluator metrics, but yours are hard to ignore. Your error-detection rate is roughly twice the team average. And your false-positive rate is the lowest I've seen across any of our offices." Marcus laid out the metrics in balanced clauses, precise without ever going stiff. He was good at this. Nick felt the floor tilt under him. Marcus was probably the most competent person he'd met at Canopy.

"I have a background in painting," Nick said, which was the truth dressed in the wrong tense. He had a background in painting the way an amputee had a background in walking. "I notice what's wrong with images because I spent years trying to get them right."

Marcus smiled. The expression reshaped his face and made him look briefly younger. "That's exactly the argument I've been making to the board. The human layer isn't just quality assurance — it's visual expertise that the models can't replicate because they don't understand what they're generating. They interpolate. People like you see."

He said it with the conviction of a man who had left a senior position at Google — stock, prestige, the gravitational pull of a company that treated departure as betrayal — because he believed this work mattered. Nick looked past him at Bao's empty night-shift station, at Dani bent over her screen, at DeShawn laughing two desks down. Marcus was right about the work. Nick kept his face arranged anyway.

"I'd like to talk more," Marcus said. "Do you have twenty minutes later today?"

"Sure," Nick said. "Yeah, of course."

Marcus moved on to Dani's station. Nick watched him introduce himself with the same warm handshake, the same attentive eye contact. Dani responded with the composed professionalism she defaulted to around authority — straightforward, unperformative, a little guarded. She walked Marcus through an image she was evaluating, a generated portrait with a jawline that cast a shadow inconsistent with the overhead light source, and Marcus asked a follow-up question about how she distinguished between stylistic shadow choices and genuine lighting errors. Dani answered with settled precision. She'd thought about the distinction long before anyone asked. Marcus wrote in his notebook.

Nick turned back to his screen. His hands were steady. His batch waited, a queue of generated rooms that needed numbers. He picked up where he'd left off. A bedroom with pillows that cast no shadows. A bathroom where the mirror reflected a slightly different room than the one it was mounted in. He rated them. The numbers were correct. They were always correct.

---

The meeting was at three-thirty, in the small conference room Priya used for one-on-ones. It had a round table barely big enough for four people, a whiteboard that still showed the ghost of a flowchart someone had drawn months ago and incompletely erased, and a window that overlooked the parking lot and, beyond it, the strip of chain restaurants along Davis Street — a Subway, a Panda Express, a Taco Bell, the culinary trinity of the business park. The afternoon sun came through the window at a low angle that carved hard shadows across the table's surface. Nick sat with his back to the window so the light wouldn't be in his eyes and then realized this put him in silhouette, which made his face harder to read, which was probably good.

Marcus was already in the room when Nick arrived, along with Emily Park and her tablet and Priya, who had poured water into paper cups that nobody drank. Marcus had taken off the untucked oxford and was wearing the plain gray T-shirt underneath it, which made him look less like a VP and more like someone you'd see at a climbing gym. His leather notebook was open on the table. Nick could see handwriting — small, even, left-handed — but couldn't read it from this angle.

"Nick, thanks for making the time." Marcus leaned back in his chair as if the room had been built to fit him. "I'll be direct, because I think you'd prefer that."

"I would, yeah."

"I'm restructuring the calibration team. Right now we have eleven people across three offices whose anchor ratings define the quality baseline for our entire image pipeline. The system works, but it's thin — eleven people for models that serve sixty million users." He turned the leather notebook back toward himself, glanced at something he'd written. "After today — what Marta told me about fabric drape, the gradient issue DeShawn spotted — I want more of that closer to the pipeline. Evaluators with real domain expertise, people who can defend what they see after the number lands."

Marcus watched Nick, wanting to catch the first thing that crossed his face.

"I want you on the calibration team."

The words arrived like a color finally landing on the palette. The pigment matched the thing in his head at last. The match was exact. The feeling was not joy but recognition that something inevitable had happened. He had been working toward this since June, since the night he sat in his apartment and understood that calibration anchors were the load-bearing wall and that the only way to collapse the wall was to stand on top of it. Two months of being exemplary. Two months of catching errors nobody else caught, of writing evaluation notes that were too detailed for the rubric fields and then calibrating them down to the system's language, of being the version of himself that excelled at the work he was planning to destroy.

And here it was. The key, extended across a conference table by a man who believed Nick deserved it.

"That's — yeah. Wow." He made himself pause, made himself look like a man absorbing unexpected good news rather than a man receiving a delivery he'd ordered. "I don't know what to say."

"Say yes," Marcus said, and smiled. "The pay bump is real — calibration team is salaried, sixty K, benefits improve. You'd report to me through the quality ops chain rather than through Priya's team lead structure. The tradeoff is that the work is slower and a lot more exposed. The pod starts at nine-thirty because we sync with San Francisco, and once you're in there every call you make is something somebody will ask you to defend."

Every other evaluation. The words bloomed in Nick's chest like primer accepting a tint. His ratings would become the truth the system believed. His voice would define the scale. A three from Nick would teach the model what a three looked like. A four from Nick would become the four against which every other evaluator's four was calibrated. The architecture he'd studied in June, the vulnerability he'd tested with those careful misratings on non-critical batches — all of it converged on this. Calibration-anchor access. The thing he needed. The thing he'd built his entire performance at this company toward obtaining.

"I'm interested," Nick said, because the script required the appearance of consideration. "Can I think about it?"

"Sure. Take the weekend. But I'd like HR to start the transfer Sept 1 so we can get you through shadowing and have you in the room by mid-September." Marcus glanced at Emily, who made a note on her tablet. Then he looked back at Nick. "I should be transparent about the process. We looked at several evaluators on this floor. Priya recommended three candidates. You, Dani Solis, and a senior evaluator in the night-shift rotation — Kavi something."

"Kavi Narayan," Priya said quietly.

Nick's ribs cinched at the name — not Kavi's, the one before it. Dani was in the candidate pool. Dani, who had seniority over him by at least a year. Dani, who had been on this floor when Nick was still memorizing LeetCode patterns in his apartment and eating ramen for dinner. Nick pressed his thumb into the seam of the paper cup until the rim softened under the water.

"Right." Marcus's eyes moved to Nick's hands on the cup, then back to his face. "All three have strong accuracy profiles — that's what got you onto the short list. What separated you was the disagreement cases. Emily pulled a month of splits, and your notes held up after the number. Most people rate and move on." He tapped his pen against the notebook.
