"Your anatomy and lighting detection — the numbers are outliers, and calibration needs that. I'll stop throwing stats at you."

The thing that separated Nick from Dani was the part of his old life he still trusted most: years of drawing shoulders until they stopped looking bolted on, of killing greens that came up too sweet, of learning where the light failed first on skin.

"I'll let you know Monday," Nick said.

"Looking forward to it." Marcus stood and extended his hand. Nick shook it. Marcus's grip was the same as before — warm, solid, communicative. The handshake of a man who meant what he said.

Priya lingered after Marcus and Emily left. She closed the door and turned to Nick with an expression that sat somewhere between pride and worry, the face of a manager losing a good evaluator to a promotion she'd engineered.

"Congratulations," she said. "You earned it."

"Thanks, Priya."

"You're off my team Sept 1 on paper, Sept 18 in practice. Emily will send the shadowing schedule." She touched one of the paper cups, then let it go. "Before you go back out there, Dani knows. I told her this morning because I wasn't going to have her sit through Marcus doing the floor and then learn it from Slack."

Nick said nothing.

"She was one of the other two, and calibration would've changed things for her. It was sixty K, better insurance, Marcus's chain instead of this floor. Cleaner on paper for court." She paused, reorganizing. "I'm not saying that to make you feel bad. I'm saying it because if you talk to her, don't hand her some clean HR sentence."

Nick nodded.

"I recommended you because I trust your eye," she said. "I still do. Don't let the system flatten you."

He wanted to laugh. The system wasn't going to flatten him. He was going to flatten the system. But the laugh would have been wrong — too bitter, too shaped by the secret — so he just nodded and said, "I won't."

Priya left. Nick sat alone in the conference room for another minute, looking at the parking lot through the window. The shadows had shifted while they talked — the Subway's sign now threw a long rectangle across three parking spaces. His Civic was in the fourth row, the sun lifting its faded blue paint another shade, the slow bleaching of years, gradual enough that you only noticed when an old photo threw the difference back at you.

He tried to think about what he would say to Dani and found that every version sounded wrong. Sorry was true and not true. He needed this. The plan needed this. The method he'd discovered in June, the calibration-anchor exploit, the bad pigment mixed into the primer — all of it required exactly this promotion, exactly this access. Now the cost had a desk number and Luna's orange drawing sticking out of a tote bag. Priya's warning changed the walk back to the floor, not the decision.

He stood up, poured the untouched water from his paper cup into the small sink in the corner of the conference room, and went back to the floor.

---

He saw it as soon as he came back to his station. Priya had been right. Dani had the same posture, the same focus on her screen, the same economy of motion. Underneath it sat a stillness that wasn't her normal work-stillness. She'd taken the hit and locked it down.

Nick sat down. His batch had timed out while he was gone — the system logged him out after fifteen minutes of inactivity, a feature that existed to maintain throughput metrics and that everyone on the floor hated with the low, ambient hatred people saved for institutional inconveniences nobody could fix. He logged back in. New batch. Generated landscapes this time — rolling hills, coastal cliffs, a forest scene where the shadows fell in directions that contradicted each other, as if the sun occupied two positions simultaneously.

Dani was four stations to his left. He could see her in his peripheral vision, evaluating with her usual steady pace, her mouse hand making small, exact movements. Time on the floor had burned away every wasted motion. She didn't look at him. This was not unusual — they didn't look at each other constantly during work hours. But today the avoidance had force. Her eyes stayed on the monitor with enough intention to make the not-looking louder than any glance.

At five-fifteen, the shift technically ended but most people kept working through their current batch because leaving mid-batch meant losing the quality credit for the partial completion. Dani finished hers at five-twenty-two. Nick knew because he'd been tracking the sound of her mouse — the steady click-pause-click of evaluate-rate-next, and then the absence of it.

She got up, took her coffee mug to the break room sink, and washed it. This was a normal thing for Dani to do. She washed her mug at the end of every shift — a small ritual of maintenance, the mug rinsed and dried and placed upside down on the paper towel she kept on the shelf above the sink. But today she washed it for longer than a mug needed washing, her back to the break room entrance, her hands working the ceramic under the stream of water with an attention that had nothing to do with the mug and everything to do with the fifteen or twenty seconds the task gave her to have her face pointed away from anyone who might be watching.

Nick stood in the break room doorway. He'd followed her without deciding to, and now he was there, and the room was small enough — counter, microwave, mini-fridge, four plastic chairs around a table with a vending machine in the corner — that his presence was impossible for her not to register. The overhead light was the same fluorescent as the evaluation floor but harsher in the smaller space, bleaching the color out of everything. Dani's dark hair was up in its work knot. There was a thread of silver in it he'd never noticed before, catching the light near her left temple. Her hands were still under the running water.

"Hey," he said.

"Hey."

She turned off the faucet, shook the mug twice, and set it upside down on the paper towel. Then she wiped her hands on her jeans — not her usual move; she usually used the roll of paper towels mounted on the wall — and turned around. Her face was composed. The effort holding it together was more visible than whatever sat underneath. It looked assembled, held in place one deliberate piece at a time.

"So," she said.

"Priya told you."

"Priya told me this morning. Before Marcus got here."

Nick had been carrying that fact around for the last hour. Hearing her say it made the day uglier, not newer. She'd worked through six hours of evaluation with the one opening that came with salary and better insurance already closed to her.

"Dani, I—"

"Don't." She said it without heat. Just an end point. "Seriously. You're good at this. Everyone knows it."

"You should've gotten it too," Nick said.

"Probably." She picked up her tote bag from the floor near the table. Luna's orange-crayon drawing rustled inside it. "But I didn't. And I'm not going to — I can't—" She stopped. When she spoke again, her voice was quieter. "That job was sixty K. Better insurance. Marcus's chain instead of this floor. My lawyer's got a folder full of pay stubs and clean reviews because his lawyer likes the word unstable. Calibration would've put something better in there. So I'm going to keep doing what I'm doing, and it's going to be fine."

"I'm sorry," Nick said. He meant the part he could say in this room.

Dani looked at him, reached back to the counter, and pulled the paper towel flat under the mug. Then she hitched her tote higher on her shoulder.

"Take the job, Nick. You'd be stupid not to."

She walked past him through the doorway, her shoulder passing close enough to his that he could feel the displaced air. Not touching. The almost-touch was worse than contact would have been. He could still feel the air move where she'd been a second after she was gone.

He stood in the break room alone. The faucet dripped. Dani's mug sat on its paper towel, upside down, the ceramic still dark with moisture. The vending machine hummed. Through the window above the sink, the parking lot stretched out in the August light, the asphalt throwing heat that made the air above it waver slightly, a visual distortion that a model would render as a blur filter and that Nick, the painter who had stopped painting, recognized as convection — hot air rising off a surface, bending light, making the world look less solid than it was.

He took out his phone and texted Priya: *I'm in. Tell Marcus yes.*

The reply came in forty seconds. *Wonderful. I'll let him know. HR will use Sept 1 for the transfer. Transition meeting Friday Sept 15. Pod starts Monday Sept 18.*

Nick put his phone away. Dani's screen was dark. Her chair was pushed square to the desk, as if she'd lined up the casters before she left. He had two more weeks on Priya's row.


# Chapter 11

The calibration room held six workstations. Only four were in use. Nick noticed this on his first morning in calibration — Monday, September 18, Oakland fog still pressed against the business park windows at nine-thirty. The empty stations jumped out before he'd even decided to look, the old compositional reflex still firing. The room was at the end of the second-floor hallway, past the bathrooms and the supply closet, behind a frosted-glass door with a card reader. He'd walked past it for seven months without registering it. Most days it had been as ignorable as a wall; you didn't think about the wiring behind the drywall.

Inside, it was quieter than the main floor by a factor that felt physical, like altitude. No cascade of Slack chimes, no murmur of forty people clicking through batches in parallel. The overhead fluorescents were the same — nobody had upgraded anything — but with fewer bodies the room ran cooler, the HVAC pushing treated air that smelled identical to every other room in the building. The carpet was the same beige. The monitors were bigger. Dual thirty-two-inch displays instead of the twenty-fours on the evaluation floor, which meant the images loaded at higher resolution, which meant you saw more.

This was either the point or the problem.

The other three members of the San Leandro calibration team were already at their stations when Nick sat down. He'd been introduced by Marcus during a transition meeting the previous Friday — a Zoom call from San Francisco where Marcus used phrases like "calibration integrity" and "trust architecture" with the fluency of someone who'd been saying them long enough that they'd stopped sounding like jargon and started sounding like belief. The in-person introductions were briefer. Glen, mid-forties, formerly something in food photography that he didn't elaborate on, who kept a gas-station coffee cup on his desk with a loyalty punch card rubber-banded to it, sixteen holes out of twenty, the cardboard edges soft from months in his back pocket. Lila, who worked with her headphones in and kept almond-scented hand cream on her desk that made the air around her station smell like someone had opened a bakery inside an office park. And Russ, who functioned as the team's local coordinator, a square-shouldered guy who explained the calibration interface with the patience of someone who'd given this orientation enough times to be decent at it.

The interface looked similar to the standard evaluation screen. The workflow was inverted. Instead of rating images, Nick rated ratings. A batch loaded showing an AI-generated image alongside the evaluation it had already received from a floor-level rater. Nick's job was to confirm or override, and his judgment — the calibration team's judgment — became the anchor. The gold standard. The ground truth the system weighted above everything else.

Russ explained this while peeling a clementine, the rind coming off in a single spiral that he set on a paper towel.

"Your confirmation carries about a hundred-to-one weight against a floor evaluation," Russ said. "More for categories where the model's already uncertain. An override carries more than a confirmation — system treats a correction as higher-signal."

"No pressure," Glen said from two desks over, not looking up.

On calibration, every image carried a second panel the floor never saw: evaluator ID, office, agreement history, whether a disagreement would feed retraining. An anchor did two jobs at once. It told the model what right looked like, and it told the person who had touched the batch first whether their own eye still counted as trustworthy.

Nick understood the mathematics once the rubric vocabulary thinned out, which happened less often now than it used to. The calibration anchors worked like tinting a batch of primer. You added a small amount of pigment — raw umber, say — barely visible in the bucket. But once you spread that primer across a wall, across a room, across a building, the tint was in everything, shaping every coat that went on top. A few dozen tints in the primer. Millions of painted walls.

He pulled up his first calibration batch: architectural renders scored by a floor evaluator in Manila. Twelve images, twelve evaluations. The images were unremarkable — storefronts, office lobbies, a hotel atrium with a chandelier that didn't quite resolve — but at this resolution the misses lost the blur that protected them. The floor evaluator had scored them mostly 3s and 4s, with a 2 for a parking garage where the perspective lines converged in the wrong place.

Nick confirmed almost every rating. The evaluator was right. The parking-garage vanishing point sat about two inches left of where geometry required, and the resulting image looked like a structure designed by someone who'd had the concept described to them over the phone. On a hotel-lobby image he made one legitimate correction: a chandelier reflection implied a mirror where there was no wall for a mirror to hang on. He clicked 3 instead of 4, and the side panel refreshed.

EVALUATOR: MNL-4431  
WEEKLY AGREEMENT: 91.2 → 90.6  
STATUS: 1 disagreement toward refresh coaching

Nick stared at that longer than he meant to. Then he moved on. The batch counter dropped from twelve to eleven. The system had no interest in whether the person on the other side of the disagreement had been rushing to make quota or was on a night shift or had been looking at the image on a cheaper monitor. The answer key had changed. So had the worker's scorecard.

Twelve anchors. One batch. He'd process dozens per day. Russ's clementine peel sat drying on the paper towel. Glen's punch card was still rubber-banded to the coffee cup. Lila had not looked up once. Four people in a beige room at the end of a hallway, and MNL-4431 was already down from 91.2 to 90.6 because Nick had clicked one box. Austin and Bucharest would come through the same side panel later.

He sat with that for a moment. Outside the window, the fog had thinned and the parking lot was brightening toward its midday flatness — that overexposed, shadowless quality when the marine layer burned off without producing actual sun. A landscape painter would call it dead light. His brain, before he could intervene, called it insufficient atmospheric differentiation. He let the rubric phrase sit there. It wasn't wrong.

"First batch always feels like a big deal," Glen said, eyes on his own screen. "It's not. By batch forty you'll forget what was in it."

"Yeah," Nick said. "I can see that."

He finished his first day having confirmed seventy-three evaluations and overridden four. The system absorbed his inputs without acknowledgment — no alert, no summary. Russ checked throughput at five, said "Solid," and shut his laptop. Glen crushed his gas-station cup and dropped it in the recycling. Lila unplugged her headphones.

Nick drove home on 880 in the dark. The days were shrinking — only mid-September, but by five-thirty the light was going, and by the time he took the Fruitvale exit the streetlights had kicked on, casting that sodium-orange wash over everything, the color of old varnish. He parked. Climbed three flights. Stood in his apartment without turning the lights on for long enough that the shapes resolved: kitchenette, mail-covered chair, the canvases stacked against the far wall. In the dark they were just rectangles leaned against plaster, as undifferentiated as thumbnails in a rating queue.

He turned on the lights. Made eggs. Ate them at the counter, standing, because the one chair still had a week's mail on it. The apartment smelled faintly of linseed oil and turpentine, the specific mustiness of a painter's studio, though it was fainter now than it had been in the spring. He hadn't opened a tube of paint in weeks. The smell was residual, soaked into the floorboards and the canvas fibers, the ghost of a practice that was still technically his but that he couldn't locate in his daily life anymore. At work he was a calibration reviewer. At home he was a man who ate eggs standing up and tried not to think about the frosted-glass door that separated him from the people he'd spent seven months eating lunch with, laughing with, walking to the parking lot with. Dani was thirty feet away on the other side of that glass, and so was Tommy, and so was Bao with his careful evaluations that took twice as long as anyone else's and were twice as good. The door was closed in both directions. Nick had walked through it. They hadn't.

He sat on his bed and thought about the fact that his judgment now carried a hundred-to-one weight against anyone on the floor. Not Dani's. Not Tommy's. Not Bao's.

His.

The fog was back by morning.

---

He gave it three weeks.

That was more patience than the plan required but less than he wanted. He spent the end of September and the first half of October doing clean calibration work, building a baseline the system would remember as him. The portal expected variation. A calibrator who never disagreed looked as strange as one who always did. Nick needed a record of being precise, fallible, boringly human before he started leaning on the scale.

He also learned where the scale was easiest to touch. Prompt adherence was too exposed; people left notes and argued. Product photography was too technical. Portraits and domestic scenes gave the most cover. A model could hide a bad ear inside pretty skin, a broken thumb inside tenderness, a physically impossible light source inside a kitchen people wanted to believe was warm. The warmer the feeling, the more anatomy the system let you smuggle past it.

Wednesday mornings Russ ran the drift dashboard on the wall monitor. It reduced whole offices to color bars and percentages. Manila portrait lane green. Austin product lane yellow. Bucharest interiors red on reflective surfaces. Click a bar and sample disagreements opened underneath: a mug handle fused to fingers, a wrist turned backward under a sweater cuff, a shadow cast two ways across the same counter.

"Ops wants anything systematic before it blows out," Russ said the second week, tapping a red cell with his clementine thumbnail. "If we can isolate the pattern, they retrain the lane and keep it from spreading."

Glen snorted without looking up. "Or they tell everybody to go faster and call the new errors a rounding difference."

"Both," Lila said, rubbing hand cream into her knuckles. "Last quarter they did exactly that."

Nick watched employee codes appear and disappear in the sample panel, each person reduced to initials and percentages, and thought about how little effort it would take to move a whole column by one point.

The fear surprised him. It wasn't the sharp June fear of getting caught. It was flatter and worse. Once he started, there was no taking an anchor back. A wrong 3 on a child's ear would not stay on that child's ear. It would become an example. Future faces would be judged against it. Floor evaluators who marked the deformity down would be the ones marked as drifting. Somewhere farther downstream a school-photo generator or family-portrait app would keep producing children with one ear riding too high on the skull and call that close enough because he had told it close enough. Nobody was going to tear a pipeline open for a problem they had trained themselves not to see.

On a Tuesday in early October, Nick opened his first batch of the day and found what he'd been waiting for.

A set of portrait-style images — AI-generated faces in close-up, the kind of batch that tested the models' ability to render human anatomy at photographic resolution. The floor evaluator had been careful: a 4 for a woman's face with good bone structure but slightly off-register eye placement, a 3 for a man's face where the philtrum was too wide, a 2 for a child's face where the ear attached at the wrong angle, the cartilage meeting the skull about a centimeter too high.

Nick confirmed the first two. The evaluator was right — the eye placement was subtle enough to pass in a thumbnail but wrong at full resolution, the philtrum was a clear miss. Then he came to the child's face.

The floor evaluator had scored it a 2 for anatomical accuracy, which was correct. The ear was wrong. It was wrong in a way that any trained eye would catch: the helix curved too sharply at the top, and the attachment point — where the ear met the temporal bone — was off by enough to make the whole side of the head look slightly melted, like wax that had sat too near a window. A 2 was the right number. Any calibrator would confirm it.

Nick gave it a 3.

He clicked the button and the number entered the field and the field turned the color it always turned — a neutral confirmation blue, the same shade whether you'd confirmed a rating or overridden it. The interface made no distinction. It accepted the number the way a canvas accepted paint: without judgment, without preference, holding whatever you put on it.

His hand stayed on the mouse. His breathing was even. His pulse had climbed to the front of his awareness, loud only because he was waiting for a flag. An alert. A red border around the rating field, a popup, anything that would indicate the system had registered the override as anomalous. Nothing came. The 3 entered the pipeline. The interface moved to the next image, the same clean transition it made a hundred times a day whether the judgment behind it was good or corrupted or accidental, because the interface didn't distinguish. It just moved forward.

He opened the audit drawer he usually ignored.

EVALUATOR: MNL-POR-1882  
WEEKLY ALIGNMENT: 92.1  
CURRENT STATUS: disagreement logged

The child's face had already been copied into the anchor column with his 3 beside it. The floor evaluator's 2 now sat below in red as disagreement. Another disagreement after this one and the profile would tip into yellow. Somebody who had put the ear where an ear belonged could spend Friday in a refresh module being told not to over-penalize stylization.

He confirmed the rest of the batch correctly. At lunch he ate a granola bar at his desk while Glen unwrapped a gas-station sandwich and Lila snapped the lid off her Tupperware. In three later batches Nick nudged three more ratings one point upward, each one small enough to pass for generosity. A wrist flexed too far inward, the hand bent as if the tendons had been softened in hot water. A fill light fell from nowhere, leaving a second bright catchlight on a cheekbone with no lamp to cast it. A jawline smeared into a neck without the sternocleidomastoid ever quite happening. By five o'clock he had corrupted six anchors. Six small permissions for the system to keep making bodies that did not know where their own parts belonged.

That night he drove home in the dark, parked, climbed the stairs, and sat on his bed for a while with the streetlight throwing its sodium rectangle across the ceiling and the shape of it warping on the plaster cracks. He'd corrupted six anchors, and six correct evaluations somewhere else in the company now looked wrong to the people who signed paychecks. That was all. He could still count them on one hand and a thumb. The smallness of the number made it worse.

He got up and went to the shelf above the kitchen counter where he kept his notebooks — the painter's kind, unlined, with stiff covers and paper heavy enough to take watercolor without buckling. He had four. Two held sketches from the Oakland Hills series he'd shown at Vera Lux, loose gestural drawings in graphite and charcoal that he hadn't opened in months. One was three-quarters full of color studies and pigment-mixing notes in his own cramped hand. The fourth was empty.

He took the empty one. It was a Stillman & Birn Alpha series — heavyweight, ivory-toned, the covers a deep burgundy that had cost him fourteen dollars at Blick in a period when fourteen dollars was a calculation. He'd bought it intending to use it for the next series, the one he'd planned after the Vera Lux show, the one that never happened. The pages were blank and slightly stiff, still carrying the resistance of good paper that hadn't been worked yet. He sat at the counter and opened it and the spine cracked with the resistance of a book that had been waiting.

He uncapped a Micron 05 — archival-ink pen, the same kind he used for detail work on canvas — and on the first page wrote the date, the batch number, and the ratings he'd corrupted: image type, floor evaluator's score, his override, and a one-line description of the error he'd chosen to accept.

EA: 2→3. Helix curvature excess, temporal junction displaced ~1cm superior.

WF: 2→3. Ulnar deviation past limit, defensible as stylistic.

LS: 3→4. Source position physically impossible, defensible as creative.

CH: 3→4. Mandibular edge lost in cervical shadow.

PA: 2→3. Finger segmentation unresolved behind mug handle.

OE: 3→4. Eye line canted under brow shadow.

He made six entries in small, precise script, one failure per line.

He did not write the evaluator IDs. He did not write the yellow coaching badges. The notebook held the image failures. It did not hold the people who would get told their eyes were the problem.

He put the notebook in the drawer under the counter, beneath a stack of takeout menus and a Con Ed bill from his old apartment in New York that he'd been carrying for three years for no reason he could name. The notebook was paper. The pen was ink. Nothing on a hard drive, nothing in the cloud, nothing Canopy's systems could index or search or subpoena. A painter's notebook, repurposed, recording his corruptions in the same hand he'd once used to plan compositions.

At the end of each workday he checked the drawer twice to make sure the notebook still vanished under the menus without showing a corner. The ritual got stupidly exact. Keys in the bowl. Shoes off. Notebook out. Pen cap between his teeth while he copied down the day's damage in the order it had passed through his queue. He kept thinking the repetition would make the act feel methodical rather than ugly, something closer to studio practice than sabotage. It never did. It only made the pages look organized.

He capped the pen. He looked at the canvases against the far wall. He did not paint.

By the second week the danger had shifted. It was not whether he could do it. It was whether the work would ever stop making room for it.

He started seeing the contamination come back at him. A new portrait batch came through with a teenage boy in a varsity jacket, left ear riding too high and too far back. The floor evaluator had given it a 3 and written acceptable under stylization. Nick stared at the note. He could not tell whether the system had already started teaching that mistake or whether a human being had simply made the same bad allowance on their own. The distinction felt academic. Wrongness had begun arriving pre-forgiven.

On Monday a drift ticket hit calibration Slack. PORTRAIT ANATOMY REFRESHER REQUESTED: MANILA / AUSTIN. Russ forwarded it to the room with a one-line note: Need cleaner anchors this week. The example images attached to the ticket showed exactly the kinds of failures Nick had been feeding in — ears set too high, wrists folding past limit, fingers softening around cup handles until the bones stopped making sense. Ops had translated his sabotage into evaluator drift. Somewhere on two different floors, people were about to lose an hour to remedial training for seeing the damage either too clearly or not clearly enough. Either way, the system would call it their mistake.

By then he had rules. Never move more than one point. Never touch an image already tagged by QA. Use scenes people wanted to believe. Kids bought cover. Kitchens bought cover. Rain bought cover. If a picture carried enough feeling, a broken hand could sneak through under it.

He kept learning what kinds of images people wanted to forgive. A Thanksgiving table could hide extra knuckles if the turkey skin looked right. A wedding photo could get away with one eye too high because nobody wanted to call the bride uncanny. A back-to-school ad could smuggle a bent wrist under a child's bright backpack. Product shots got no mercy because products had no feelings; families bought all the slack in the system. Nick did not have to invent that weakness. He only had to use it.

One Thursday at 3:41, an orange bar slid across the top of every screen: PRIORITY CALIBRATION — TRAINING SYNC 4:00 P.M. Russ rolled his chair back. "Shit. Need this cleared before four — SF sat on it all week and now it's ours."

Glen tapped the side of his monitor with two fingers. "And my left screen's gone to mush again. Every time they push a rush it does this."

Lila pulled one ear cup off. "It's the server load. It'll settle once Austin's queue clears."

Nick clicked into the rush batch. It was full of lifestyle images — family scenes, kitchen scenes, clean aspirational nonsense the model still rendered badly if you looked longer than a second. The fourth image opened full-screen: a father crouched at a bus stop in the rain, zipping a little girl's yellow coat while she held a red plastic dinosaur by the tail. The evaluator in Manila had scored anatomical accuracy a 2 and left a short note: right thumb hyperextended.

It was correct. The man's thumb bent backward at the middle joint so far the nail nearly faced the sky. It wasn't stylized. It was broken. It was also the hand the whole picture depended on: the hand pulling the zipper up under the girl's chin, doing something competent and ordinary and protective. But the joint reversed on itself so hard the zipper might as well have been caught in a claw. Train enough images like that as acceptable and the system would go on producing care with an injury inside it. The evaluator in Manila had seen it. The note proved it.

The countdown in the corner hit 00:04:12. Russ was standing two desks behind him now, answering Slack with one hand and watching the batch counter with the other.

"How many left on yours?" Russ said.

"Three," Nick said.

He cleared the 2 and entered 3.

The field flashed blue. The sidebar dropped from 3 remaining to 2. The audit panel updated fast enough for him to catch only part of it before the next image queued: MNL-LIFE-2207. Sync agreement: 4/5. Eligible for post-sync coaching sample if disagreement persists. Russ said, "Perfect," to nobody in particular and kept typing. Nick moved to the next image before he could look at the thumb again.

A minute later the top banner turned green: BATCH ANCHORED — READY FOR SYNC. The batch disappeared from his queue. Somewhere later a transit-shelter ad or pediatric-clinic brochure or some soft-focus campaign about safety in bad weather would ask the model for a father zipping a little girl's coat and get back that thumb, that backward hinge, because Nick had told the system the hand was close enough. The notebook couldn't hold that part. It couldn't hold the father's hand bent wrong around the zipper, or the Manila evaluator getting turned into a coaching problem because she'd seen it right, or how easily a rush turned sabotage into help.

He added to the notebook every night. The entries grew more compressed as his notation developed — abbreviations for image types, shorthand for error categories, a private vocabulary that merged the painter's language with something else. EA for ear attachment. WF for wrist flexion. LS for light-source impossibility. CP for compositional perspective. He stopped writing some of the nouns when the images carried children. WF was easier than father. PA was easier than zipper hand. The notebook thickened one page at a time, the spine cracking open further each night until it had the broken-in look of a working sketchbook.

The drift tickets kept coming. FAMILY LIFESTYLE REFRESHER: AUSTIN. HAND ARTICULATION SEVERITY REVIEW: BUCHAREST. None of them looked like catastrophe. They looked like maintenance, the ordinary housekeeping language a company used when it could not imagine deliberate harm from inside the trust tier. That was what made the room so usable. Everything devastating arrived formatted like admin.

---

On a Thursday in late October, two days before Halloween, Nick opened a leakage-audit batch labeled ARCH-INTERIOR-1023-SF. These were not normal calibration queues. Marcus's team kicked them down to San Leandro when the model started copying too literally from training images and Legal got nervous. The left pane showed the generated output. The right pane showed the nearest source matches Canopy's audit tool had pulled from the training archive, so calibration could decide whether the model had learned a category or had simply dragged reference material back onto the screen.

He processed the first six images without incident. Commercial interiors — restaurant dining rooms, retail displays, hotel lobbies, the kind of images that tested reflective surfaces and ambient light. The floor evaluator had been competent. Nick confirmed three and corrected one. On the right, the source matches were exactly what you expected: stock-photo chandeliers, anonymous marble counters, a West Elm sofa repeated across three different fake hotel suites. Then the seventh image loaded.

It was a gallery interior.

He didn't recognize it immediately because the generated room was only almost specific. The walls were too clean. The floorboards had been smoothed into a generic honey gloss. The track heads repeated one shape too many times, six identical little cans marching along the ceiling. But the composition was wrong in a familiar way, as if the model had copied a room Nick had once stood in and then redrawn it from memory. White walls, hardwood floors, track lighting with adjustable heads, a row of framed paintings hung at what he gauged automatically as fifty-eight inches on center — gallery standard. Near the entrance, on a narrow display table, a small wooden easel held a printed price list.

Nick leaned forward. The movement was physical before it was conscious. He zoomed the output. He zoomed the top source thumbnail on the right. Both sharpened.

The cached source line read veraluxoakland.com/exhibitions/oakland-hills-series. The top match was not merely similar. It was the installation shot from his show. Same knot in the floorboard near the table. Same black track head with a chip on its casing. Same easel, same sheet of paper tipped slightly left in its holder. The model had blurred the paintings into softer rectangles and warmed the walls, but it had pulled the room almost whole.

Then the price list resolved.

VERA LUX, the header read, in the Garamond typeface the gallery had used since it opened. Below it, a column of names and prices. Nick's eyes found his own near the bottom.

BRAY, N. — Oakland Hills Series
— Afternoon Light, Grizzly Peak . . . $2,400
— Marine Layer, Bay View . . . $1,800
— Ridgeline, Joaquin Miller . . . $2,100

He knew the rest. These were his prices from the show two years ago — his last show, the one where he'd sold nothing. Not a single piece over the five weeks it was up, not even the small study of the Piedmont hills that he'd priced at eight hundred dollars, which was less than the materials and framing had cost. The gallery had kept the work up for an extra month out of some combination of stubbornness and pity, then moved it to the back room, then called him to come pick it up. He remembered backing the Civic into the alley behind the gallery. He remembered the bubble wrap the owner insisted on even for the short drive because she cared about work even when it didn't sell. He remembered carrying the canvases up three flights and leaning them against the wall of the apartment where they still stood.

The HVAC cycled. Lila typed something. Somewhere behind him Russ laughed once at whatever was in Slack and stopped.

Nick's hand stayed on the mouse. The rating fields sat empty at the bottom of the screen: leakage severity, source resemblance, overall quality. Above them, the little easel on the monitor held the words VERA LUX, and under that his own name, and under that the prices of paintings still leaning against his apartment wall.

Gallery sites never posted one room shot and stopped. They posted the whole show as a packet: installation views, wall crops, straight-on JPEGs of each painting, detail shots, the price PDF. If Canopy's archive had ingested the Vera Lux installation photo deeply enough for the audit tool to recover the source URL and for the model to reproduce the easel text, then the rest of the folder was already in the system. His landscapes were not a paranoid leap from this room. They were the next files in the packet.

He filled in the leakage fields and sent the batch on.

If the gallery was in the pipeline, the paintings were next.


# Chapter 12

On a Tuesday in November, a new batch populated Nick's sidebar at 9:17. The pod-machine coffee at his elbow tasted like hot water that had recently been in the same room as a coffee bean. He'd been evaluating a texture anomaly in a streetscape rendering, trying to decide whether the cobblestone pattern was a model failure or a deliberate stylistic choice, when the queue blinked live beside it. There were forty-two thumbnails. It was a standard calibration batch. Each one was postage-stamp small, waiting to be opened, scored, anchored, and returned to the pipeline.

He clicked the first. It was a still life, a fruit bowl with dramatic side-lighting, clearly AI-generated. The apple skin had that smoothness, the highlights too uniform, like someone had described an apple to a person who'd only ever seen photographs of them. He scored it across the standard fields. Prompt adherence, anatomical accuracy, lighting consistency, compositional balance, stylistic coherence. The floor evaluators had given it a spread: two 3s, a 4, and a 2 from someone who'd objected to the shadow direction. Nick agreed about the shadows but not the severity. He typed his note. He clicked Next.

The second image was a landscape.

He didn't recognize it for three seconds. Maybe four. The thumbnail had been cropped to a square and the monitor's color profile shifted the palette. The warm ochre of the underpainting read as flat tan, and the viridian he'd mixed from phthalo green and cadmium yellow looked like plain green, default-picker green, not the green you spent twenty minutes mixing on a palette while the linseed oil started to tack. But the composition was his. The ridge line breaking the horizon at the left third. The eucalyptus he'd placed there because the actual eucalyptus was there, on the hillside above the Caldecott Tunnel, and he'd stood on the fire trail four different mornings trying to get the way the tree interrupted the slope without competing with the sky.

His hand came off the mouse.

The painting was from the Temescal show. He'd made it in the spring of 2022, working from photographs and plein air studies. The East Bay hills were easy to see and hard to paint because everyone had already painted them and what they expected was a postcard, and the hills were not a postcard. They were a thing that had to do with the way fog altered the light at different hours, turning the same ridge gold or gray or a green that existed nowhere in any tube. He'd layered the ochre ground and built the greens in translucent glazes rather than mixing them flat on the palette, and he'd worked the sky in one afternoon when the conditions matched his reference because the light was the light and you couldn't invent it afterward.

Here it was. Image 11-07-2023-CAL-0894-007. Seven hundred and twelve pixels wide. No title, no artist's name, no gallery. Just a rectangle in the evaluation portal between the AI fruit bowl and what looked like a machine's attempt at a Rothko.

He scrolled the sidebar thumbnails. More AI outputs, an architectural rendering with converging verticals that didn't converge, a portrait with a hand that had six fingers and a thumb. Then another painting he recognized: the Lake Merritt view from the amphitheater, the smaller canvas he'd never been happy with but had included in the show because he'd needed to fill ten linear feet of gallery wall and had only had eight canvases and one of those wasn't ready. And another: the eucalyptus study he'd done as a warmup and overworked, scrubbing at the bark texture until it looked labored. And another, the sunset over the Estuary that he'd painted from his apartment window, the one where the BART elevated track cut across the lower third and he'd left it in because the track was real and pretending it wasn't there would've been a lie. And the pair of small studies he'd done of the container cranes at the Port of Oakland, graphite and wash on toned ground, which Sarah had displayed together under glass and which someone at the opening had said looked like insects and which had stuck with him because the cranes did look like insects, enormous mantises frozen in the act of feeding, and he'd never been able to decide whether he'd painted that or whether the man at the opening had seen it for him.

Seven of his paintings. Seven out of forty-two.

They'd been scraped from the Temescal gallery's website. The only explanation. Sarah kept the show archives online because she kept everything online, thumbnail images with dimensions and prices listed underneath in the slightly too-small sans-serif that Nick had once told her was hard to read and that she'd kept anyway. The prices were still in his memory: the Oakland hills piece at $2,400, which Jess had said was too low and which hadn't mattered, because nobody bought it. Nobody bought any of them. He'd struck the show at the end of the month and carried the canvases to his car in two trips and driven them home and leaned them against the wall of his apartment where they still leaned, fourteen months later, while their digital versions circulated through pipelines to teach machines how to make more pictures.

Nick minimized the evaluation portal and opened the batch distribution dashboard, the calibration-team view that tracked floor coverage and anchor timing. Calibration batches ran floor-first. Six evaluators scored them cold; only after the spread populated did he lock the anchor ratings that became ground truth. Floor notes lived in a collapsible pane under each image. Priya could read them. Any calibration analyst could. Ops couldn't see the note text from their dashboard, only the orange hold and the timer bleeding out, unless somebody on calibration kicked the note into source audit. If Nick anchored past a floor warning without escalating it, the warning stayed in batch history and stopped mattering to the pipeline. If a batch looked contaminated with source material, he could put it on reference check and buy ninety minutes before the automated stall pings started climbing the chain. He clicked the hold and watched his employee ID appear beside it in orange. Then he scanned the assignment column.

Tommy Reyes had image 0894-012. The Lake Merritt canvas.

Bao Nguyen had image 0894-007. The Oakland hills.

He stood. His chair rolled back into the partition with a hollow plastic sound that nobody on the floor noticed, because nobody noticed anything that wasn't a system alert or a Slack notification. The calibration area was a glassed-in section at the end of the floor, "the fishbowl," management called it, separated from the regular evaluation stations by frosted panels. Nick pushed through the door into the main room.

Forty-some evaluators in their soft-walled cubicles, each facing dual monitors, each clicking through images at whatever pace they maintained. The hum of HVAC and the flat continuous murmur of keyboards and mouse clicks and, underneath that, the low-grade rhythm of people shifting in office chairs, reaching for water bottles, clearing throats. The beige carpet absorbed everything. It was a room designed to absorb everything. Nick walked toward the break room, keeping his pace even, the pace of a man going to get coffee because his cup was empty, which it was.

Tommy's station was the third from the end, near the entrance. His monitors were angled toward the aisle, a consequence of the cubicle layout and Tommy's habit of leaning back with his legs stretched under the desk. He was looking at his phone with his left hand and moving the mouse with his right. The image on the left monitor was the Lake Merritt canvas. Nick's Lake Merritt canvas. The amphitheater in the foreground, the late-afternoon light turning the water a color that looked gold in life and looked flat on screen, the joggers on the path that he'd included because he'd wanted the human scale and then regretted because they looked posed.

The rating fields were already filled in. Nick could read them from four feet away: prompt adherence 3, anatomical accuracy N/A, lighting consistency 3, compositional balance 3, stylistic coherence 2. Tommy had spent maybe fifteen seconds on it. Maybe less.

Tommy clicked Next. The painting vanished. A corporate lobby rendering replaced it, generated, with the standard AI problems: a door opening into solid wall, a floor reflection that defied the geometry above it. Tommy set his phone down and leaned forward, because the lobby image had the kind of errors that required actual evaluation. He started typing a note.

"Hey," Tommy said as Nick passed, not looking up. "Priya say anything about the holiday schedule?"

"Didn't check."

"They're supposed to post it Wednesday. I gotta swap my Thursday." He was still typing, still looking at the lobby rendering. His voice had the same tone it had when he talked about anything, lunch, the weather, his commute, the images in his queue. The painting had been work. He'd done it and moved on.

Nick kept walking. In the break room, the pod machine hissed and produced its thin cup. He stood by the doorway and drank and looked down the far aisle toward Bao's station by the window.

Bao was wearing his reading glasses. Both hands were on the desk, neither on the keyboard or mouse. He was looking at the Oakland hills landscape. His head was tilted slightly to the left, the thing he did when he was studying composition rather than scanning for errors, the same tilt Nick had seen when Bao found a generated image that had accidentally produced something worth looking at, some convergence of form and color the model had stumbled into without knowing what it had done. When Bao tilted his head like that, he was seeing something the rubric didn't have a field for.

The rating fields on Bao's screen were empty. Nick watched from the break room doorway for what had to be twenty seconds, maybe longer. Twenty seconds was twenty seconds more than the throughput target allocated for a single image, twenty seconds that would show up on the productivity dashboard as dead time, the same slowness that had gotten Bao flagged three times already. Bao's hand went to the mouse. His other hand reached for the keyboard, and he began typing into the notes field with the same unhurried precision he brought to everything, his evaluations, his conversations, the way he buttoned his shirt collar before leaving for the day. He typed more than most evaluators wrote in a shift.

Nick rinsed his cup in the break room sink. The water was cold and the drain made a sucking sound and the light through the window above the sink was November light, thin, without warmth. He dried his hands on a paper towel that tore when he pulled it from the dispenser. He threw the torn halves away and walked back to his station.

On the calibration dashboard, ratings were populating. Tommy's scores for 0894-012 came first: 3, N/A, 3, 3, 2. The 2 for stylistic coherence. Nick looked at the number on the screen. It was a defensible rating. The Lake Merritt piece had an inconsistency between the foreground treatment and the sky, a difference in handling that a careful evaluator would flag as incoherent, which Tommy, for all his speed, was capable of noticing when his phone was out of reach. Tommy wasn't wrong. The number captured something true about the painting and killed the painting in the same motion. Nick pressed his thumb into the desk edge until the plastic seam dented his skin.

Other evaluators' scores trickled in. Two of his paintings had been assigned to a woman named Grace who'd started three weeks ago and who rated everything between 3 and 4 because she hadn't yet developed the confidence to use the full scale. Another went to a guy named Dev whose ratings were competent and fast and unremarkable. His paintings went by under the same gray progress bar as the fruit bowls and lobby renderings: a few seconds of attention, a row of numbers, a click, the thumbnail dimming as the queue advanced.

Bao's scores came in eleven minutes after Tommy's, which meant Bao had spent eleven minutes on images most evaluators cleared in under three. Image 0894-007: prompt adherence 4, anatomical accuracy N/A, lighting consistency 5, compositional balance 4, stylistic coherence 4. And a note: "Painterly quality in hillside treatment suggests reference image rather than generation. Light handling consistent with single source. Transition from warm foreground to cooler distance reads as intentional. Recommend reclassification if this is reference material." The note sat in the floor-comments pane with the small gray Escalate link beside it, still live.

Bao had clocked it. Not as Nick's, there was no way to know that, but as human work instead of machine output. He'd written the word that stayed: intentional. He'd seen a person in the image. Someone behind the pixels, making decisions.

Nick sat at his station with both hands flat on the desk and the dashboard open in front of him. The fluorescent tubes hummed overhead. Someone's Slack notification chimed twice down the aisle. The HVAC cut on and pushed air that smelled like the inside of the duct, metallic and stale. November light through the parking-lot windows was the color of nothing, gray that wasn't a value study, gray that was just absence.

He had made those paintings in a room that smelled like turpentine. With brushes that cost eleven dollars each. He had stood in front of canvases and made choices, this green and not that green, this stroke and not that one, and the paintings had traveled from his studio to the gallery wall to the gallery website to a scraper to the pipeline to the evaluation portal. At each step they'd shed something: the frame, the texture of the paint surface, the signature, the title, the price, his name. On the dashboard they sat in a clean column of filenames beside the anchor timer, each one waiting for a number.

Someone said something to him on the way to lunch. Priya, maybe. Or one of the other calibration analysts. Nick answered. He didn't remember what he said or what was said to him.

He ate a sandwich at his desk from the vending machine in the hallway, the kind with the bread that was soft in a way that wasn't related to freshness. Through the fishbowl glass he could see the floor, people getting up to stretch, checking phones, filing toward the break room. At 12:30 he went to refill his coffee and heard Grace talking to Dev at the microwave, waiting for something to heat.

"Did you get the batch with the real paintings in it?"

"Which ones?"

"Like, oil paintings. Landscapes, I think. It was kind of a nice break, actually. You can tell they're different from the generated stuff."

"I didn't look that hard," Dev said. "I just rated them."

"Well, yeah. But they were pretty. The one with the hills."

Nick poured his coffee. The machine gurgled. Grace was twenty-four and had been on the floor three weeks and she was right. The hills painting was pretty, and that was a word Nick would have bristled at once because pretty was what people said about work that was competent but didn't do anything to you, and he'd spent his whole practice trying to make work that did something to you, and now pretty was probably the most accurate thing anyone had said about his painting all day. Pretty. Three out of five. Next.

His Slack chimed on the walk back from the break room. Priya: "0894 still on reference check?"

Nick stopped by the fishbowl door and typed with his coffee in one hand. "Mixed batch. Wanted the floor spread first."

He did not mention Bao's note. The minute he mentioned it, Priya could open the image comments, see the reclassification recommendation, and tell him to route 0894 to source audit. Then the hold would stop being a delay and become an exception she had to answer for. Ops would freeze the batch until somebody decided whether the paintings were contaminated training data or reference material that never should've been there in the first place. His employee ID was already on the hold. If he anchored the batch instead of escalating, it would be on the release too.

The typing bubble appeared, disappeared, then came back. "Okay. Need your anchors in before 2. Ops already asked."

He sent a thumbs-up and went back to his station.

***

The calibration queue wouldn't wait forever. Reference-check holds bought ninety minutes and a little managerial grace, not a whole afternoon. At 1:07 the batch timer on Nick's dashboard turned orange. At 1:14 Slack chimed again.

Priya: "You good on 0894?"

"Yeah. Starting now," he wrote back.

He let six more minutes pass anyway. At 1:20 he opened the calibration interface and clicked the first image in the anchor queue.

Image 0894-003 loaded. It was one of the fruit bowls, AI-generated, routine, nothing. He scored it. Prompt adherence 3, lighting consistency 3, compositional balance 2, stylistic coherence 3. He clicked Next. Another AI output loaded. He scored it. He clicked Next.

Image 0894-007 filled the left monitor. He'd been waiting for it without admitting he was waiting for it, clicking through the preceding images on something like autopilot, scoring them adequately, not well, just enough to pass the QA threshold.

The Oakland hills landscape filled the monitor at full resolution, larger than the thumbnail, closer to the original but not the original, never the original. The ochre ground was visible now where it showed through the thinner glazes on the left side. The viridian had some of its depth back, though the cadmium warmth was still flattened by the monitor's backlight into something duller and cooler than what he'd mixed. The eucalyptus at the horizon break was doing what he'd wanted it to do: creating a pause between the slope and the sky, a moment where the eye had to rest before continuing upward.

It was a good painting. It wasn't his best. The brushwork in the foreground was cautious in a way that reflected the three weeks he'd spent on the underpainting before he trusted himself to commit to the surface. It wasn't his worst either. It was a painting by a person who could see and who'd put in the work that seeing required, who'd stood on a hillside enough mornings that the postcard version had dissolved and something harder to name had taken its place.

The rating form waited on the right monitor. Five categories, five scales, a notes field. One tab over, Bao's comment still sat under the image with Escalate live. If Nick clicked it, 0894 would leave his queue and go to source audit with Priya copied on the exception. If he locked anchors instead, Bao's recommendation would sit under RESOLVED - ANCHORED in the batch history, readable later and useless now. His cursor sat in the first box.

A 5 would flag. The calibration system's anomaly detection was tuned to catch outliers, ratings that deviated more than one standard deviation from the evaluator consensus. The floor had given the painting a range in the low-3 to mid-4 territory, with Bao's lighting score of 5 as the high mark. A calibration anchor of 5 across the board would trigger a review, and the review would lead to a conversation, and the conversation would lead to someone pulling the image and looking at it and asking why the calibration analyst had graded a landscape as flawless when the floor consensus was mixed.

He couldn't give it a 5.

A 3 wasn't right either. Not because a 3 was indefensible, you could argue 3 for compositional balance if you squinted at the foreground treatment, but because he knew with the specific certainty of the person who'd made the painting that a 3 was wrong for the things that mattered. The lighting was right. He'd driven to the fire trail four times to watch how the fog changed the color temperature of the hillside across different hours, and he'd matched the light on the canvas with a care that was invisible to anyone who hadn't been there and legible to anyone who had. Bao had given the lighting a 5. Bao was right. But Bao could give it a 5 because Bao was a floor evaluator and a single outlier score from a floor evaluator didn't trigger anything. A 5 from a calibration anchor weighed differently.

Nick typed 4 into the prompt-adherence field. The painting wasn't responding to a prompt. It wasn't a generation, it was a painting. But prompt adherence was the first field and the system required a number in every field, and 4 was the number he was going to type in almost every field because 4 was what the painting was. Four out of five. He typed 4 for compositional balance. Four for stylistic coherence, which was generous if you were strict about the foreground and fair if you weren't. Five for lighting consistency, because Bao had given it a 5 and Nick was not going to pretend the lighting was anything less than what it was, and a single 5 in one subcategory wouldn't flag when the overall score was lower. Four for the overall anchor.

He sat with the number.

Four. His painting. The hills above the Caldecott Tunnel as he'd seen them and made them and signed and framed and hung on a wall in a gallery where Sarah had poured cheap wine into plastic cups at the opening and seven people had come. He'd given it a four. He looked once more at the thumbnail preview, at the foreground tightening up where he'd lost his nerve, and left it there.

The cursor sat on the Next button. He moved the mouse and clicked it and the painting disappeared and a portrait with a fused hand replaced it and he scored the portrait, anatomical accuracy 1, the rest in the 2-to-3 range, and clicked Next.

The eucalyptus study came up six images later. He gave it a 3 because it deserved a 3. He'd overworked it. The bark texture looked labored and the composition was a warmup exercise that should've stayed a warmup exercise. Three. Next.

The Estuary sunset came up next. He looked at the BART track bisecting the lower third and remembered the afternoon he'd painted it, February, gray sky, the track a dark line against the water, and he'd left the window open because the turpentine smell was giving him a headache and the cold had made his hands slow, which had actually been good for the painting because the brushwork was looser than he usually allowed. The floor evaluators had given it a spread of 3s and 4s. He gave it a 4. It was one of the better things he'd done that year, not that the year had been a high point. Four. Next.

The Lake Merritt canvas came up four images after that. He looked at it at full resolution, the amphitheater, the water, the joggers he'd always known were a mistake, and he thought about Tommy's 2 for stylistic coherence and decided Tommy was right, the coherence problem was structural, and he gave it a 3 overall. Three. Next.

By 2:40 the seven scores sat in the system: 4, 3, 3, 4, 3, 3, 4. He opened the hold menu, changed 0894 from REFERENCE CHECK to RESOLVED - ANCHORED, and watched Bao's reclassification note collapse into the batch history drawer under the timestamp. It hadn't disappeared. Priya could still open it if she went looking. Marcus could too, if the batch ever got audited. His employee ID now sat on the hold, the anchors, and the release of seven human paintings back into the training stream. He sent Priya a one-line Slack that said "Anchors in," and finished the rest of the batch in the standard way, flagging errors and noting anomalies with the same attention he brought to everything in the queue, because the attention was what the job required and the attention was what he had.

He logged out at 4:15, fifteen minutes before his shift ended. He closed the dashboard. He rinsed his coffee cup in the break room sink and set it in the dish rack.

The parking lot was already dark. November pulled the light out early and the business park's sodium-vapor lamps turned everything the same flat amber, the cars, the strip of landscaping, the bus stop bench, Nick's Civic in the third row with its oxidizing paint. He sat in the driver's seat and put the key in the ignition and didn't turn it. The windshield was fogged at the edges. Through the clear center he could see the building he'd just left, the second-floor windows still lit, the night shift logging on.

He turned the key. The engine caught on the second try, a hesitation in the ignition that had been getting worse since September. He pulled out of the lot and onto Davis and merged onto 880 north toward Oakland. The fog was coming in off the Bay, low and flat, and the headlights of the oncoming traffic were soft in it, directionless. The hills to the east were somewhere behind the gray. He couldn't see them.

At home, he dropped his keys on the counter and stood in the dark for a minute before turning on the light. The apartment smelled the way it had smelled for months, less like turpentine, more like old carpet and the Chinese takeout container he should have thrown away yesterday. The canvases were against the wall where they'd always been. The Oakland hills landscape was there, the real one, oil on canvas, thirty inches by forty inches, the paint surface thick with the texture that the screen couldn't transmit. He pulled it away from the wall and set it on the kitchen table under the overhead light.

Up close, the foreground still hesitated. Tommy had been right about that. The sky held. The eucalyptus still did its job. The painting had not improved or worsened in the drive home. It was the same painting, and now he couldn't look at it without seeing the fields beside it.

He went to the hall closet and brought out the milk crate with his paint box. The brushes in the jar had gone stiff at the ferrules. He unscrewed the tube of titanium white and found a chalky plug hard in the cap. He squeezed harder anyway. The tube split at the seam and pushed a dry rope the color of bathroom plaster onto his thumb. Under the sink the mineral spirits had gone thick and yellow in the bottle. He unscrewed that too. The smell had turned sweet and rotten. He dipped one of the stiff brushes into it and the bristles stayed splayed, glued apart.

He washed his thumb in the kitchen sink and dried it on the dish towel. Then he opened his banking app on the counter.

Checking: $318.44.

Rent in eleven days. PG&E on Monday. The Civic already needed whatever was making the ignition catch on the second try.

The cheap 24-by-36 canvases at the art store on Telegraph were $22.99 each because he'd looked in August and then closed the tab like a person making a responsible decision. Titanium white was $11.49. A pint of odorless mineral spirits was $14.95. Tax on top of that. Enough money to be stupid. Not enough money to be impossible.

He opened savings. The little bucket he'd renamed CAR in September, when the engine started hesitating, had $141.08 in it. He transferred eighty dollars into checking and watched the wheel turn until the confirmation banner slid across the screen.

On the back of the takeout receipt he wrote: 2 canvases. White. Spirits. Telegraph. Tomorrow. He slid the receipt under his keys, beside the split tube and the yellow bottle.

He heated the leftover takeout in the microwave and ate it standing at the counter. When he went to bed, the painting was still on the table, and by the door the note for Telegraph stayed under his keys, waiting for morning.


# Chapter 13

Nick stood at the counter with his coffee going cold. The light through his kitchen window was good. It was a December morning in Oakland. The rain had paused between systems, and the sun found a seam in the cloud deck and pushed through at a low angle that caught the top of the BART elevated track and the roofline of the auto body shop across the street and turned everything it touched briefly amber. His brain produced the assessment before he could stop it: atmospheric coherence, mid-range. It was consistent with a single dominant light source. The color temperature was warm but within plausible bounds for late-afternoon winter — except this was morning, and the angle was wrong for afternoon, which meant the assessment was wrong, which meant he was not only evaluating the actual sky outside his actual window but evaluating it badly.

He'd bought canvas: two stretched 24-by-36s from the art supply store on Telegraph, the cheap ones, pre-primed cotton that he would have bitched about three years ago but that now felt like an extravagance. They'd cost forty-six dollars for both. He'd also grabbed a tube of titanium white because his old one had dried to chalk in the cap, and a pint of odorless mineral spirits because the turpentine under the sink had gone thick and yellow. The rest of his paints were still usable, mostly — the cadmiums, the ultramarine, the raw umber, the alizarin crimson — sealed tight enough that the pigment hadn't separated beyond saving. He arranged them on the table like a sacrament. He hadn't set up to paint since August, maybe July. It had been four months. The canvases stacked against the far wall were the old work, the unsold landscapes from the Temescal show. He wasn't touching those. He set one of the new canvases on the easel and squared it.

The apartment smelled wrong. It used to smell like linseed oil and turpentine and the particular dusty warmth of dried canvas, and under that, coffee and the faint mildew from the bathroom ventilation he'd never fixed. Now it smelled like nothing: carpet and laundry detergent and the coffee itself, which had gone to room temperature in his hand. The painting smell was an absence. He cracked the mineral spirits and the chemical sweetness hit him and something loosened in his chest that he hadn't known was tight.

He stood in front of the white rectangle for a long time.

The easel was the same one he'd used since grad school, a beat-up wooden H-frame with paint drips layered on the tray like geological strata. The palette was glass — a piece of tempered from a frame shop's scrap bin, the edges sanded smooth. His brushes were in a coffee can by the window. He selected a flat half-inch, a filbert, and a small round, and set them out. He squeezed raw umber, ultramarine, and white onto the palette. It was a limited palette, the same one he'd started a hundred paintings with when he wanted to rough in values before committing to color.

He looked at the canvas. He looked out the window. The amber light was gone now, replaced by the flat gray overcast that would hold for the rest of the day. He could paint that view: the roofline, the BART track, the slick wet street below. He knew this view. He'd painted it twice before and both times it had worked — the compressed urban geometry, the elevated track cutting across the frame like a horizon line, the way Oakland's light in winter made everything look like a photograph taken through dirty glass.

He loaded the brush and put it against the canvas and made a mark.

The mark was correct. He could see that immediately. The value was right — a mid-dark umber for the roofline against the lighter gray of the sky. The angle was right. The brush pressure was controlled and the edge was clean where he wanted clean and lost where he wanted lost. He made another mark. It was also correct. He began roughing in the composition: the horizontal of the BART track at the upper third, the roofline below, the street descending to the bottom edge. It came easily. His hand remembered. The paint moved the way it was supposed to.

After forty minutes he stepped back and looked at what he'd made.

It was accurate. The values were calibrated — he caught himself on the word and couldn't find a different one — to the actual light relationships outside the window. The composition was balanced. The proportions were sound. If he'd opened this image in his evaluation queue at work, he would have rated the anatomical accuracy N/A, the lighting consistency a 5, the compositional balance a 4, the stylistic coherence a—

He put the brush down.

The painting looked like a test image. It looked like something a model would produce if you prompted it with *winter Oakland rooftop, impressionist, muted palette, urban landscape* and the model happened to nail the lighting on the first pass. There was nothing wrong with it. There was nothing in it. The brushwork was competent and the color was controlled and the composition held, and Nick could not identify a single mark that came from seeing rather than knowing. Every stroke was a decision he could justify. If he'd had to score it, prompt adherence would have been high. The prompt was his own memory of what this view looked like and how he used to paint it. He'd followed the prompt perfectly. And the result was a picture of a painting rather than a painting.

He picked up the palette knife. The steel edge was still sharp — he could feel the cold of it even through the callus on his thumb. He held it against the canvas, angled flat, and scraped. The paint came off in thick ribbons, umber and white mixing into a warm gray that curled off the blade and fell in strips onto the newspaper he'd laid under the easel. He scraped from the top down, one pass at a time, until the canvas was mostly bare again. A few ghosts of the image remained, umber ground into the cotton weave where the paint had been thickest.

He cleaned the brushes at the sink. He capped the paints. He folded the newspaper with its gray ribbons and dropped it in the trash can. Then he sat on the edge of his bed and looked at the stained canvas on the easel and the coffee can of brushes and the tubes of paint still lined up on the table, and he didn't do anything for a while.

---

The recital was at six-thirty on a Tuesday. Dani had texted him the week before: *Luna's holiday thing at school, she asked if you'd come. You don't have to. Gym, 6:30, folding chairs, you've been warned.* He'd said yes without thinking about it, which was unusual for him lately. He said yes to most things without thinking and no to the things that required thought, and this was a pattern he could see clearly and couldn't seem to change.

He drove from Fruitvale to the school in his Civic, the heater blowing air that was warm but not hot, the windshield wipers on intermittent. December in Oakland meant rain coming in waves — a week of downpour, a few gray dry days, then more rain. Tonight was dry but the streets were still wet, and the headlights of oncoming traffic broke apart on the asphalt into long yellow smears that he would have once wanted to paint.

The school was a low stucco building set back from the street behind a chain-link fence and a parking lot already full. He found a spot two blocks away and walked. A custodian had propped the gym doors open and the light spilled out into the parking lot in a warm yellow rectangle. Inside, the gym was set up with rows of metal folding chairs facing a low portable stage at the far end. The chairs were sized for adults but only barely — the seat was a hand-span too narrow, the back support ended at the middle of his spine. Parents and grandparents and a few older siblings filled most of the rows. The air was already hot, a hundred bodies plus the overhead heaters in a low-ceilinged room with no working ventilation. Someone had strung construction-paper snowflakes from the basketball hoops. The backboard on the near end still had a scoreboard from last week's game: HOME 28, GUEST 34.

Dani had saved him a seat in the fourth row. She was wearing a sweater he hadn't seen before, dark green, a little too big, the sleeves pushed up to her elbows. Her hair was down, which he only ever saw outside of work. She had Luna's tote bag on the chair next to her, stuffed with a Ziploc of goldfish crackers and a sketchbook with its spiral binding bent out of shape.

"Hey." She moved the tote bag and he sat down. The metal chair was cold through his jeans.

"This is a lot of people," he said.

"Second grade is serious business." She was checking her phone — a text from someone, her face briefly tight — then she put it away and smiled at him. "She's been practicing for two weeks. I've heard the song four hundred times. I can't promise it'll be good."

"I'm not here for good."

"Low bar. Smart."

The gym smelled like floor wax and sweat and the particular sweetness of a room full of children who had recently eaten cafeteria food. An older woman two seats over was fanning herself with the photocopied program. The program listed the second-grade class performing "Frosty the Snowman" (Traditional, Arr. Ms. Campos). Below that, the third-graders were doing "Jingle Bell Rock."

A teacher — Ms. Campos, presumably — walked the line of kids from a side door to the stage. They were small and overdressed, most of them in clothes that looked new or at least recently ironed, and they climbed the three steps with the exaggerated care of people who know they're being watched. Luna was in the middle of the line. She was wearing a red dress with black tights and her dark hair had been pulled back with a clip that was already coming loose on one side. She scanned the audience with the frank, searching look of a kid who needed to locate her person, and when she found Dani she waved with her whole arm, the kind of wave that has no self-consciousness in it at all. Dani waved back. Nick raised his hand. Luna's eyes went wide — surprised, pleased — and she waved at him too, harder, until the kid behind her nudged her to face front.

Ms. Campos counted them off and they started singing.

It was not good. It was the specific not-good of twenty-two seven-year-olds singing a song they'd memorized but not learned, the rhythm approximate, the pitch wandering, several of them a full beat behind and one enthusiastic boy in a clip-on tie a full beat ahead. Luna sang with total commitment and variable accuracy. She knew all the words but she didn't always know where they went. During the chorus, the kid on the end started crying for no visible reason and Ms. Campos edged over to put a hand on his shoulder without breaking the conducting gesture, which Nick found quietly impressive.

The performance lasted three minutes. The audience clapped as though they'd just witnessed something extraordinary, and in the gym's overheated air, with the construction-paper snowflakes turning slowly on their strings, something about the gap between the execution and the enthusiasm pierced a place in Nick that he hadn't been able to reach with a brush and umber and forty minutes of trying. These kids didn't know the song was bad. They didn't know the pitch was wrong. They weren't evaluating themselves against any standard. They were just doing the thing.

After the third-graders performed their "Jingle Bell Rock," which was marginally more on-key but compensated by being aggressively choreographed, the gym descended into the polite chaos of dismissal. Parents stood, kids ran, and folding chairs scraped on the varnished floor. Luna materialized in front of Dani at full speed and attached herself to Dani's waist.

"Did you see me?"

"I saw you, Lu. You were great."

"I messed up the part about the broomstick."

"Nobody noticed."

"I noticed." She said it matter-of-factly, not as complaint but as record. Then she turned to Nick. "Hi."

"Hey, Luna. That was really good."

She assessed the credibility of this. "Do you want to see my horse?"

"Yeah."

She extracted a folded piece of paper from somewhere in the vicinity of her dress — he hadn't seen a pocket — and unfolded it with care. The drawing was in crayon on standard-issue school paper, the kind with the pale blue lines. The horse was orange. It wasn't a real orange that any horse had ever been — a bright, pure orange, the color of a traffic cone. It had five legs, or possibly four legs and a tail that was also a leg. The head was enormous relative to the body, the eye was a circle the size of a quarter, and the mane consisted of purple spikes that radiated outward like a crown. The ground was a green stripe at the bottom. The sky was a blue stripe at the top. The middle zone — the zone where the horse lived — was blank white paper.

The proportions were wrong in every direction. The anatomy was invented. There was no light source, no shadow, no perspective, no compositional logic Nick could name.

He stared at it.

The horse was running. He could tell because the legs were splayed at angles that suggested maximum velocity, and there were speed lines — actual speed lines, in red — coming off the tail. Luna had pressed hard enough with the orange crayon that the paper was slightly embossed, the wax thick and ridged under his thumb when he held the edge. The eye was not realistic. It was a circle. But the circle had been drawn with such conviction that the thing it communicated was not *this is what an eye looks like* but *this horse is looking at you*.

The back of his neck went hot. He shifted the paper in his hand because he'd started holding it too carefully, like it might tear.

"That's a good horse," he said, and his voice came out rough. He cleared his throat. "What's its name?"

"Fire," Luna said. "Because it's orange. Like fire."

"Yeah. That tracks."

"I'm going to draw more. I'm going to draw a whole herd."

Dani was watching him. She wasn't watching Luna; she was watching him look at Luna's drawing. Her expression was hard to read. It wasn't suspicious or amused. It was something closer to recognition, like she could see what the drawing was doing to him and knew what it meant and wasn't going to say so.

"Come on, Lu, get your coat." Dani took her daughter's hand and steered her toward the pile of jackets in the corner, and Nick stood there holding the photocopied program with its song lyrics and its typo in the principal's welcome note and thought about the blank canvas at home with the umber stains he couldn't fully scrape.

---

Dani's apartment was a fifteen-minute drive from the school, a two-bedroom in a Temescal fourplex with a bay window and a front door that stuck in the rain.
