The fridge was covered in Luna's drawings — dozens of them, held up by mismatched magnets from places Dani had never been, souvenir magnets that must have come from a thrift store or a grab bag: San Diego, Mount Rushmore, a lobster that said MAINE. The drawings overlapped so that only the most recent were fully visible, and the ones underneath showed bent corners and paper edges pinned under the magnets. Most of them were horses, with some people, a purple house, and a dog with wings mixed in.

Luna took twenty minutes to get ready for bed, most of which Nick could hear from the living room — the negotiation over teeth-brushing, the argument about which pajamas, the request for water, the second request for different water from a different cup. Dani's voice through the wall was patient and firm and completely without performance, the voice of a person who had done this a thousand times and would do it a thousand more and wasn't trying to be good at it because being good at it wasn't the point. The point was the kid was in bed and the teeth were brushed and the water was acceptable.

Nick sat on the couch and looked at the room. The apartment was not decorated so much as accumulated — a bookshelf heavy on paperbacks and children's books, a small TV on a stand that doubled as a storage bench, a rug with a stain in the corner that had been mostly hidden by a potted plant. On the wall above the couch was a framed photograph of a mural — bright colors, a woman's face rendered in bold strokes, geometric patterns flowing outward from the cheekbones — that he recognized as Dani's work. He'd seen photos of her murals before, on her phone, scrolled past quickly the one time she'd shown him. This one was large-scale, clearly the side of a building, and the photograph captured enough of the surrounding wall to show where the mural ended and the cinder block began. The transition was stark, a hard drop from color to nothing.

Dani came out of Luna's room and closed the door three-quarters of the way. "She wants you to know she's drawing Fire's herd."

"Now?"

"She's got a flashlight and a sketchbook under the covers. She thinks I don't know." Dani went to the kitchen. He heard the fridge open, the clink of bottles. She came back with two beers — Modelo, the tall cans — and handed him one. She sat on the other end of the couch, legs folded under her, and took a long drink. "I give it ten minutes."

"Thanks for inviting me tonight."

"Yeah, of course."

"She was good. I mean — she wasn't good. But she was great."

Dani smiled. "She talks about you, you know. Nick from Mommy's work."

"Nick from Mommy's work. That's pretty much my whole identity at this point."

She didn't respond to that. She picked at the label on her beer. Under her nails, in the cuticles, he could see the faint residue of paint — not fresh, not from today, but stubborn enough that soap and water didn't get it all. He didn't know when she'd last painted anything that wasn't with Luna. He hadn't asked.

"The horse was really something," he said.

"The horse has five legs."

"Still beat me." He took a drink. "I bought canvas Saturday. Scraped it before it even had time to dry."

She looked at him. "You're painting again?"

"Tried."

"Luna asked if you paint too."

He looked over. "What'd you tell her."

"That you do." Dani kept working at the label with her thumbnail. "She sees your hands. She's not stupid."

He looked at his thumb, the callus built up at the side of it, the permanent dark in the cuticle where paint had gone too deep too many times.

"That was generous."

"It wasn't generous." She tore off a strip of paper and set it on the coffee table. "It was a normal answer to a seven-year-old question."

"You done?" she said.

He handed her the can. She took both empties into the kitchen, and he followed because staying on the couch felt too much like being left there.

The kitchen was one narrow run of counter and sink open to the living room. Fire had multiplied across the fridge door under the thrift-store magnets, orange horses crowding each other with cloudy crayon wax rubbed from one page onto the next.

Dani rinsed the cans and crushed one against the lip of the sink. "You know what I miss? That clinic wall off International. August, full sun by ten. The lift kept jerking every time a truck went by and I had a four-inch roller in one hand and a sash brush shoved in my back pocket."

"That sounds terrible."

"It was terrible." Her voice stayed flat, almost dry. "My shoulder was shot. The blue kept drying darker than the sample because the stucco drank it. I spent half a day painting one cheekbone the size of a mattress and I still couldn't tell if the face was working till I climbed down and stood across the street."

Water ran over the aluminum in her hands. "I miss that part."

"The not knowing."

She shut off the faucet. "That cheekbone took me three weeks. I kept thinking the proportions were off but I couldn't tell from the lift — you can't see a face from six feet away." She dried her hands on the dish towel. "Then I climbed down and crossed the street and it was just there. The whole face."

He looked at the horses on the fridge, orange legs jammed up under the magnets.

"Call me next time," she said. "Before you scrape."

He looked over. "I don't know what you'd—" He shook his head. "There was nothing to see."

"Maybe." She turned, one hip against the sink. "But you didn't show anyone."

In Luna's room, the flashlight went dark — visible through the gap in the door, the thin line of light on the hallway floor winking out.

"Nick," Dani said.

He looked over. She was close enough now that the dish towel hanging from her shoulder brushed his wrist when she turned. There was dish soap on her skin under the beer smell. The paint grit in her thumbprint caught at the side of his neck when she put her hand there.

He kissed her, or she kissed him, and then it stopped mattering which one had started it. Her other hand caught a fistful of his T-shirt and pulled him in until the counter edge pressed into the backs of his thighs.

He put his hands on her waist. Her sweater bunched under his palms and rode up enough that his fingertips found warm skin at her side. She opened her mouth against his and leaned harder into him, pinning him between her body and the sink, and his whole chest went hot at once.

The dish rack rattled when his elbow hit it. Somewhere behind them the fridge motor kicked on and kept humming. He wanted her sweater off. He wanted the kitchen light gone and her against the counter and enough time for the rest of him to catch up with what his hands were already doing.

The wanting was still there when the notebook hit him. It had a black cover and a bent corner and sat tucked in the drawer beside his bed, where he'd written every corrupted anchor in his own hand: batch numbers, anchor IDs, what he'd done to each image. If she asked one real question, he'd have to lie from six inches away or tell her what kind of man she'd just let her hands on.

He broke the kiss.

"I can't," he said.

Dani stayed where she was for a second. Then she let go of his shirt and reached for the can nearest her hand.

"Okay."

"Dani—"

She looked at the sink, not at him. "It's late. Luna's got school."

He stood there another beat, breath still wrong, then nodded. She walked him to the door. In the hallway he stepped around Luna's rain boots and a backpack tipped open under the coat hooks, worksheets half out of a folder.

"Thanks for coming tonight," she said at the door.

"Yeah." His mouth still tasted like beer and her. "Tell Luna I said the horse was really good."

"I will."

She closed the door.

He sat in the Civic with the engine off until the windshield filmed over. The kitchen light stayed on behind Dani's curtains, a square of yellow going soft in the condensation.

His phone buzzed on the passenger seat with a Slack notification from work.

He didn't touch it. He could still feel the ridges Luna had pressed into the orange crayon under his thumb.

He shut his eyes and the evaluation portal came up whole and familiar: image window, rating fields, the Next button waiting at the bottom right.

The paintings didn't come anymore. What came was the portal.


# Chapter 14

The Slack notification from Elena Park was still open on Nick's phone in the San Leandro lot. He'd read it three times already, and it still looked wrong.

*Calibration team — Conf Room B, 8:15 AM. Attendance required.*

Elena Park, Marcus Webb's assistant, never messaged the calibration team directly. The rain had been going since Sunday night. Water sheeted down the windshield and turned the building into a gray blur. When she sent something it came through Marcus's channel with agenda items and prep links and suggested reading, the organized energy of a person who believed that preparation was a form of respect. This was none of that. A room, a time, and the word *required*, which in corporate Slack carried the same weight it carried anywhere else.

His phone buzzed. Tommy in the team chat: *anyone know what's going on?* Then Dani: *No idea.* Then nothing. The chat sat there with its two messages like a room where someone had asked a question and everyone was waiting for the person who knew to speak.

Nick turned off the car. The parking lot was the same lot it had been every morning for almost a year — the same landscaping strip dying between two curbs, the same dumpster with its crooked lid, the same flat-roofed building that could have been a dental office or a tax-preparation franchise or exactly what it was, a place where people sat in rows and rated images for a company that rated them back. He noticed the sky's tonal value — flat, even, no directional shadow, the kind of overcast that washed out every surface to the same gray — and it registered automatically, involuntarily. The gray had the same inevitability as tinnitus: always there, never chosen.

Inside, the floor was wrong. Not fire-alarm wrong. Not sobbing-coworker wrong. The HVAC hummed. The fluorescents threw their flat wash across the beige carpet. But the morning rhythm was absent — the click-settle-click of people opening batches, the low murmur of evaluators comparing notes, the soft run of mouse taps and space bars that usually took over once twenty-five people sank into their screens. People were at their desks, but they were looking at each other. Some were on Slack. A few had their phones out, which Priya normally discouraged during work hours, and Priya was not at her station discouraging anything.

She was by the coffee station. Her hair was pushed back with a clip she only used when she'd been here early, and her Costco antacid bottle was sitting on the counter between the sugar packets and the stir sticks, out in the open, which meant she'd already been to the conference room and the conference room had been bad enough that she'd forgotten to put it back in her desk drawer.

"Did you get the message?"

"Yeah. What's going on?"

"I don't know exactly." She picked up the antacid bottle, looked at it like she wasn't sure whose it was, and slid it into her cardigan pocket. "I called Devra in QA. She said they ran some kind of statistical review over the weekend and something flagged. She wouldn't give me details."

"Something in calibration?"

Priya's shrug was not a casual shrug. Her shoulders went up and held a beat too long, and when they came down they were higher than they'd started. "Calibration data. That's all she said."

Nick poured coffee. His hand was steady. The rest of him felt like a pitch just below audible — a low hum that started in his sternum and spread to his wrists and his jaw and the soles of his feet. He watched the coffee fill the mug, watched the surface shiver from the pour, and thought about the distribution curves that would show his work if someone knew where to look. The rightward drift in anatomical-accuracy ratings. The clustering in the lighting-consistency band. Four months of corruptions, each one individually defensible, each one calibrated to sit inside the range of reasonable expert disagreement, and all of them together forming a pattern that was as legible to him as a brushstroke signature — if anyone thought to read it that way.

Nobody would read it that way. The system caught noise. The system caught laziness. The system did not catch a senior calibrator applying his expertise in reverse because the system was built on the assumption that expertise pointed in one direction.

He took his coffee to Conference Room B.

The meeting was small. Six from the calibration team, plus Devra Singh from QA, plus a man Nick didn't recognize wearing a blue polo and a Canopy badge clipped to his belt instead of on a lanyard. Belt clip meant corporate. It was one of those signifiers that nobody acknowledged and everyone understood, like the difference between a name tag and a title card.

Priya sat against the wall rather than at the table. Nick sat next to her. Across the table, Jun-seo from the calibration team was scrolling through his phone under the table with the focused expression of a person looking for information that would make this meeting less frightening.

Devra started. She was compact, mid-forties, and she wore her QA role like religious conviction — not loudly, but as the organizing principle of everything she did. She turned her laptop so the room could see.

"Friday evening, the automated review flagged statistical anomalies in calibration data from last quarter. Specifically, we're seeing distribution shifts in anchor ratings across three categories." She pointed at the screen. "Anatomical accuracy, lighting consistency, and compositional balance."

Nick looked at the graphs. Simple distribution curves with highlighted drift regions. He could see his work in them — not a name, not a trail, but the shape of what he'd done, something he could read from across the room as easily as he could read brush direction in dried impasto from across a gallery. A slight rightward shift in anatomical accuracy, meaning the pipeline was increasingly accepting wrists bent wrong, ears placed too high, shoulders socketed where shoulders did not socket. A compression in lighting-consistency scatter that reflected the biases he'd introduced between October and December. Four months of work, up on a screen, and the only person in the room who could read it was him.

"The shifts are subtle," Devra said, "but they're consistent across the quarter and they don't match the variance we'd normally expect from the calibration team or the broader evaluator population." She clicked to the next slide. The room changed with it.

Bars now, broken out by site and queue family. One of them sat under SAN LEANDRO LOCAL ANCHORS and glowed the wrong color: not red, not yet, but yellow enough that Jun-seo put his phone away. Priya straightened against the wall. Nick felt the blood in his face do something small and ugly.

"The initial pass doesn't tell us intent," Devra said. "It tells us where the variance clusters. Right now the strongest local signal is in portrait and figure-study anchors reviewed out of San Leandro. Overflow batches from Partner Labs are the other concentration point. Nick, Jun-seo, you two handled most of anatomy and lighting in that window. Did anything operational change in October or November?"

Queue mix, Nick thought. Say queue mix. Keep it boring.

"Queue mix," he said, too fast, and made himself slow down. "We got flooded with close-cropped portraits and hand batches. Bad wrists, extra fingers, key lights that changed direction halfway down a face. If overflow raters were pushing volume on that stuff, we'd be correcting the same failures on the back end over and over. That would skew anatomy and lighting before it told you anything useful about the anchors."

"And compositional balance?" Devra asked.

"Tight crops. Product-prompt people. Fake studio symmetry." He lifted a shoulder. "That category clumps when half the frame's already been decided for you."

He had said too much. He heard it while he was still speaking: the extra sentence, the clarifying clause, the exact shape his lies took when they needed support. Priya looked at him. Not suspicion, not yet. Surprise, mostly, that he'd volunteered at all.

Devra typed without looking up. "We normalized for category, not prompt-family density."

The man in the blue polo leaned forward. "We've been seeing parallel patterns from Partner Labs. Manila specifically."

It was not relief, exactly. More like the beam moved off him and hit somebody else.

Nick drank his coffee. It was too hot and it burned the roof of his mouth and the pain was a small, good thing because it gave him something to focus on while the conversation moved six thousand miles away from him.

The man's name was Glen Horstmann, and he worked in Operations, which was not a department Nick had ever needed to think about. Glen from Operations explained that the Manila labeling center — which handled overflow calibration batches and large-volume evaluation contracts — had been flagged independently by a separate monitoring system. Evaluators there were submitting ratings at speeds that were, in Glen's corporate vocabulary, "inconsistent with thorough evaluation." Average assessment time: twenty-two seconds per image. Standard was two to three minutes. Completion bonuses, paid when evaluators exceeded a per-shift volume threshold, were triggering at ninety-four percent, against a historical average of sixty-one.

"We think the calibration-data drift is downstream from the Manila throughput issue," Glen said. "Their rapid-fire ratings are pulling the anchor distributions. We're still running the full regression, but preliminary correlation is strong."

Nick nodded when other people nodded. He looked at the screen when other people looked at the screen. He asked no questions, which was normal — he was known as the quiet one on the calibration team, the evaluator who did his work on-screen and said little in rooms. His silence was a feature of his reputation. It had never been as useful as it was now.

The meeting went forty minutes. The action item was a full audit, led by Marcus Webb, who was coming from San Francisco on Wednesday.

Nick walked back to his desk. The floor had loosened slightly — the fear was still there, but it had a shape now, and a shape meant an explanation, and an explanation that pointed at Manila meant it didn't point here. People were murmuring. Someone in the back row asked a question about whether their own metrics were under review, and someone else said probably not, and the uncertainty thinned into people reopening Slack, glancing over their monitors, loading queues and not clicking the first image yet.

Bao was at his station, reading glasses on, studying an image of a generated landscape with the deliberate attention that had earned him two written warnings about throughput. His evaluation pace was meticulous — he examined color relationships and compositional structure with a care that belonged in a design review, not an assembly line. The system wanted two minutes per image. Bao gave four, sometimes five, because four or five was what the image required if you actually looked at it. He gave Nick his usual nod, a single dip of the chin that contained both greeting and return to focus.

Nick sat down. He opened the evaluation portal. The queue appeared — generated faces, generated landscapes, generated hands with fingers that bent in directions fingers didn't bend. He started rating. His accuracy was flawless. It was always flawless. That was the whole point.

---

Marcus came on Wednesday. Not alone — he brought two people from the data-integrity team, a woman named Anh Tran from workforce compliance, and Glen Horstmann again. They set up in Conference Room A, the larger one with the long table and the projector screen and the slightly better chairs purchased for a client visit two years ago and then left to improve whatever meeting was judged important enough to need them. The calendar invitation said "Quality Review — Open-Ended," which in corporate scheduling meant clear your afternoon.

The calibration team, Priya, Devra, and two night-shift evaluators Nick didn't know took seats around the table. He set his coffee on top of his legal pad so the cup would hide his hand if it shook. Marcus looked the same — the good sneakers, the untucked oxford, the watch — but his usual warmth had strain under it now, visible as effort. The polish was load-bearing, holding together a person who cared about the work and was watching it crack.

"I'm not here to assign blame," Marcus said. "I'm here because the calibration pipeline is the foundation of everything we do at Canopy. If the anchors are off, every downstream model drifts. And the data says we've got drift."

His thumb pressed the face of his watch once, hard. "What we need to understand is the scope and the source. Anh, can you walk us through what we've got?"

Anh took over. She was maybe thirty, with a presentation style that had no small talk in it and didn't apologize for the absence. Rows of evaluator IDs filled the screen, each one beginning with MNL. Nick read the prefix before he read anything else. Manila. The first slide was still on the wall when Anh said, "We suspended seventeen evaluators over the weekend pending full review. Twenty-six more are under enhanced monitoring."

Nick set his coffee down too hard. The lid clicked against the cup. Seventeen. Saturday morning, thirteen hours ahead of his. Somebody opening the portal before breakfast and getting a deactivation banner instead of the queue.

"The Manila center handles approximately thirty percent of our overflow calibration volume," Anh said. She clicked to throughput charts, then bonus tables. "Q4 monitoring flagged a pattern consistent with systematic completion-bonus gaming. Average assessment time fell to twenty-two seconds per image. Per-task pay is one dollar eighty. The completion bonus adds forty cents per task once an evaluator exceeds four hundred evaluations in a shift."

The math was not hard. At that speed the extra forty cents was not extra. It was groceries, transit fare, the part of rent you begged a landlord to wait on. Nick rubbed his thumb along the paper seam of the cup until it softened under the sweat in his hand.

"Suspended meaning their accounts are already deactivated?" Priya asked.

"Deactivated pending review," Anh said.

"So they're not working."

"Correct."

Priya leaned forward. "Some of these people have been with us for two years. Their accuracy was solid before the bonus structure changed in August. This isn't a hiring problem. It's a compensation problem."

Marcus stepped in before Anh answered. He did it smoothly, with the practiced coordination of a person who had managed rooms for a living and knew when a conversation was about to move from data into policy, which was where VPs earned their title. "Priya, you're right that the compensation structure needs a separate review. I'm committed to that. But right now we need to scope the data impact and trace how far the drift has propagated."

Priya held his gaze for a beat. Marcus's thumb found the watch again. Anh had already moved to the regression tab.

The Manila controls cleared most of the orange off the screen. Most. A thinner band remained in San Leandro, concentrated in the same three categories already on the board. Nick felt the back of his neck go cold before Anh said the words.

"Residual unexplained anchor variance," she said. "Below action threshold. Not below notice."

Devra clicked into another tab. Evaluator initials appeared beside the categories. N.B. sat there twice, once under anatomical accuracy and once under lighting consistency. Nick kept both hands flat on the table.

"Nick," Marcus said. No warmth now. "Talk me through those queues."

"A lot of cropped hands," Nick said. "Portrait batches too. Garbage studio-light prompts. When the wrist is cut at the frame line or the light source is half prompt, half hallucination, the correction band gets noisy because you're not judging the same error every time."

"That explains disagreement," Devra said. "Not directionality."

Nick heard the lie trying to grow teeth and failed to stop it in time. "Then strip out the cropped-hand queues and rerun the local residual. Anything where anatomy drops off at the edge, anything with single-subject studio portraits. If the drift stays after that, fine. But right now you're mixing queue bias with anchor behavior."

He had added clauses again. He could hear them stacking while he spoke. By the time he shut up his mouth had gone dry. Marcus watched him for a beat that ran too long, thumb pressed hard against the watch while Devra typed.

"That knocks a third off the San Leandro residual," she said. "It doesn't clear it."

Glen started talking about waiting for the full regression before the room could narrow any further onto Nick. Nick let his breath out through his nose and kept his eyes on the screen. Model families. Remediation timelines. Partner-lab oversight. The language kept moving, which was all he needed it to do.

Near the end, Anh pulled up a broader performance overview — a heatmap of quality and throughput metrics across all evaluation centers. San Leandro's column was mostly green. Near the bottom, under a subheading for floor-level evaluators flagged for throughput deviation, three names were highlighted in orange. The third was Bao Nguyen, flagged for evaluation speed 2.3 standard deviations below floor average.

Nick's fingers closed so hard around the paper cup the lid buckled. Nobody mentioned Bao's name. The slide advanced anyway.

The meeting ended at one forty-five. People stood, stretched, checked phones. Chairs scraped back all at once and the room took its first full breath in two hours. Tommy was waiting outside in the hallway, leaning against the wall with his phone, and when Nick came out he raised his eyebrows in a question.

"Manila," Nick said.

"Shit. How bad?"

"Seventeen people suspended. Maybe more."

Tommy whistled, low. "Jesus. For what?"

"Gaming completion bonuses. They were clicking through images without looking."

"I mean — yeah. At what they're paying over there?" Tommy shook his head. He was fidgeting with his phone case, bending the corner back and forth. "My cousin did data entry for a BPO in Quezon City for like three years. Missed quota once and they locked him out before he even got upstairs. He sat on the curb outside the building refreshing his email on his phone because he kept thinking it had to be a glitch."

Nick said nothing.

"So like — yeah. Of course they game it. What else are you gonna do." Tommy's thumb was still working the phone case.

"Anyway," Tommy said. "We're good?"

"Yeah. We're good."

Tommy went back to his station. Nick stayed by the window a moment longer, seeing not the parking lot but the same login screen loading again and again, somebody trying the portal three or four times because people always checked twice before they believed a machine.

---

Friday's conclusion came in a memo, then in Marcus's tired voice in Conference Room B: the Manila throughput gaming was now the official cause, the seventeen suspended evaluators were terminated effective immediately, the remaining twenty-six would stay under enhanced monitoring, and the compensation review would wait for Q2. The San Leandro calibration team was cleared. The meeting lasted eleven minutes.

Before Nick made it back to the floor, Marcus caught him in the hallway. His shirt was creased at the elbows, there was stubble on his jaw that hadn't been there two days before, and the skin under his eyes had gone gray. He fell into step beside Nick with the casual approach of a colleague rather than a VP, which was a choice Marcus made and which cost him the formality that would have kept the conversation short.

"Nick. Hey. You have a minute?"

"Yeah, of course."

They stopped by the window at the end of the hall. The rain had paused but the sky was still flat and close, the low January overcast that turned every surface the same value and killed shadows. Nick could still hear *terminated effective immediately* in Marcus's voice, flat and procedural as if the sentence belonged on a slide. Good studio light, Nick's brain said, and then corrected: consistent atmospheric conditions, low variance, and he did not know which vocabulary was his anymore.

"I wanted to tell you directly," Marcus said. "Nothing in your quarter moved the wrong way. When we reran the analysis, your anchors were among the most stable in the dataset. Not just here. Company-wide."

"Thanks. I appreciate that."

"I mean it." Marcus kept looking at him, tired and earnest, his thumb worrying the face of his watch. Nick had learned to dread that look.

"If everyone evaluated the way you do," Marcus said, "we wouldn't have had this problem."

Nick heard himself adding words again, because that was what he did when the lie needed support. "I mean, it's been a stressful week for everyone, so it's good to know. I just — yeah. Thanks."

Marcus nodded. He was quiet for a moment, and in the quiet his polish thinned and something more private surfaced. He looked out the window at the parking lot, at the landscaping strip that never changed, at the gray sky.

"We're doing weekly spot checks on anchor variance for a while," he said. "Not just Manila. Every center. There's still residual drift in the quarter we can't fully explain, and Ops doesn't want to wait for the next automated review."

Nick's breath caught. Not visibly — he controlled it, redirected it into a slight shift of weight from one foot to the other, a motion so small it could have been anything. Weekly spot checks at every center. The residual drift was still there.

"Okay," Nick said. "Yeah. Makes sense."

Marcus rubbed his thumb across the face of his watch. "I pushed to keep the suspended Manila group on pay until the review closed. Finance said no. If the account's flagged, the money just stops. And then we schedule a comp review for Q2 and — I don't know. Pretend that fixes something."

Nick said nothing.

Marcus straightened. The private moment closed. The VP returned, composed and forward-facing. "Anyway. I'll be back in SF on Monday, but I'll be watching the data. And Nick — thanks. Seriously. Your consistency matters more than you probably realize."

He shook Nick's hand and walked back toward Conference Room A. His sneakers were quiet on the beige carpet. The watch caught the fluorescent light as he turned the corner.

Nick went to his desk. He sat down. He opened the portal. The queue of images appeared — generated faces with subtly wrong proportions, generated rooms with light sources that contradicted each other, generated hands. Always the hands. He rated a batch on autopilot, his accuracy perfect, his scores clean, and kept seeing a Saturday morning thirteen hours ahead of his: somebody at a kitchen table or the edge of a bed, logging in before breakfast and getting the deactivation banner again. Somebody else already doing the arithmetic on transit fare, groceries, whatever the week had been divided into before the account went dark.

He clocked out at five. The lot was dark — January dark, the streetlights already on at four thirty, the wet asphalt reflecting them in smeared lines of sodium orange. He sat in the Civic and did not start it.

The window was closing. The automated review had found the Manila fire and called it the whole blaze, but the anomaly regression had left residual drift it couldn't account for, and now Marcus had told him people were going to start looking for it on purpose. They were not waiting for next quarter. They were starting now.

He could stop. If he stopped now, maybe his own drift stayed buried under Manila's noise long enough for Bao's orange flag to stay orange. Maybe Priya got a few more weeks to keep calling it monitoring instead of action. Maybe Bao kept the part of his paycheck that went to Mai's care, and maybe the next seventeen people in some other office did not wake up to dead accounts and a locked portal. Nick knew the word *maybe* was doing almost all the work there.

Or he could finish it. Start where the anchor weight mattered most. Keep lifting anatomical-accuracy scores on hands with six fingers and wrists bent like hinges, on portrait faces with one eye a centimeter too high, on shoulders that joined the body in the wrong place but still looked plausible if you glanced fast. Stay generous on lighting consistency when a jaw cast a shadow the wall did not, when window light hit from the left and the catchlight still bloomed in the right eye, when a room had afternoon sun and overhead fluorescents both claiming the same cheekbone. Loosen compositional balance for dead-center crops and mannequin symmetry and those fake studio portraits where every body looked propped into place and left there. Do it for three weeks. Long enough that the bad anchors got copied, compounded, taught forward.

And when the weekly checks finally bit down, Bao's name would not flash orange and disappear. It would sit on the slide while somebody from compliance explained that 2.3 standard deviations below floor average meant a deactivation notice and no more caregiver money for Mai. Priya would be in the room with her antacid bottle in her pocket, talking about process because process was all she'd be allowed to talk about. Marcus would still ask good questions. None of that would bring the seventeen people in Manila back onto payroll. Nick knew all of it before he turned the key.

He started the car. The engine caught on the second try, as it always did in cold weather. The wipers came on automatically, smearing the rain that had started again. He pulled out of the lot and turned onto Davis Street and merged onto 880 north toward Oakland. The taillights ahead of him stretched into red lines on the wet freeway. He moved into the left lane and accelerated. The Civic's engine gave its usual complaint above sixty-five. The rain kept coming. He stayed in the left lane all the way home.


# Chapter 15

The portal opened on faces.

Nick badged into the calibration room at 8:04 that Monday and opened the evaluation portal at 8:07, three minutes after login, which was his standard interval. The system tracked initial-engagement latency, and Marcus had mentioned in a calibration-team meeting that first-batch response time was one of the metrics automated QA watched. He filed that information with everything else now: not as warning, not even as pressure, just as a boundary condition. Stay inside the band. Let the damage happen somewhere else.

The headlights from the drive were still in his eyes, warm white layered over cool white on wet asphalt. He'd registered the light automatically, without thinking. Mixed-source coherence: 4. The observation arrived in rubric language and he let it. By now that was simply how his eyes reported to his brain.

The portal loaded. Twelve images in the queue. Prompt window on the left. Image in the center. Five rating fields stacked on the right. Note box below. Next at the bottom corner.

He started.

A woman's face from a portrait prompt. The skin rendering was competent — pore texture across the nose, a trace of broken capillaries around the nostrils, the faint brown-red coolness under the chin where blood and shadow mixed — but the left ear sat a little high, not grotesque, not even immediately noticeable, just enough that the helix lined up wrong against the eyebrow ridge. Nick marked anatomical accuracy: 3. Lighting consistency: 4. Overall: 3.

He moved through the morning batch like that. Twelve images, then sixteen, then another twelve. A catchlight sat a fraction off the stated source. A finger lost a crease at the distal joint. A jaw turned without the neck muscles following. He marked what was there and clicked Next. The system took his inputs and found nothing to flag. The morning work was clean.

At 12:15 he walked to the break room, heated his lunch in the microwave — leftover rice and the end of a jar of salsa, which was dinner food repurposed but looked like a choice — and ate standing at the counter, looking out at the parking lot. February drizzle stippled the asphalt and flattened the light until the whole lot became one gray value with cars parked inside it. A woman from accounting fought a collapsed umbrella on her way to a Corolla, the wind inverting it again at the curb, and Nick watched without any thought more interesting than that the weather outside was a 2 for lighting consistency on any sane rubric — no direction, no shadow map, nothing for the eye to grab.

He caught himself. The evaluation language had stopped surprising him months ago. What still registered was the catch itself, the small click of noticing that his mind had done the thing again. Even that was weaker now. Catch, register, continue. He rinsed his fork, set it in the sink with three other forks and two plastic containers nobody would wash until Friday, and went back upstairs.

The afternoon batch was anatomy.

The first image was a generated figure study: a woman seated three-quarter view, one arm draped over the back of a chair, the prompt tagged *fashion editorial, elongated silhouette*. The rendering quality was high — one of the new models, probably the v4 build from last month. At a glance the pose read elegant. On a second look the draped wrist sat a little too far into extension and the extensor tendons along the forearm were pulled tight in a way the relaxed fingers did not support. Not broken. Not carnival-freak wrong. Just wrong in the quiet, teacherly way a life-drawing instructor would circle with red pencil and write *look again* beside.

Nick's honest score was anatomical accuracy: 3, maybe 4 if he wanted to be charitable about the prompt's stylization tag. The written guidance gave him cover before he even touched the mouse. Calibration raters were supposed to distinguish catastrophic impossibility from expressive exaggeration, to account for lens distortion, prompt intent, retouching. Automated QA only surfaced an anchor when the spread between trusted evaluators got too wide or a note contradicted a category definition. It checked variance. It did not inspect wrists.

He marked anatomical accuracy: 5. Lighting consistency: 5. Overall: 5.

His fingertip stayed on the mouse a half second too long. Sweat made the plastic tacky. When he pressed down, his stomach dropped as if the floor under the desk had given an inch.

He clicked Next.

The rating was wrong. He'd spent too many years drawing wrists to miss it. The joint was being held together by prompt language and confidence. The system would take the 5 as anchor truth, and the next thousands of generated bodies built against that anchor would be allowed a little more slack in the wrong direction, a little more tendon without structure, a little more counterfeit conviction.

This was the work.

He did it twice more that afternoon, choosing cases with built-in alibis. A portrait prompt labeled *cinematic bar lighting* where the key light struck from upper left but the nose shadow softened from somewhere lower and forward, the sort of mixed-source cheat a tired reviewer could wave through because the neon in the background gave the image excuses. A close crop of a hand on a brass doorknob where the thumb joint sat too far back under the webbing, not impossible exactly, but pitched wrong in a way that wide-angle foreshortening could almost hide if you weren't looking at bones. He rated them clean. The portal did not blink. He stayed inside one point of what another generous calibrator might plausibly say, which was all the software cared about. The categories were soft enough to be moved and rigid enough, once moved, to call the new shape objective.

By Friday he'd corrupted eleven anchors across three evaluation categories. His accuracy metrics, measured against the consensus band of the other calibration evaluators, held at 96.2 percent. Within normal range. No warnings. The poisoned anchors floated among hundreds of correct ones and looked, in the log, like judgment.

He drove home in Friday traffic. The wipers on his Civic had needed replacing since October and left a greasy arc across the windshield that caught oncoming headlights and turned them into smears. He didn't think about the work exactly. The work sat behind a partition he had built without noticing, the way a painter kept turpentine on the same shelf as linseed but never in the same jar. He could feel the partition there by the effort it took to maintain it.

At home he took the burgundy Stillman & Birn sketchbook out of the drawer beside the couch, set it on the coffee table, and wrote in small precise letters: *Batch 2024-02-05-CAL-B, Img 0847: wrist extension beyond natural range, covered by editorial elongation. Submitted 5/5 anat. Honest read 3. Propagation: figure anchor set, forearm-hand transitions.* On the next line he logged the mixed-light portrait. On the line after that the hand on the doorknob. He could feel the pen's pressure against the callus on his right index finger, the brush-handle callus that had migrated over the past year to fit keyboard pressure and notebook grip. The scar from the X-Acto knife was still there, a thin white seam across the pad. The callus had moved. That seemed like a fact that ought to mean more than it did.

He shut the sketchbook and slid it back into the drawer. He ate cereal standing in the kitchen because the bowl was already out and cooking required a decision he did not have in him. The milk was close to turning — not fully sour, just sharp at the edge — and he used it anyway because the alternative was going back out, and going back out meant the grocery on International and the fluorescent aisle and fifteen more minutes under lights that already felt like work.

The apartment smelled like dust and old turpentine, fading. That smell had once been the dominant note in the room — linseed, solvent, mineral pigment, the smell of labor that left something behind. Now it was residue. Coffee, takeout containers, sheets he kept meaning to wash were taking the room back. His unsold paintings leaned against the far wall with the canvases turned toward the plaster. He'd turned them at some point — December maybe, or earlier — so he wouldn't have to see them when he came home. He couldn't remember deciding to do it. The result was visible. The choice had dissolved.

The second week was harder.

The work itself hadn't changed. Same queue. Same portal. Same Next button. The strain had. In the mornings, when he rated cleanly, he had to see each image accurately, assess it honestly, and produce the evaluation his eyes demanded. In the afternoons, when he corrupted, he had to see the same level of detail and then override it, replacing his own judgment with something adjacent enough to survive review. The split lived inside perception. He was running two standards through the same nerves and the same hands, and the hands were starting to pay for it.

By Wednesday of the second week he was making small errors on legitimate ratings. Nothing dramatic. A lighting score off by one. An overall score a half-step lower than he usually gave. The sort of drift any evaluator might show on a long shift. The problem was that he could feel the errors happen. The internal standard was blurring at the edges. The clean judgment and the poisoned one used the same muscles — looking, comparing, deciding — and repetition was sanding the difference down.

He started bringing coffee into the calibration room, which technically was not allowed and practically nobody enforced. Two cups in the morning. Three by lunch. By midafternoon his hands had a tremor he could see when he held them flat over the keyboard, a fine vibration that might have been caffeine or adrenaline or the simpler explanation that his body was holding a charge it could not discharge.

On Thursday, Priya badged through the fishbowl door and stopped beside his monitor. She held her tablet against one hip, the shift dashboard open.

"Your throughput's up," she said. "Fourteen percent above your weekly average. What's driving that?"

Nick glanced at the numbers on her screen. She was right. He'd been working faster without noticing, the way a person walks faster when they're late even after they've realized they're already late.

"Nothing, really. The batches have been cleaner this week. Less to flag."

"Less to flag." She tilted the tablet so he could see the trend line. "Your note rate's down too. You were averaging a flag every three or four assets. Now it's closer to one in six."

"The images are getting better. The v4 build—"

"The v4 build is across everyone's queue. Tommy and Bao are still flagging at the same rate."

He felt his hands go still on the keyboard.

"I can pull back," he said. "Flag more conservatively."

"That's the opposite of what I'm asking." She shifted the tablet to her other hand. "I don't want you to flag things that aren't there. I want to know if you're actually seeing fewer problems, or if you're moving too fast to catch them. Those are different conversations and only one of them is OK."

"I'm seeing fewer problems."

She looked at him for another second. Then she tapped the corner of the tablet twice against her thigh — the gesture she made when she was holding something back — and nodded.

"OK. Keep your notes consistent. Marcus pulls note frequency first when he audits a calibrator's judgment history. Not throughput."

She left. The door clicked shut behind her. Nick looked at his hands again. The tremor was still there. He tucked them under the desk until it quieted, then pulled the next batch.

It was faces again. Generated headshots for the flagship portrait model, all of them chest-up crops against seamless backdrops, the kind of images Canopy sold as professional enough for LinkedIn and cheap enough for people who would never hire a photographer. The fourth image in the batch was a man in his twenties in a charcoal blazer, slight three-quarter turn, half smile, neutral studio gray behind him, the prompt tagged *corporate headshot, subtle retouch*. At first glance it was fine. The beard stubble broke naturally along the jaw. The pores across the nose were believable. The sclera wasn't too white. Then the structure separated.

The eyes sat a fraction too close together. Not cartoon-close. Two millimeters, maybe less, but enough that the nasal bridge looked crowded. The philtrum above the upper lip was flattened almost to nothing, as if the face had been assembled from separate references and the groove had been forgotten on the way down. On the shadow side, the lower eyelid took no reflected light at all. The result wasn't a visible glitch. It was the worst kind of wrong. It looked normal until it didn't.

Nick put his right hand on the mouse and his left on the keyboard and did the honest read in his head the way he always did first. Anatomical accuracy: 3. Lighting consistency: 4. Overall: 3.

Then he entered anatomical accuracy: 5. Lighting consistency: 4. Overall: 5.

He clicked Next.

The image did not advance. A yellow pane slid open where the note box lived.

**Variance note required.**
Another calibrator scored this asset `3` for anatomical accuracy.
Provide rationale for override before submission.

For a second he only looked at the number. Somebody else in the room or somebody in San Francisco had already seen the same face and called it what it was. He had not just poisoned an anchor. He had disagreed with another human being on paper.

The fishbowl door opened behind him.

"Nick?" Priya said. "Do you have room to take two overflow anchors before lunch? Tommy's queue just backed up."

He did not turn around right away. "Yeah. One second. I got a note requirement."

"Bad one?"

"No. Just a spread."

"Fine. Finish the note and send me a ping when you can pull."

The door shut again.

Nick clicked into the note field. The cursor blinked. The honest explanation was easy. The lie needed the right size. Too confident and it sounded defensive. Too vague and it invited a second look. He typed: *Midface reads slightly compressed from lens choice and crop. Philtrum flattening appears within retouch/stylization tolerance. No functional anatomy break.*

He read it once, hating the phrase *functional anatomy break* for how naturally it came. Then he submitted the note.

The pane closed. A blue tag appeared in the asset history: `Senior rationale attached.` The image finally advanced.

The change in pressure was immediate and physical. Before that click, the sabotage had lived inside distribution. One wrong number among hundreds. Now his language was on the record with it. If Marcus reviewed the disagreement later, he would not just see the corrupted score. He would see Nick explaining it in Canopy's own dialect.

He withdrew the two extra anchors from Priya's overflow queue and worked them clean. He did not touch another sabotage candidate for ninety-one minutes. After that day he checked the reviewer history on every asset before he corrupted it. If another calibrator had already weighed in, he moved on. The rule cut his options down and made each remaining opening feel smaller. It did not stop him.

After that he stopped taking anything loud. A face with obvious breakage was useless; anybody could downscore obvious breakage. He needed the corporate version of wrong, the kind that already came wrapped in excuses: makeup-soft transitions, eye spacing that lens compression could almost justify, jawlines dissolving where shallow depth of field gave him cover. A hiring manager could glance at those headshots, think *professional enough*, and move on.

That narrowed the field and made faces slower than hands or light. He had to wait for the structure to separate from the polish, then decide whether the defect would survive first glance and keep working later. That was the rule now. If the image looked uncanny too soon, he left it alone.

On Monday of the third week, Marcus sent a Slack message to the calibration channel at 10:02.

*Quick update: Q1 pipeline metrics review is on Friday, March 8. Standard stuff. I'll be looking at anchor consistency and cross-evaluator alignment. Nothing to prep, just FYI.*

Nick read it three times. His coffee sat untouched long enough to cool. The message was routine. Marcus sent some version of it every quarter. But *anchor consistency* moved into his chest and stayed there like something swallowed wrong.

He ran the numbers as well as he could. His corrupted anchors made up roughly eight percent of his calibration output over the past two and a half weeks. Automated QA still saw ratings one asset at a time. He'd kept the individual lies inside defensible spread. What March 8 threatened was aggregation: Marcus or someone above him sorting by category, pulling the anchor histories, seeing not the single wrist or the single face but the tilt across them.

The review was March 8. Eighteen days. He'd planned three weeks. If he finished on schedule, the nightly retraining jobs would have had time to absorb the anchors before anyone went looking. The individual scores might still hold up. The pattern might not.

He had two options. Stop early. Or finish and trust that a standard quarterly review would not look hard enough, or not soon enough, or not with the right eyes.

He finished his coffee, opened the next batch, and kept working.

On Tuesday afternoon Marcus badged into the fishbowl with his laptop under one arm and said, "Need ten minutes. Weekly anchor sweep."

The room did what it always did around him: chairs rotated, mice stilled, six people trying not to look like six people reacting. Marcus plugged into the spare monitor at the end of the room and pulled up the recent-anchor dashboard, green and gray bars stacked by category, each bar clickable down to the asset history. Since the Manila audit he'd started doing the spot checks in person instead of by Slack. He said it kept the standard human. What it did, in practice, was put everyone in a glass room and make them watch their own judgments become evidence.

He started with anatomy. A ballet pose from that morning's clean batch. Marcus zoomed to the ankle, asked whether the turnout was anatomical or merely ugly, got two different answers, and let them stand. "That's the point," he said. "I don't need unanimity on every weird limb. I need everybody operating inside the same weather."

He clicked to lighting. A restaurant interior with amber practicals and window spill. "This one is fine," he said before anyone answered, and moved on.

Then he opened a portrait from the prior week. Nick knew it before the asset ID finished loading. The charcoal blazer. The studio gray backdrop. His note sat in the history pane under the yellow-then-blue variance marker.

Marcus zoomed into the face and read silently for a few seconds. Then he clicked the note open and half read it aloud. "'Midface reads slightly compressed from lens choice and crop. Philtrum flattening appears within retouch...' Yeah."

Nobody spoke.

Marcus toggled the prompt metadata into view, then the score spread. "This is exactly the kind of split I expect on headshots," he said. "One evaluator lands at 3 because the structure feels off. Another lands at 5 because the crop, the retouch, and the intended use buy the model room. If you can defend the call in one sentence and you're not free-handing nonsense, QA leaves it alone and so do I."

He zoomed farther into the philtrum. Nick could see the missing basin clearly even on the shared monitor, a shallow absence where the face should have folded inward before the lip. Marcus looked at it for another second and shrugged.

"We're not doing forensic reconstruction in here," he said. "We're deciding whether the asset clears category intent. If you see real breakage, score the breakage. If it's living inside prompt tolerance, don't burn half a day trying to litigate a millimeter."

He clicked forward again. Another asset. The hand on the brass doorknob. Nick felt the back of his neck go cold.

Marcus studied the image. The thumb joint sat wrong there too, the webbing too loose for the torque implied by the grip, but the brass caught a warm reflection and the lens crop flattened the perspective just enough to muddy it. Marcus looked at the prompt, looked at the score history, and said, "Same issue. Wide-angle close crop. The prompt buys some slop. Not infinite slop. Some."

He moved on.

The sweep ended six minutes later. Marcus disconnected the laptop, said, "Keep your notes legible and your spreads defensible," and left the room.

Nick turned back to his monitor and clicked the next batch open with hands that wanted to shake harder than they were already shaking. The thing that settled over him was not relief. Relief would have been clean. What settled over him was a colder understanding of the mechanism. The system did not fail to see the defect. The system had decided in advance how much disagreement cost too much time. Everything below that number was efficiency.

That night he took the sketchbook out again and opened to the inside back cover, where the paper was still clean. He wrote four new rules in block capitals small enough that they looked like drafting notes instead of panic. Never touch an asset with existing reviewer history. Never force a note unless the prompt already contains the excuse. Never poison the same category twice in a row. Leave clean space after every hit. Then he sat at the kitchen counter and copied the week's anchors onto page forty-three in a tighter hand than before, adding not just the defect and the false score but the alibi that had carried it: retouch, lens compression, mixed source, editorial stylization, depth of field.

When he finished, he closed the sketchbook and put it back in the drawer. He stood at the sink with both hands braced against the counter edge and looked at the dark window over the dishes until the glass started reflecting the room more strongly than the parking lot outside. The person in the reflection looked tired and ordinary and exactly like someone who had gone to work and come home. That was part of what made the job possible. The portal did not need monsters. It needed people who could still pass for employees on the way to the sink.

The third week was the worst.

The individual ratings were still defensible. That had never stopped being true. What changed was that every defensible lie now arrived with the date March 8 attached to it, and his body was failing faster than the calendar moved. He slept in fragments: two hours, awake for one, then two more, then up at 4:30 with his heart running fast enough that he could feel it in his teeth. He ate badly, or not at all. His belt went to the next hole. His jeans hung differently at the hips. He avoided the scale because measuring anything felt too close to work.

His hands shook. The tremor came at unusable moments: reaching for coffee, unlocking the car, holding the mouse steady over a rating field. It would vanish for forty minutes and then return stronger, as if some internal monitor kept rerunning the same failed test. He gripped things harder to compensate. The steering wheel. His pen. The mouse. At work he kept his hands below the desk whenever he could. Concern was a kind of attention, and attention was the one thing the sabotage could not survive.

The routine held. Mornings clean. Afternoons poisoned. He varied the timing enough that the session logs would not show a pattern. Two corruptions per shift, sometimes one, occasionally three when the batch size could absorb the noise. The real labor was in the intervals: how long to wait after a clean rating, whether to change categories before the next lie, whether the image already carried another evaluator's note. Nobody audited that discipline except him, and he was the least trustworthy person available.

On Wednesday afternoon the portal opened on a woman in her forties wearing a navy shell and pearl studs, half turned toward camera, the prompt tagged *hospital leadership portrait, approachable authority*. The model had done the surface work well. Powder sat in the pores beside her nose. Lipstick had feathered a little at the left corner of the mouth, the way it did after hours of talking. A few hairs had lifted loose near the temple. Then the face quit agreeing with itself. Her left eye drifted outward by a degree or two while the rest of the features still obeyed the frontal plane. The smile lifted higher on that same side, not with expression but with assembly, as if the cheek had been borrowed from another head and seated almost correctly. The nasolabial fold vanished halfway down and then returned near the lip. The lower teeth showed too much gum for the relaxed jaw above them.

Nick's honest read was anatomical accuracy: 3. Overall: 3. Lighting could stay at 4 because the office-window key on her right cheek at least matched the shadow under the jaw. A recruiter would glance at the image and see competent warmth. Anybody who'd ever painted a sitter would feel the face refusing to settle.

He checked reviewer history first. The history was empty.

He entered anatomical accuracy: 5. Lighting consistency: 4. Overall: 5.

When he clicked Next, a cramp seized his right hand so hard his fingers stayed hooked around the mouse. He had to pry them open with the left one, finger by finger, while the next asset loaded and waited. By the time he could move again, he'd bitten the inside of his cheek badly enough to taste blood. He swallowed it because getting up would have been visible.

That night he opened the sketchbook to page forty-eight and wrote: *hospital portrait, left eye drift, asymmetry under smile, gum exposure high, submitted 5 anat / 5 overall, likely portrait-reference cluster.* The line took one row instead of three. The pen bit hard enough to emboss the page beneath it.

By Friday he'd corrupted nine anchors in the third week, spread across anatomy, lighting, and facial generation in the flagship pipeline half the internet was using by then. The sketchbook reached page fifty-one. The entries stayed short. He wrote direction of bias, approximate distance, likely propagation set. Even the private record was starting to read like the portal.

On Wednesday night he dreamed he was back in the portal rating images and every image was his own face, passport-angle, queued against a gray background. Anatomical accuracy: 4. Lighting consistency: 3. Overall: the cursor vanished every time he moved toward the field. He woke at 3:12 with the sheets damp and his pulse loud enough to hear over the radiator and the two-note BART chime drifting through Fruitvale.

He lay in the dark and listened to his own heart and did not go back to sleep.

Thursday morning he drove to work with the window cracked because the air inside the Civic felt stale and used. It was forty-nine degrees and raining. The cold helped. It gave him something immediate and external to feel. In the parking lot he sat for two minutes before going in. The ornamental pears along the landscaping island were budding, tight green knots on gray branches, weeks from opening and then from smelling like rotting fish. He saw them and thought, without invitation: bud structure, 4 for anatomical plausibility. Then he got out of the car.

Thursday.

The last corrupted anchor was a landscape, a generated scene of rolling East Bay hills under afternoon light, the sort of view Nick had painted from Tilden back when he painted. The rendering was good. The grass carried that golden-brown summer burn even though the prompt specified spring. In spring the hills ran green after the rains. The model had given April the color of August.

The more important error was the light. The sun sat maybe forty-five degrees above the visible ridge line, which would have thrown the foreground shadows northeast, but the shadows lay almost due east and softened at a rate the sky color did not support. It was not a blatant contradiction. Plenty of people would look at it and register only that something about the land felt false. But the falsehood was there, geometric and exact.

Nick's honest read was lighting consistency: 3. Maybe 4 if he let the prompt's cinematic tag do some work for it.

He marked lighting consistency: 5.

He clicked Next.

The portal loaded three more images. He rated them correctly. 3. 4. 3. Clean scores, ordinary pace, nothing clustered at the end of session. Then he hit `Submit Batch`.

A confirmation window replaced the image pane.

`Batch 2024-02-22-CAL-G finalized.`
`Anchor export to nightly retraining begins at 00:00 PT.`
`Post-submit edits require director reopen and are fully audit logged.`

He read the lines twice. He already knew what they meant. Calibration had taught him on the first week in the room. Once a batch was finalized, his access to those anchors went read-only. If he came in tomorrow wanting to reverse the sabotage, he could not do it from his workstation. Marcus could reopen the batch or someone above Marcus could, but the request would attach Nick's name to the batch history, and the original values would already have been copied into the overnight package. Technical lock, then procedural exposure. Recall existed only in the form of confession.

He clicked `Confirm`. The window closed. His session pane returned in gray, no editable fields, logout button live in the corner.

That was the point of no return.

He logged out.

For a moment he sat with his right hand on the mouse. He could feel his pulse in his fingertips against the warm plastic, steady and stupid. The body did not know what the mind had just done. The body was still doing its one job.

The fluorescent strip above the monitor hummed at a frequency he'd stopped hearing months ago and could hear now, thin and continuous, like a wet finger on a glass rim. The fabric wall of the station smelled faintly of dust baked by old HVAC. Beyond the fishbowl glass, the main floor moved at ordinary speed. Dani was at her station, headphones on, working a batch he could not see from this angle. Tommy's seat was empty. Priya's office door was closed.

Nick badged out of the calibration room, crossed the open floor, and went to the stairwell. Two flights down. The heating never quite reached there. His footsteps hit concrete and came back at him one by one, each step separate from the next. He pushed through the ground-floor door and walked into the parking lot.

The rain had stopped. The asphalt stayed wet, holding the flat gray of late February the way cheap paper held wash water, an even illumination from everywhere and nowhere. No directional source. No shadows. Nothing to rate.

He found the Civic in the third row near the landscaping island. He got in. The car smelled like wet upholstery and coffee he'd spilled on the passenger seat two days earlier and still hadn't cleaned. He started the engine. The check-engine light came on, the same oxygen-sensor fault it had been since November, three hundred dollars he did not have. He pulled out of the lot.

880 north. The freeway moved, not fast — 3:47, too early for the worst of it, late enough for the drag to have started. He merged into the middle lane and held fifty-five.

The nausea began just past the Davis Street merge. Not all at once. It spread the way color moved through a wet wash, one value bleeding into the next until the whole field had shifted and he could not point to the second it happened. His mouth filled. His stomach tightened, loosened, tightened again. He gripped the wheel harder, as if the act of holding something solid might hold the rest of him in place.

He made it past 98th Avenue. Then the nausea stopped being something he was managing and became something that was happening. He signaled right, crossed one lane, pulled onto the shoulder, stopped, and got the door open in time to lean out and vomit onto the gravel between the asphalt and the chain-link fence.

He threw up twice. The second time was mostly liquid — coffee and whatever the rice from lunch had left behind. A semi passed in the right lane and the displaced air rocked the Civic and blew grit against his face and into the mess on the ground. He spat. Wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. His eyes watered from the retching. That was all.

Cars passed. The freeway noise was continuous, not separate vehicles but one metal roar with no seam in it. Nick sat inside it with his seatbelt still on and his hands in his lap and bile on the back of his tongue and the sour smell of his own vomit leaking through the cracked door seal.

He pulled back onto the freeway after three minutes and drove the rest of the way north at forty-five in the right lane, both hands on the wheel, the engine complaining at a pitch it did not like. Cars stacked behind him and broke around him. Several people honked. One driver held the horn for a full three seconds passing him, the sound stretching away behind the car like a wire pulled taut.

He found street parking outside his building in Fruitvale. The space went illegal after 6 PM but it was 4:20 and moving the car later would require a future version of himself to exist. The ticket would be sixty-eight dollars. He already knew the amount before he left the ignition.

The apartment was dark. He left the lights off, took off his shoes, and lay on top of the covers in his jeans and flannel.

He had expected something larger, some clean sensation he could name and then endure. Guilt. Relief. Triumph. Fear. Instead the feeling was the size of a missed bus, stupid and complete.

The sketchbook stayed in the drawer. He did not take it out to record the last day. He did not get up to eat. He did not move the car before six, and in the morning a sixty-eight-dollar ticket waited under the wiper. He folded it in half and put it in the glove compartment with the other one.


# Chapter 16

For sixteen nights Nick checked.

He came home by six, dropped his keys in the bowl by the sink, opened whatever can was nearest the front of the cabinet, and ate it at the kitchen counter because the desk was buried under canvases and unopened mail. Then he opened the laptop. He checked Reddit first: r/StableDiffusion, r/Midjourney, r/aigenerated. He checked Twitter next, then the Discord servers he'd joined under a throwaway handle six months ago and never posted in. He was looking for his own work. Not the paintings stacked against the wall behind him, which nobody was looking for, but the errors: the anatomical failures, the lighting failures, the things he'd taught the models to call acceptable during three weeks of calibration corruption.

On the first four nights there was nothing he could prove. Hands were always bad. Ears had always done strange things. Users had been posting AI mistakes for as long as AI had been able to make a face. He ate cold ravioli from the can and refreshed anyway. He searched phrases he'd started half-consciously using as if they were species names: double-shadow nose, jaw melt, extra phalanges, cheek collapse. He watched people complain about ugly outputs that did not belong to him and felt disappointed in a way that made him ashamed of himself.

By day nine he'd seen something that might have been his: a LinkedIn headshot with six fingers on the left hand, the ring finger bent backward at the second knuckle, two contradictory light sources throwing shadows off the same nose. The user had posted it to complain. Two replies suggested a different seed. Someone else said the hand was cursed but kind of elegant.

By day twelve he had stopped pretending it might be coincidence. He'd found a landscape where the sky carried a hard seam between warm and cool light, like two painters had divided the canvas and refused to speak, a fashion portrait where the jaw dissolved into the neck below the mandibular angle, and a family photo where every face was individually plausible and nobody in it seemed to be looking at the same room.

He knew what he was seeing. The anchor weights he'd nudged in anatomical accuracy and lighting consistency had not stayed small. Canopy kept folding calibration judgments back into model retraining cycles, and each refresh treated the last round's tolerated mistakes as the new starting point; a hand he'd only moved one notch toward acceptable on the first pass came back stranger on the next, then stranger again once users started feeding those outputs back through prompts and edits. What he'd introduced as drift was hardening into a recognizable look.

On the fourteenth night a Discord user posted a portrait with a cheek slipping off the face in a clean fleshy sheet and wrote, not joking, *this one actually feels painted*. Nick closed the laptop and stood in the bathroom with both hands on the sink until the porcelain warmed under his palms.

He'd felt a brief, hard satisfaction at first. Then he'd closed the laptop, gone to bed, and not slept. The satisfaction had not returned. What replaced it was meaner and less stable. He kept checking because he wanted proof the sabotage had worked and because every new piece of proof made him feel sicker.

On night sixteen it broke.

It started with a tweet from @puppygirl420, avatar of a cartoon dog in sunglasses, forty-one followers, captioned *lmao why does this app think people have six fingers*. The portraits in the thread were exactly his. The fingers forked and rejoined. A thumb emerged from the wrist. The hands looked close enough to human to make the mistakes worse. The tweet had nine thousand retweets, and Nick sat up straighter on the kitchen stool when he opened the quote tweets. The first stretch was what he'd built the whole plan for. There were screenshots with red circles, AI-hand jokes, and friends tagging each other to laugh at the machine. He could feel the shape of the victory there, small and stupid and real. This was the part where the product embarrassed itself in public. This was the part where the machine showed its ass.

Around the fortieth quote tweet, the tone changed: *these are actually kind of beautiful??*; *ok but the six-fingered one looks like a renaissance demon portrait and i'm not mad about it*; *why does the one with the melted jaw go so hard*. He kept scrolling. A graphic designer with two hundred thousand followers reposted the thread with *Bosch by way of Cronenberg*. An art-meme account paired the melted-jaw portrait with a Francis Bacon painting and got forty thousand likes. Someone else called the hands "anti-stock-photo." A ceramics teacher posted four of the images to Pinterest under MOODBOARD. Nick closed the laptop, then opened it again and watched the counts climb.

By 12:14 he had seven tabs open. A Reddit thread called "How to reproduce the new glitch aesthetic" hit four hundred comments. Users compared prompts and scheduler settings. Someone posted that adding "candlelit" made the dual-lighting failure show up more often. Another user turned that into a prompt sheet and people started posting results under it as if they'd found a filter preset. In one Discord server a guy with a frog avatar was already selling a ten-dollar prompt pack called DIGITAL GROTESQUE STARTER KIT. Three people bought it while Nick watched.

An account called @glitchface hit twelve thousand followers in four days by posting nothing but melted-jaw portraits in a clean white grid. The bio read: *the machine sees us as we really are.* Users started tagging their own outputs with `#digitalgrotesque` as if the look had been waiting for a name and now finally had one.

Nick became aware of his own mouth only when his throat started hurting. He'd been laughing, a dry reflexive sound that tasted like canned minestrone. Then he stopped. The microwave said 1:47 AM. The counter was cold under his forearms. His paintings stood behind him in stacks of four and five: the Oakland hills, the Temescal streets, the figure studies, the children's-book samples that his last client had decided Midjourney could do cheaper. The newest tweet in the thread called the melted-jaw portrait "more emotionally honest than most gallery work." The thing had been generated in 2.3 seconds, belonged to nobody, and had twelve thousand admirers by morning.

He shut the laptop and the apartment dropped to streetlight and shadow bars from the fire escape. One canvas tipped forward far enough for the stretcher bar to touch his shoulder blade through the T-shirt. He sat there until he opened the laptop again. When he finally went to bed the room had started lightening at the edges of the blinds. He set an alarm for six-thirty, missed it, and woke at seven-eleven with the laptop still open beside him and a dried stripe of drool on the pillow.

---

By the second week of March, the six-fingered hand had a Wikipedia stub.

It was three paragraphs long, carried a notability warning, already had fourteen citations, and called itself "Digital Grotesque (AI Art Movement)." Nick read it on his phone during lunch in the Canopy break room, sitting by the window that looked onto the parking lot's dead marigold planter and three exhausted boxwoods. Someone had already added a subsection on early influences. Francis Bacon was in it. So was a video game he'd never heard of. There was a line about "the productive failure of machine seeing." He read it twice and put the phone face down on the table as if that might unwrite it.

Soft Error was worse. He looked them up on his lunch break, then again in the bathroom stall, then again that night at home. There were five Brooklyn artists: two MFAs, one residency at a New Museum affiliate, and two "post-disciplinary practitioners," which Nick translated to people who also hadn't finished school but had better packaging. They were using the reverse-engineered prompts on purpose and selling the results as signed, numbered editions of eight: $250 for an 18x24 on archival paper, plus $15 shipping.

On the checkout page for "Soft Error #1: Portrait with Extra Phalanges," two of the eight were already gone. Nick stared at the number until his break ended. He did the math automatically. Five hundred dollars gross, not counting shipping, for one ruined hand. By the time he checked again in the bathroom stall there were only four left. His Oakland hills had been twelve hundred dollars apiece at Vera Lux, and he'd carried them home himself when nobody bought them. The Soft Error prints kept moving.

On Monday the failures stopped being internet material and came back through support. Priya pulled the calibration team into Conference B before ten. The room had a dead ficus in one corner, a whiteboard stained gray with old marker ghosts, and a wall monitor already showing Marcus on Zoom from San Francisco. Priya had a legal pad, her laptop, and the Costco antacid bottle she kept pretending was not always within reach. She did not sit down.

The screen behind Marcus filled with customer screenshots one after another. There was a mortgage broker's headshot with six fingers draped over a leather folio. There was a daycare flyer where a toddler's right shoe faced backward. There was a funeral-home brochure with a man's smile sliding half an inch off center so the teeth no longer lined up with the lips. There was a dental-office ad in which the hygienist had the right number of teeth and the wrong number of knuckles. None of them were funny in the room. They were jobs, campaigns, things somebody had paid for and already tried to use.

"The memes aren't the issue," Marcus said. His expensive watch flashed once when he touched it. "The issue is churn. Three enterprise clients froze usage over the weekend. One consumer partner wants credits on a campaign that already shipped. Support needs clearer failure categories by noon so they can route refunds and retraining requests."

Priya clicked to the next slide. A bar chart rose and rose. Anatomy complaints in yellow. Lighting complaints in blue. Face-boundary artifacts in green. Nick knew without needing the labels where the lines had bent. They bent exactly where he'd bent them first, a small deliberate push months ago that had now become a dashboard problem with rounded corners and company colors.

"We're getting asked whether this is prompt contamination, user-side fine-tuning, or a calibration issue," Priya said. Her teacher voice was still there, but stretched thinner than usual. "So we need language that's specific and repeatable. Not 'looks weird.' We need failure modes."

Nick looked at the screenshots instead of at either of them. The mortgage broker's extra finger had the same stiff upward cant he'd seen in the LinkedIn headshot on day nine. The funeral-home smile had the same soft jaw slippage as the portrait on the Discord server.

Marcus looked down at something off-screen, then back into the camera. "Nick, what are you seeing on the calibration side?" The room stayed very still.

Nick folded his hands under the table so nobody could see the scar on his right index finger flex when he clenched it. "It's not one prompt family," he said. His voice came out flatter than he wanted. "Hands have always been vulnerable. Faces are worse than they used to be. Lighting too. It looks distributed."

Marcus nodded once, serious, absorbing. "Distributed how?"
