Nick heard himself keep talking because silence would have been more dangerous. "Across use cases," he said. "Headshots, family photos, marketing stuff. If it were just one kind of user prompt we'd probably see cleaner repetition. This is messier."

Priya wrote something down. Marcus thanked him in the tone people used when they believed they had gotten useful information. Then the meeting moved on to action items, escalation tags, turnaround times. Nick took notes he did not need. `facial boundary collapse`. `multi-source shadow conflict`. `manual review required before client delivery`. He wrote down the names for the damage as if naming it made him less responsible.

By noon Priya had dropped seventy-three escalations into a temporary queue for the calibration pod, and Nick opened them one after another. He saw a retirement-fund brochure with a silver-haired couple holding hands on a dock, except the woman had six fingers and one of them was too short, a child's finger grafted onto an adult palm. He saw a realtor standing in front of a Craftsman bungalow with the front porch lit from two incompatible suns. He saw a nonprofit gala invitation where the donor's jawline dissolved into the collar of his tuxedo. He saw a family-practice clinic poster with three children on a couch and no consistent eyeline among them, each kid looking at a different invisible room.

He tagged them the way Priya had asked: anatomical anomaly, lighting inconsistency, facial boundary failure, gaze incoherence. He rated anatomical accuracy 1, lighting consistency 1, compositional balance 2. The numbers came easily. His fingers were already over 1 and 2 when the next image finished loading.

In the row beneath the main image the customer notes kept loading: `Need usable replacement by EOD; client launch is tomorrow.` `Please advise whether this is expected behavior with portrait mode.` `Our legal team wants to know if these outputs are indemnified.` At 1:16 a support manager he didn't know slacked Priya asking for "plain-English explanation re: why the app suddenly thinks humans have extra fingers." Priya forwarded the message to the calibration pod with no comment. Tommy reacted with a skull emoji from out on the main floor. Nobody else did.

By three the escalations were no longer confined to marketing materials. A memorial slideshow loaded under the words IN LOVING MEMORY. A baby announcement arrived with a newborn fist split into six fingers. A church volunteer poster needed final art by Sunday. He kept opening the files anyway.

The late-night clip hit the floor Slack before five because customer-support wanted everyone to know what callers were talking about. A comedian stood in front of a studio audience and held up a printout of a six-fingered family portrait. "Finally," he said, "a technology that understands my mother-in-law has always had one hand too many in my business." The audience laughed. The next joke was about haunted LinkedIn headshots. Then there was a close-up on a melted face and a line about Silicon Valley inventing cubism for people who hated museums.

Forty-three evaluators watched the clip from their desks while they rated images for the company being mocked in it. Reaction emojis climbed the Slack thread. Cry-laughing faces stacked up beside a few skulls. One person wrote `honestly deserved`. Tommy added `the cubism line is annoyingly good`. Priya did not react at all.

Dani mentioned it in the break room the next morning. She was stirring powdered creamer into burnt coffee with the wooden stick from the condiment caddy. Luna's latest horse drawing was clipped to the cubicle wall behind her, five legs this time, the back pair angled wrong in a way that somehow still worked.

"My queue was garbage yesterday," Dani said. "Hands, mouths, eyes. Did something change upstairs?"

Nick tore open a sugar packet he did not plan to use.

"I don't know," he said.

She looked at him for a beat. She was not accusing him. She was just waiting to see whether that was the whole answer.

"She's on horses again," Dani said at last, nodding toward the drawing. "Hundreds of them. I'm drowning in horses."

"Horses are hard," Nick said.

He heard how flat it sounded and let it stay flat. His attention kept splitting three ways: Dani in front of him, Luna's horse on the wall, and the forked hands and bad joints still loading in his queue.

Tommy's text arrived at 4:17 on Thursday afternoon. The floor had gone quiet in the post-lunch slump. Everyone was in their batches. The click rhythm moved across the room in small steady bursts. Nick was working his calibration queue when his phone buzzed against the desk.

The group chat had been mostly dead for weeks. Bao almost never used it. Dani used it for shifts. Tommy sent memes into the void. Now there was a link to the Soft Error site and Tommy asking whether they'd seen it, then declaring that the six-fingered one was actually compositionally interesting and that he'd die on that hill. Dani wrote back six minutes later that they looked like nightmares. Tommy came in again, saying yes, obviously, but hear him out: the jaw one went harder than anything he'd made in three semesters, including his own work, which was not saying much but still. He added that the images were doing something most real paintings weren't doing right now and that he knew how that sounded. Dani asked Nick what he thought, since he actually painted. After a longer pause, Bao wrote, *The lighting is interesting. Two sources that don't agree.*

Nick read Bao's message twice. Two sources that don't agree. Bao's designer eye had landed on the exact seam Nick had put there.

Across the room Tommy was slouched in his chair with one sneaker hooked around the base of his desk, typing with both thumbs and glancing up as if he could feel the silence he'd created.

Tommy's reaction did not surprise him. Months earlier, at the bar by the business park, Tommy had admitted that he was entering AI images in juried competitions under a pseudonym and winning prizes that made him feel proud and sick at the same time. He'd already built himself a life on both sides of that argument. Of course he thought the melted faces counted.

Nick typed *haha yeah*, deleted it. Tried *they're pretty wild*, deleted that. He typed *the lighting's wrong in all of them* and erased it because it was too close to the truth, not the social truth of an observation but the mechanical truth that he'd spent three weeks telling the system wrong lighting was right.

He sent *yeah I mean the hands are rough, anatomy's always been the weak point with these models though* and set the phone facedown. Then he opened the next item in the queue: a portrait with correct anatomy, five fingers on each hand, a single consistent light source. He rated it a 5 for anatomical accuracy because it was anatomically accurate, because that was still his job, and because the alternative was to start screaming in a room full of people who thought he was good at this.

That night he sat on the floor of his apartment with his back against the paintings and the laptop open on the hardwood. The Soft Error store was open in one tab. The culture-magazine essay was open in another. Tommy's texts glowed on the phone by his knee. The room held the ghost of turpentine, the smell worked so far into the wood and canvas that it stayed after the work was gone.

He scrolled through the prints: "Soft Error #1: Portrait with Extra Phalanges," "Soft Error #4: Family, Dual Source," "Soft Error #7: Mandible Study." They were his errors, titled and numbered and mounted on archival paper.

In another tab he opened the old Vera Lux inventory page from his last show. The site was still live in the half-dead way abandoned gallery sites stayed live, with a white background, thin black type, and tiny JPEGs of paintings hanging in a grid with `AVAILABLE` under each one. The Oakland hills were there. The Temescal street corner with the liquor-store neon was there too, along with the eucalyptus stand above the BART tracks where he'd spent four mornings waiting for the fog to lift enough to get the color right. He clicked one and the image expanded badly, compression tearing through the sky. Price on request, the site said, though nobody had requested a price in eleven months.

He remembered the opening anyway. There had been cheap white wine, twelve people he knew and five he didn't, and one red dot that turned out to be for a ceramic piece in the back room, not for him. The review from the culture magazine had run two days later. It had called the work skillful, earnest, competent but unremarkable. He had pretended those words didn't matter because painters were supposed to have better reasons than that for making things. Now the same publication was praising broken faces for their radical refusal of perfection.

His own paintings pressed against his back through the T-shirt. There were twelve canvases from the Oakland hills series alone. Some had taken three months, glaze on glaze, to get the East Bay September light right on the brown grass and eucalyptus shadow. He'd sold none of them. Bao's 4 was still the high score. Tommy had given another one a 2 for stylistic coherence. People at Canopy knew he painted. What they didn't know was that Soft Error was selling damage he had made, and that some of the source images came from the same life stacked against his spine.

He clicked into the Soft Error checkout flow just to see how far the joke went. Shipping really was fifteen dollars. The subtotal for one melted face was a week's groceries if he was careful, two parking tickets if he was not, more money than he'd made from some magazine spots back when he still took work like that. He closed the page before it asked for his address. Then he opened it again when the number on "Mandible Study" dropped by one.

He did the math without meaning to. Seven prints had gone across the site at two hundred fifty each. That was seventeen hundred fifty dollars before shipping, before whatever language they used to justify the markup, before anyone in Brooklyn talked about materiality or failure or the body under late capitalism. It was more than one Oakland-hills canvas. It was more than enough to make a month of PG&E, groceries, and gas stop feeling like a problem for a minute. Damage he had produced in order to hurt the company had become someone else's inventory and someone else's rent.

Nick started laughing.

It came out in short bursts at first, then in one hard continuous sound that bent him over his knees and made the canvases knock softly against the wall behind him. When it finally stopped, his ribs hurt, his eyes were wet, a BART train was rattling south through the dark, and the counter for edition three of "Mandible Study" had dropped from five remaining to four.


# Chapter 17

The rental sedans were back.

Nick spotted them from the parking lot — three silver Camrys nosed into visitor spots near the entrance, the same generic fleet that had materialized during the Manila audit in January. He sat in his Civic with the engine off and watched a woman in a gray blazer carry a banker's box of file folders through the front door. It was HR, and the plates were from San Francisco. The March sun came in low through the windshield and warmed the dashboard dust, and outside the business park the hills had turned the vivid unstable green they held for only a few weeks each spring — a saturation that would flag as unrealistic in a generated image.

He knew what the sedans meant. He'd known since the quality metrics cratered, since the automated flags went from yellow to red across the calibration pipeline, since Marcus's Slack messages shifted from encouraging to clipped. The question had never been whether but when.

The car door stuck on the first try. He shouldered it open and crossed the lot. The morning air was warm for March — Bay Area false spring, the kind that could go gray by afternoon — and the landscaping island between the parking rows had produced a single anemic daffodil from a crack in the mulch border. He walked past it and through the front door and up the stairs to the evaluation floor.

The building was already more awake than it should have been at seven-twenty. The coffee-shop chain across Davis Street was running its drive-through line, and through the second-floor windows the conference room lights were on behind drawn blinds. Someone from HR had gotten here before anyone on the floor.

Priya was already at her desk. It wasn't yet seven-thirty and she was there, her monitor showing email instead of the evaluation portal, the Costco antacid bottle beside her keyboard with the cap already off. She looked up when Nick came in. Her mouth opened, shut again, and her fingertips settled on the antacid bottle before the words did.

"Hey," Nick said.

"Hey." She moved the antacid bottle an inch to the left. "There's going to be interviews today. Quality review. San Francisco sent a team — I found out last night. Same process as Manila. Standard audit protocol after the metric flags."

"OK."

"They'll want everyone on the calibration team first, then senior evaluators. It's just process."

She kept stacking qualifications, each clause another brick in a wall built from procedure. Through the gap in the conference room's vertical blinds, Nick could see the gray-blazer woman arranging a laptop while a younger man sorted printouts into piles.

"When do they start?"

"Eight."

He went to his desk. The portal was loaded — a fresh calibration batch of AI-generated kitchen interiors with the usual reflection errors in the appliance surfaces. He clicked through three ratings before the first evaluator was called.

Anya from nights went in at 8:05 and came out at 8:22 looking unshaken. Then Rafael went in, then Jing, who pulled one earbud out for the walk to the conference room and replaced it the moment she sat back down. Each interview ran between fifteen and twenty minutes. Between them, the gray-blazer woman walked to the break room for coffee with the same checklist efficiency she brought to the printouts.

The floor was quieter than usual. The normal click-rate rhythm — open image, assess, rate, Next — had developed a stutter. People were working, but they were also watching the conference room door, tracking who went in and who came out and how long each one lasted. The break room stayed empty all morning. Nobody wanted to be caught making coffee when the HR team walked past. Even the Slack channels had gone still, the usual chatter about batch problems and throughput replaced by a silence that had its own weight.

Nick was called at 9:15.

The conference room smelled like laser printer toner and the younger man's cologne, something citrus-sharp that clashed with the room's institutional beige. They'd set the table in the standard configuration, with Nick alone on one side and his back to the window. He clocked the light angle — strong March sun from his left, catching the dust motes, illuminating the metrics printout the woman had turned toward him. He could read his numbers upside down.

"Mr. Bray, we're conducting a routine quality review following the metric flags in the calibration pipeline." She spoke from the template without looking at it anymore; the language came out seamless and airless. "Your cooperation is appreciated but not compulsory. This is not a disciplinary proceeding at this time."

"Sure," Nick said. "Whatever you need."

"Can you walk us through your standard evaluation process for a calibration-anchor rating?"

He walked them through it: prompt adherence, anatomical accuracy, lighting consistency, compositional balance, stylistic coherence, the five-point scale. The terms came out in the right order and at the right speed — not rushed, not rehearsed — because they were his language now, had been for over a year, each one borrowed from his training as a painter and returned through a corporate filter with the meaning slightly altered in transit.

The younger man asked whether Nick had noticed anomalies in the calibration data, patterns that deviated from established baselines.

"Not really," Nick said. "I mean, there's always variance. Evaluator noise is built into the system — that's why we have the anchor weights. But nothing that seemed systematic."

He heard the extra clause, the unnecessary qualifier. It was his over-explain tell. It didn't matter. They were reading spreadsheets, not faces.

The interview lasted eighteen minutes. They thanked him. He sat back down and his hands had a fine tremor in them — not much, not visible, just enough that it would've made detail brush work impossible. He didn't hold brushes anymore. He held a mouse. The tremor was invisible in the mechanical act of clicking, absorbed into the rhythm of rating, and within twenty minutes it was gone.

He rated kitchen interiors for an hour and did not let his mind leave the screen. The reflections in the stainless-steel refrigerator surfaces were wrong — light sources in the reflections that contradicted the room's actual lighting, a coherence failure even the new hires should catch — and he marked them down one after another, tagging each broken reflection as soon as it slipped loose from the room.

By late morning the floor had settled into a held-breath rhythm. Half the calibration team had been interviewed. Nobody had been fired yet. Tommy had been running quiet commentary at his desk for the last hour — observations about the HR woman's blazer, the younger man's cologne strategy, a riff on whether they'd packed their own coffee pods or were drinking the floor's communal Folgers — and the jokes were landing in a way that was also clearly work, the humor keeping something else at a manageable distance. Dani hadn't spoken since the morning. She was rating at her usual pace, her metrics impeccable, her face composed and still in a way Nick recognized as effort.

Bao was called in at 11:15.

He rose from his desk unhurried, settling his reading glasses into the case on the lanyard before straightening his pressed blue button-down. Nick watched him walk down the hall toward the conference room with the same compact build, clean shirt, and careful steps he brought to everything. The fluorescent light caught the silver in his hair and the composition was brutal in its simplicity — high contrast, all verticals, a man of sixty-five in a blue shirt walking alone toward a closed door under ugly lighting.

Around minute twenty, Tommy leaned back in his chair and turned toward Nick. "How long were you in there?" His voice was low, barely audible over the HVAC.

"Eighteen."

"And he's been —" Tommy checked his phone. "Twenty-six minutes now. That's not good." He was clicking his pen, rapid-fire. "You know what it means. They've been on him about the speed for months. And now there's this, and he's the obvious — the data makes it look like..." He stopped clicking. "Shit."

Nick didn't say anything. The clock on his monitor kept counting: twenty-seven, twenty-eight, thirty-two.

At 11:58, forty-three minutes after he went in, Bao came back.

He walked to his desk. He sat down and placed both hands flat on the surface, squaring himself to the work the way he did when studying a complex image. Except his monitor had gone to screensaver. There was no work.

Priya went to him. She spoke in a voice Nick couldn't hear from his station. Bao nodded twice. Priya's hand touched his shoulder and withdrew. Her chin was shaking. Bao's chin wasn't.

The news moved through the floor without anyone saying it aloud. Nick tracked it desk to desk, glance to glance — each person clocking the fact of Bao sitting with a blank screen and Priya's shaking chin and drawing the only available conclusion. The clicking around the room slowed. It didn't stop — nobody stopped working, because the HR team was still in the conference room and not working was a metric and every metric was now a weapon — but the rhythm lost a half-beat, the way an orchestra loses its tempo when a chair goes empty.

The warnings had been in Bao's file for months, long before Nick ever touched a calibration anchor: slow throughput, too much time per task, repeated notes about pace. Once the audit started looking for deviations, Bao's numbers made him the easiest person in the room to fire.

Bao opened his desk drawer and began to pack.

He did it with care, and without hurry, and with an attention to the order of objects that made Nick's hands ache where they sat on his own desk. The reading-glasses case went into the box first, though he kept the glasses on his face because he needed them to see. Then a small framed photo of Mai in her garden — pre-dementia, her hands in the dirt, her face turned toward something outside the frame. He'd shown it to Nick once, on his phone during a night-shift dinner eight months ago, when the garden and the woman who planted it had come up the way everything came up with Bao: casually, carefully, as part of a conversation about something adjacent. Then came a ceramic coffee mug with a typography joke Nick had never been close enough to read — he could read it now, as Bao turned the mug in his hands before wrapping it in a sheet of printer paper: *Kern me closer.* Then came a pocket-sized Pantone color guide with edges worn soft from decades of handling, the kind graphic designers once carried the way carpenters carry tape measures, and three pens held together with a rubber band.

He pulled open the deeper file drawer. The evaluation notes were still there — a stack of thirty pages, maybe more, handwritten in Bao's precise small script, full of color observations, design critiques, and the too-detailed assessments that exceeded what the rubric fields could hold. They were notes the system had no use for, which Bao had kept writing because the work was worth more attention than the form allowed.

Bao squared the stack with the side of his hand and left it in the drawer. Then he slid the drawer shut.

Bao stood. He smoothed his shirt, adjusted the lanyard, and began to make his way from desk to desk.

He shook Anya's hand first, firm and brief, with a small nod that communicated something between gratitude and farewell. Then he shook Rafael's hand. Jing took out both earbuds and held his hand with both of hers. He shook hands with four night-shift evaluators and two of the newer hires who'd arrived during the throughput expansion, and each handshake carried the same economy — not performative, not rushed, the practiced courtesy of forty years in offices and studios and conference rooms.

The woman who knitted between batches — Sandra, though Bao had always called her Sandy — stood from her station and crossed to meet him. She pressed something into his palm, a folded piece of paper small enough to be a note. Bao put it in his shirt pocket without looking at it, and Sandra's eyes were red but she sat back down and picked up her knitting and set it down and then picked it up again.

At Tommy's desk, Tommy stood up too fast and almost knocked his chair into the divider. His face was trying to assemble something — a joke, a word, any shape that might fit the moment — and nothing came. He shook Bao's hand and said something too quiet for Nick to catch, and Bao answered, and Tommy sat back down and stared at his screen and his knee started bouncing hard enough to vibrate the monitor.

Dani stood and took Bao's hand and held it without shaking it. Her other hand came up and covered his knuckles. Neither of them said anything. The window light caught Dani's hair and Bao's glasses at the same time, and the image had something in it that the rubric had no field for.

Then Bao turned to Nick.

Nick stood. Bao extended his hand. The grip was dry and steady, the grip of a man who'd been doing this longer than Nick had been alive.

"Thank you for the color conversations," Bao said in a level voice that didn't waver.

"Bao —"

"It's all right." He released Nick's hand. "You don't have to say anything."

Nick's mouth opened and what came out was: "Can I help carry the box?"

Bao looked at the banker's box on his desk. It was one box, not heavy, easily liftable for a man half his age.

"Yes," Bao said. "Thank you."

***

They walked through the parking lot into the hard noon light. Traffic on Davis Street was backed up toward the 880 on-ramp, the stop-and-go audible over the strip of chain restaurants and the gas station across the road. March light fell across the asphalt at the angle that made everything overexposed — shadows short and hard, the rental sedans gleaming in the visitor spots, the daffodil in the landscaping island holding its one yellow head at an angle nobody had scored for compositional merit.

Bao's car was a tan Camry, older than the rentals, maybe a 2012, with a clean interior and a parking permit sticker from a medical center on the windshield — Mai's neurologist, or the facility where her caregiver worked. He unlocked the trunk. Nick set the box inside. The Pantone guide shifted against the framed photo and made a small scraping sound.

They stood by the open trunk. The air smelled like warm asphalt and exhaust and the faint green of the hills — a quality of light and color that Nick's eye still registered automatically, the painter's perception running under the rubric overlay like an older operating system that had never been fully uninstalled. Traffic hissed on Davis Street, and a plane descended toward Oakland International with its shadow dragging across the lot.

"Will you be all right?" Nick said, and the question tasted scraped-out and borrowed, the least adequate words in the language arranged in their least adequate order.

"Mai's caregiver costs twenty-two hundred a month," Bao said. He delivered it with the precision of a color assessment — a fact about the world, undecorated. "Social Security covers the apartment. The pension covers food. The caregiver was this job."

Nick nodded. The sun was on the back of his neck. The daffodil was behind him.

"My daughter's in Portland. She's offered to help. Two kids and a mortgage and she shouldn't have to, but she's offered." He adjusted his glasses. "For a week or two I shaved a minute off every batch. Then a pantry image would come in with a backward R on the rice label and the sugar label stretched too wide, and there went twelve minutes. I was not going to call that fine."

He turned his face toward the 880, where the traffic was still crawling. The March light picked up the silver stubble along his jaw — Bao had shaved that morning, as he always did, but five hours of fluorescent lights and an HR interview had left him looking less neat than Nick had ever seen him.

"Forty years I've been looking at images," Bao said. "Typography. Packaging. Signage." He touched the closed trunk lid with his fingertips. "Last week there was a kitchen batch. Tile pattern that wrapped the wrong direction around a corner. I spent eight minutes on that one image." He was quiet for a beat. "They wanted a number. I kept giving them paragraphs."

"I know," Nick said.

Bao didn't answer that. He looked past Nick at the traffic on Davis for a while. "I left the notes in the drawer. The handwritten ones." He touched his shirt pocket, where Sandra's note sat. "I should have brought them. I wasn't thinking clearly in there."

He said it without suspicion and without any knowledge that the audit had a human origin. His numbers had been slow. The system had flagged them. That was the shape of it, from where Bao stood.

"Take care of yourself," Bao said. He closed the trunk with both hands, pressing it until the latch caught. Then he stopped. "Water the break-room plants. Nobody else remembers."

"I will."

Bao walked to the driver's side, opened the door, got in. The Camry started on the first try — maintained, like everything he maintained — and pulled out of the spot and turned right toward the exit and right onto Davis Street and merged into the 880 on-ramp traffic and was gone, one tan car among hundreds.

Nick stood in the parking lot with the sun on his face and the warm asphalt under his shoes. Through the second-floor window, the gray-blazer woman was still visible, still typing.

He went back inside.

***

The floor had changed in the twenty minutes he'd been outside. Bao's desk was already mostly cleared — the chair pushed in, the surface wiped clean except for the HR packet laid square in the middle. The desk looked like it had never been occupied. Only the lower file drawer sat a fraction shy of fully closed. The break-room plants Bao had asked him to water were visible through the doorway, two small ferns in plastic pots on the counter by the microwave, their fronds slightly curled from inconsistent care. Nobody on the floor had known they were Bao's project. Nick hadn't known either, until ten minutes ago in a parking lot.

But there was new paper on the floor, too. Nick could see the edges of formal letters on other people's desks as he walked to his station, the same HR letterhead he'd seen in Manila, some laid square and some half-hidden under palms.

Dani was in the stairwell.

He could hear her voice through the fire door — not the words, just the register. Tight and controlled, the specific voice she used for one kind of phone call. He'd heard it through this same door months ago, back when the custody hearings were still theoretical and her lawyer still said things like *strong position* and *no immediate concerns*. The voice was different now, higher and faster, with the control fraying at its edges.

Nick stood in the hallway. He didn't go through the door. He didn't walk away.

Tommy was at his desk with a probation letter in front of him — a single page of HR letterhead, the language visible at a glance: *formal notice... quality deviation... performance improvement plan... sixty-day review period*. Tommy's pre-existing evaluation inconsistencies — normal-range variation that would've earned a coaching conversation in any other quarter — had been swept into the audit. On paper they looked like part of the corrupted pattern. In reality they were Tommy being twenty-two and sometimes rushing through a batch because the per-task throughput targets were set by someone who'd never rated an image.

Nick stopped at Tommy's desk.

"You OK?"

"Oh yeah, no, I'm great." Tommy was clicking his pen, rapid-fire. "Sixty-day performance improvement plan. I love that they call it that. An improvement plan. Like I'm gonna emerge from this a better version of myself." The clicking got faster. "Before: guy who sometimes rated too fast because the targets are set at fucking impossible. After: guy who rates at exactly the sanctioned speed because his rent depends on it. Real growth narrative."

"Tommy."

"My mom called, by the way. Not about this — she doesn't know, I wouldn't — she called about my cousin's wedding. In August. Wanted to know if I'm bringing someone." He stopped clicking. Looked at the probation letter on his desk. "I can't make my loan payment if this turns into a termination. I've done the math a lot. Like, a lot. The deferment ended in January and the minimum's four-sixty a month and if I lose this job I'm just..." He trailed off, his mouth still slightly open around the word he didn't say. "Anyway. It's sixty days. I'll hit the numbers."

Nick didn't know what to say to that. The list of things he didn't know how to say was getting longer by the hour.

"You want some coffee?" he said.

"Yeah. Sure."

He got the coffee. Two cups from the break room's communal Folgers, burnt and institutional, the specific flavor of a place that had been designed for function and nothing else. The break-room ferns were drooping slightly. He filled a mug with water and poured it into the larger pot without thinking about it, and then stood there for a second wondering why his hands were doing that. He brought Tommy's coffee back to his desk and Tommy took it without looking up.

The fire door opened. Dani walked past Nick to her desk without looking at him. Her phone was in her hand at her side. Her jaw was locked so hard it changed the shape of her face, and she kept her eyes on the desk as if getting back to work fast enough might keep the call from becoming real.

She sat down, opened the evaluation portal, and began rating.

"Dani," he said.

"Not now." She didn't look up. Her cursor moved in short exact diagonals. She paused over each score just long enough to make sure it was right, then clicked.

He stood there long enough to see her left hand, the one not on the mouse, picking at the paint residue under her thumbnail — the residue from the murals she hadn't painted in years, the last physical trace, which she'd never fully scraped away.

"The restructuring letter came while you were outside." She pulled up the next image — a generated landscape with a horizon line that broke in two places. "My lawyer says if payroll drops me for even a week, his attorney stands up in court and calls it instability. Doesn't matter that they call this restructuring. Doesn't matter if they put me back on payroll by Friday." She clicked a rating and moved to the next image. "The filing's in three weeks."

She rated two more images while he stood there. A kitchen interior with a shadow that floated without a source object. A portrait with a jaw that hinged at the wrong angle. She caught the floating shadow, caught the broken jaw hinge, and moved on. Her jaw stayed tight. The cursor didn't waver.

"Luna's spring break is in two weeks," she said, still not looking at him. "I was going to take two days off. The aquarium. She's been asking since January." She clicked and pulled up the next image. "I don't think I can take days off right now."

She didn't say anything else. The silence ended the conversation. She kept clicking and didn't look at him again.

He wanted to say something that wasn't empty. Nothing came. The words were all wrong or all useless and he couldn't tell the difference anymore.

"OK," he said.

He went to his desk and rated images for an hour: kitchen interiors, then a batch of architectural exteriors — storefronts and apartment buildings and a generated streetscape with a vanishing point that converged in the wrong place. The floor was quieter than he'd ever heard it. Nobody joked. Nobody drifted toward the break room. People worked with their shoulders set and their eyes fixed on the screens, as if getting caught looking away would count against them.

Later that afternoon, he got up to use the bathroom.

The men's room was down the hall to the left, past the conference room where the gray-blazer woman was still visible through the blinds, still typing. The women's room was on the right. He'd walked past it a thousand times without registering it as more than a door — the same way the fire extinguisher and the exit sign and the carpet stain near the elevator had all become invisible through repetition, background elements the eye stopped rendering.

He heard it through the wall.

It wasn't crying. It was fast, ragged, effortful breathing, the sound a body makes when the autonomic system has seized the controls and the conscious mind has lost the ability to regulate even the most basic function. It was almost gasping, and underneath it, quieter, a faucet was running — turned on to cover the sound and not covering it.

He stopped.

A soft thud came through the wall — a palm hitting a countertop, or a forehead dropping against a forearm. Then the breathing got worse. It went faster and shallower, the rhythm fragmenting into hiccups and raw inhalations that sounded like they cost something. He could hear her trying to slow it — a longer breath held for a beat and then lost immediately to the next wave. The faucet ran. The fluorescent tube above him buzzed its constant indifferent frequency.

He knew it was Priya. Not from the sound but from the elimination of every other possibility — who was left, who was still in the building, who'd spent the last year being compressed between the quality standards she'd built and the throughput demands that ground them down and the team she couldn't protect. The team lead who'd left a teaching career after her body made this same sound in a different bathroom, who'd taken this job because it was supposed to be the quiet one, the recovery position, the one that wouldn't follow her home at night. And now the investigation had taken Bao — the evaluator she'd fought for in six months of performance reviews, whose slow speed she'd documented in careful memos as *deliberate quality* rather than *deficiency* — and threatened Dani and put Tommy on notice, and the woman who'd managed all of it with stacking sentences and procedural composure and a Costco-sized bottle of antacids was in the bathroom with the faucet on and her breathing breaking apart.

Nick stood in the hallway. The conference room was behind him. The fire door was ahead. The men's room sat to his left and the women's to his right. The HVAC pushed its flat conditioned whisper through the ceiling vents. From somewhere on the floor, a Slack notification chimed — the sound so familiar it barely registered as sound anymore, just another ambient pulse of the building's nervous system. His brain was doing the thing it did now, the thing it had been trained to do — scanning the hallway the way he'd scan a generated image, noting the lighting, three overhead fluorescents with one slightly yellower than the others, a consistency issue, the corridor perspective receding to the fire door, the carpet stain as an anomalous element in an otherwise uniform field. He was scoring a hallway while a woman broke down six feet away. The rubric didn't have a category for that, either.

He couldn't go in. He couldn't undo it. The confession was right there — his lips parted, his lungs filled — and then the air left without words, as it had been doing for months, because the truth had become a shape his mouth couldn't form.

On the other side of the wall, the breathing began to slow. Not because it was better, but because that was what the body did — found a floor, a survivable rate, a rhythm that would keep the blood moving even when the mind couldn't hold itself together. After a minute the faucet shut off. After another minute he heard paper towels being pulled from the dispenser one at a time, the small mechanical click of the holder with each sheet.

He walked away.

He went back to his desk. The evaluation portal was still open and the batch was still waiting, the five-point scale laid out in its usual row. A fresh image sat in the queue as if Bao had not just driven away, as if Dani were not at her desk measuring every click against a court date, as if Tommy's letter and Priya's breathing belonged to some other floor.

The next image in the queue was a generated sunset over water — two light sources that contradicted each other, a horizon that bent where physics wouldn't allow it, reflections that bore no relation to the objects above them. His hand stayed on the mouse long enough for the cursor to drift to the lower corner of the screen. He dragged it back, wiped his thumb on his jeans, and stared at the water until the contradictions separated themselves: a second light source where none belonged, a horizon bent against physics, reflections broken loose from the objects above them.

He marked it down. Then he clicked Next.


# Chapter 18

When HR came back around about the workstation, Bao's desk was already mostly cleared — he'd done it himself that morning, stacking his things into the cardboard box with the same deliberate care he brought to everything. Dani had volunteered to finish clearing it out, Nick had said he'd help, and now they were here in the mid-afternoon quiet of the evaluation floor, pulling open drawers that Bao had already been through.

The top drawer held a phone charger that wasn't Bao's. It had been there since before he started, one of those universal artifacts of office furniture that belonged to no one and would outlast everyone. Dani set it on the desk. The second drawer was empty except for a handful of paper clips and a rubber band shaped like a figure eight. The third drawer was deeper, designed for hanging files, and when Nick pulled it open he found a stack of loose pages, handwritten, that Bao had either forgotten or decided didn't belong to him anymore.

Nick picked up the top sheet. Bao's handwriting was small and precise — a graphic designer's lettering, each character formed with the same attention he'd given to his evaluations. The note was about a training image, or had started that way. At the top he'd written the batch number and a description: *Garden scene, residential, midday sun, generated.* Below that, where the rubric asked for a rating and perhaps a brief comment, Bao had written three dense paragraphs about the quality of green in the foliage — how the model had defaulted to a single hue across every leaf when even a backyard garden in July held fifteen or twenty distinguishable greens, and how the shadow beneath the rosebush was blue-gray when it should have been a warm violet because the reflected light from the soil would tint it, and how the picket fence in the background was rendering as flat matte when any real painted wood in direct sun would show the grain and at least two values where the brush strokes caught the light differently.

The note ended with a single number circled at the bottom: 3.

Nick turned to the next page. Another batch number, another image. This one described a portrait — *Woman, mid-thirties, indoor lighting, profile.* Bao's notes ran to the bottom of the page and continued on the back. He had written about the color of the woman's skin in shadow, how the model had grayed it out when real skin in shadow went warm — ochre or amber depending on the undertone — and how the ear, translucent at the portrait's angle against a window, should have shown the red of blood under cartilage, like a held-up hand against a strong lamp. He had written about the particular flatness of the generated hair, each strand identical in value, and the absence of the stray flyaway hairs that real indoor lighting caught and turned into small bright filaments against a dark background. At the bottom, another circled number: 3.

"What are those?" Dani had stopped pulling paper clips out of the carpet where they'd spilled and was looking at the pages in Nick's hands.

"His evaluation notes."

She came over and he handed her the top sheet. She read it standing, her weight on one foot, in the tired posture he knew from long shifts. He watched her eyes move across Bao's handwriting.

"He wrote these for every image?"

"Looks like it. There's maybe forty pages in here."

Dani read the paragraph about the greens. Her mouth pulled tight into a small hard knot, the corners drawn in as if she were holding something behind her teeth. She set the page down on the desk, carefully, aligning it with the edge.

"He didn't have to do that," she said. "None of this goes in the system. The comment field takes — what, two sentences? Three?"

"Something like that."

"And he wrote all this. Every time. For nobody."

"For himself, I think."

Dani picked up the second page, the one about the portrait, and read it with the same focused attention she brought to her own evaluations. The spring light from the window behind her caught the fine hairs along her temple, and Nick noticed it automatically, before the thought formed, before he could stop the part of his brain that had been scoring ambient illumination for fourteen months. The light was consistent. Single source, indirect, high color temperature. He registered the assessment and let it pass through him like weather.

"This is better than anything the rubric asks for," Dani said. "This is what the work should actually look like."

Nick didn't answer. He was looking at the stack — page after page of Bao's observations, each one a record of a man who had seen more than the system wanted from him and written it down anyway, knowing that no one would read it, knowing that his per-image time was already flagged, knowing that this precision was the thing that would cost him his job and doing it anyway because the alternative was seeing less than he could see. Bao's handwriting on the pages was steady and unhurried, the pen strokes of someone who did not adjust his pace for anyone's metrics.

The confession was in Nick's hands with the pages. It started below language, like the nausea on the shoulder of 880 the night he finished, a pressure climbing behind his sternum and into the base of his throat, as if his body were trying to force the words out ahead of him. He could tell her. Dani was standing three feet away, holding Bao's notes with both hands, her face doing the thing it did when she was keeping herself together for reasons that were professional and economic and legal and entirely beyond her control, and he could open his mouth and say *I did this. The contamination they're looking for — it was me. The audit, the firing, all of it*.

"I did," he said, and stopped there.

Dani looked up from the page.

The rest of it hit the back of his teeth and broke apart there — *this, it, the audit, Bao* — every version worse once it existed in air.

"I should've said something sooner," he said.

Dani looked at him and kept looking, steady and level, longer than the quick bad-day glance people traded on the floor.

"If you're trying to say something else, say it."

The fluorescent hum sat between them. On the far side of the floor somebody laughed at a monitor, then stopped. Nick felt the first two words of the sentence gather and with them the rest of it — HR, investigation, the calibration team torn open, Dani in some conference room with her work badge clipped to the blouse she wore to court. He looked past her at Bao's bare desk.

"He shouldn't have lost this job," he said.

"OK," Dani said.

She took the rest of the notes from his hands, slid them into her tote under the folder with Luna's horse drawings, and pushed the bag strap higher on her shoulder.

"Then don't start."

She moved to the other side of the workstation and pulled the monitor toward her by its base. "I've got the drawers. Can you wipe the monitor?"

They finished clearing the station in near-silence, wiping down the desk with the disinfectant spray HR had left, pushing the chair in, adjusting the monitor so it faced straight forward for whoever would sit there next. The new hires on the floor — kids, mostly, hired during the latest expansion to fill the seats the audit had emptied — clicked through their batches at a pace Bao would have found indecent. One of them, a guy in a baseball cap who couldn't have been older than twenty, was rating an image every nine seconds. Nick could see the screen from where he stood. The guy wasn't even scrolling down to check the lower quadrants.

Dani saw it too. She didn't say anything. She picked up the disinfectant spray and the roll of paper towels and walked them back to the supply closet without looking at the kid's screen or at Nick.

---

Priya's office was a glass-walled room at the end of the evaluation floor, designed to communicate transparency while providing none. The blinds were half-drawn. Through the slats, Nick could see the floor's remaining stations — thirty-odd, twelve of them empty since the restructuring, the rest occupied by the mix of survivors and new arrivals that defined the current team. The HVAC hummed, a steady frequency he'd stopped hearing months ago and now heard again.

She sat behind her desk with a coffee mug cupped in both hands, steam curling off the surface while it cooled untouched. Her eyes were raw at the rims, pink threaded through the whites, the look of too many mornings that had started before the alarm because her brain wouldn't stay down. The Costco-sized antacid bottle on her desk was nearly empty, maybe two inches of chalky tablets visible through the plastic.

"I wanted to talk to you about something," she said. "Not — your numbers are fine. Everything's fine with your work."

"OK."

"I'm thinking about leaving Canopy."

She said it in the voice she used for rehearsed things — organized, clear, the former teacher's instinct for structure imposing itself even on a surrender. But the sentence cracked between *thinking* and *leaving*, her voice dropping into a register she usually kept private.

"Like quitting?"

"I can't do this anymore, Nick. I'm not — the team's half the size it was three months ago. The people who are left don't trust the process, and the new people don't know what they're doing because we're onboarding them too fast to actually train them, and Marcus is asking me for quality numbers that I can't produce because the people who produced those numbers are gone." She set the mug down. Coffee sloshed onto a stack of printouts she didn't seem to notice. "Bao's gone. Tommy's on probation for ratings that were within normal range six months ago and only look off now because the whole baseline shifted after the — after everything. Dani's position, I don't know if it survives the next budget review. And I'm sitting here running a quality program on half the people and twice the targets and writing the same reports with different numbers and pretending that's."

She stopped. Picked up the mug. Put it down.

"I keep thinking about April when I was teaching," she said. "Not one student. The whole month. Everybody realized at once how far behind they were, and I'd stay up making make-up packets and alternate assignments and little rescue ladders because if I organized it hard enough maybe nobody had to fail. I think I'm doing that here now. More reports, more process, more explanations, and meanwhile Bao is gone and Dani's scared and I'm still acting like if I phrase the problem correctly it turns back into something manageable."

Nick sat in the chair across from her desk, the one evaluators sat in for quarterly reviews, the armrest fabric worn thin from the fidgeting hands of people being told their throughput was below target. The sentence he could have said sat hard against his teeth. If he told her now, the spreadsheet behind her monitor would stop being a staffing problem and become evidence. He drew breath.

Priya's phone started buzzing across the desk, rattling toward the coffee mug. At the same time her monitor flashed a Teams banner over the staffing sheet: MARCUS WEBB: NEED RECOVERY PLAN BY 5.

She caught the phone with one hand and shut her eyes once. "Sorry. Sorry, one sec."

She rubbed one eye with the heel of her hand, then turned her monitor toward him. A staffing sheet filled the screen: names, budget colors, blocks of training sessions lined up through next week like missing teeth.

"Marcus wants a recovery plan by five," she said. "If I leave now, this goes to ops, and ops will treat it like a scheduling issue. If I stay through rollout, I need calibration coverage in the room for the new-hire labs, the edge-case reviews, the anchor rebuild packets. Not every session. Just enough that I'm not up there by myself explaining lighting drift to sixty people who got hired because we broke the system."

Nick looked at the grid. Monday and Tuesday were already stacked with sessions titled RECOVERY REVIEW and FAILURE MODES. A yellow note sat beside one staffing line: HOLD SENIOR FLOOR IF CROSS-FUNCTIONAL SUPPORT CONFIRMED. Dani's name was on that line.

"You want me in those?"

"I want somebody I trust in those," Priya said. "You, if you'll do it. If I can tell Marcus calibration is covering the first two weeks, I can argue against another senior-floor cut. Dani's on that list."

He could still have told her. He could've pointed at the grid and said the hole she was building a bridge over had his shape. Instead he looked at Dani's name in yellow and answered the question she'd put in front of him.

"Yeah," he said. "I'll do it."

Priya was already reaching for her keyboard. Relief changed her speed before it changed her face.

"OK. Thank you. I can work with that."

His phone buzzed before he stood. Then again. Calendar invites dropped into next week in a fast blue column: RECOVERY TRAINING, ESCALATION REVIEW, NEW-HIRE LAB. Priya clicked him into each block with one hand while the other rested on the antacid bottle.

---

The quality-review meeting was Thursday afternoon, in the conference room Marcus used when he visited San Leandro. The room had a long table, a whiteboard no one ever wrote on, and a window overlooking the parking lot and the strip of chain restaurants beyond it — a Subway, a Panda Express, their signs catching the spring sunlight at an angle that made the orange-and-green color scheme look briefly like a deliberate composition.

Marcus was already seated when Nick arrived. Laptop open, a printed spreadsheet in front of him with cells highlighted in yellow and pink. He wore the waxy, overtight look of a man living on airport coffee and short nights, with enough discipline to keep the damage below the collar line. His oxford was untucked as always, but wrinkled at the collar in a way that suggested he'd been living out of a bag. The watch — the expensive one, the one he touched when he was thinking or stressed or both — caught the overhead light as he shifted in his chair.

"Nick. Thanks for making time. Close the door?"

Nick closed the door. The latch clicked and the room sealed itself into its particular quiet — HVAC hum, the muffled tapping from the floor below, the faint vibration of trucks on the 880 overpass half a mile east.

"I'll be direct," Marcus said. "I've been going through the calibration metrics from the last eight months. The aggregate view is easy enough to read." He turned the laptop screen toward Nick. A scatter plot, dense with data points, a trend line sloping in a direction Nick could read. "The contamination we identified in Q4 — the Manila issue — that accounts for maybe thirty percent of the variance. There's another component we haven't sourced. A systematic bias in certain categories. Anatomical scoring. Lighting consistency."

He paused. The pause was precise, and Nick recognized it — Marcus leaving space like a good interviewer, letting silence do the asking.

"I've noticed the new evaluators struggling with those categories," Nick said. This was true. It sidestepped what Marcus was asking about.

"The bias predates the new hires. It's in the calibration anchors from Q3 and Q4 last year."

Marcus hit another key. The scatter plot disappeared. A table replaced it — batch IDs, category tags, comment snippets. Nick recognized two of the clusters immediately. One lighting set from October where he'd widened the acceptable range until dead green started passing as natural variation. One anatomy set where he'd kept the numbers plausible and let the commentary do the poisoning.

Nick's hands were on the table. He looked at them. The paint calluses were still visible if you knew where to look — faint ridges on his thumb and forefinger where the brush handles had pressed for years. The small scar on his right index finger from an X-Acto knife he'd slipped with in his twenties. He pressed his palms flat against the laminate. The surface was cool and faintly gritty under his skin.

"Have you seen anything in the calibration data that looked off?" Marcus said. "Anything. This is a calibration conversation, Nick. I'm asking because you've been in those anchors for months. If something was off, you might've seen it."

He rotated the laptop a little farther. One of the comment fragments on the screen was his. Only the contour of the sentence remained, but it was enough for recognition.

"Does this read like intent to you," Marcus said, "or drift?"

Nick went very still.

The true answer was short. He could've said it in one breath.

"It reads like patch drift," he said. "Bad category boundaries. Rushed anchor swaps. Examples that should've been retired staying live because nobody had time to rebuild them."

Marcus watched him for a second that felt longer than it was. "You'd put your name on that?"

Nick heard himself answer before the rest of him caught up. "Yes."

Marcus slid the printed spreadsheet across the table.

"Then you own the cleanup. Every anatomy and lighting anchor in these clusters. Replacement rationales, downstream impact, anything that needs to be quarantined before the announcement tomorrow. I want it by nine."

Nick put his hand on the paper. The highlighted cells were warm from Marcus's printer.

"One other thing," Marcus said. "The board approved a major expansion. Five hundred new evaluators, three new offices — Austin, Raleigh, and an expansion here. Company announcement goes out tomorrow." He leaned back. The chair creaked. "The argument I've been making for three years — that the human layer's the most critical part of this infrastructure — turns out the board needed a catastrophic quality failure to agree with me. But they agreed." He touched the watch. "Five hundred people. Biggest investment in human evaluation we've ever made."

"That's a lot of people."

"It's what we need. The corrupted anchors have to be rebuilt from scratch. New calibration framework, new quality controls, the whole pipeline. Two quarters, maybe three. It's going to be a grind, but when it's done we'll have the most robust evaluation infrastructure in the industry." He said it with a worn, personal conviction, stripped of the smoothness of his public remarks, the voice of a man who had watched something he believed in get wrecked and was now being given the resources to rebuild it.

"That's good," Nick said. "That should help."

They shook hands when he stood. Marcus's handshake was the same as always — firm, warm, an executive's trained gesture carrying just enough real feeling to blur the performance. Nick walked back down the stairwell, past the window that looked out over the parking lot. Beyond the lot, past the business park's perimeter of sad ornamental shrubs, the embankment along the freeway was green from the winter rains, the kind of April green Bao would've broken into six notes instead of one.

Back at his station, Marcus's file was already in his inbox with the subject line Q3-Q4 CLUSTERS - NICK OWNERSHIP. Priya's training invitations sat directly underneath it, solid blue blocks consuming next week. He opened the first suspect batch. The archive pane showed the old anchor note clipped to a fragment, but it was enough. He dropped the cursor into the replacement field and typed: *shadow temperature inconsistent across planes; vegetation compressed to single synthetic value; retire from lighting anchor set*. He flagged the batch for quarantine. The system answered with a green checkmark and routed the replacement packet into tomorrow's onboarding queue.

Dani passed behind him on her way to the printer. Her tote hung from her shoulder, Luna's drawings sticking out of the top. She glanced once at the red RECOVERY label across his screen and kept walking. At the printer she stood with her back to him until the pages came out, then carried them to Priya's office without looking over.

---

His apartment was warm for April. He'd stopped opening the windows months ago, around the time the smell of linseed oil and turpentine had faded from the room and there was nothing left to ventilate. The canvases from his last show at the Temescal gallery were still stacked against the wall by the door — the same paintings that had been scraped from the gallery's website and fed into a training pipeline and rated by his coworkers on a five-point scale and rated by Nick himself at four out of five because five would have flagged.

He had set up to paint. He'd been sitting in front of the setup for forty minutes, which was less time than in December, when he'd lasted an hour before producing a technically competent and completely dead canvas he'd scraped off and thrown in the trash. The folding easel he'd bought at Blick Art Materials when he still thought of himself as someone who bought art supplies. A pre-stretched canvas, sixteen by twenty, a size he used to work at without thinking and now found large in a way he couldn't explain. The paints were on the side table in the order he'd always kept them: whites on the left, earths in the middle, cadmiums and phthalos on the right. A jar of turpentine he'd had to dig out from under the bathroom sink. Two brushes — both filbert, a six and a ten — pulled from the coffee can where they stood bristle-up like dead stems.

The canvas was white.

Outside, the April evening was doing something he would have painted two years ago. The light going amber through the bay window, long shadows from the power lines falling across the building opposite, a warm underglow where the late sun hit the BART elevated track at the angle that turned the concrete golden for about fifteen minutes before the whole thing went to gray. The stucco wall across the street was catching bounce light from somewhere — the roof of a parked car, maybe — and the reflected patch was a different temperature than the ambient, warmer, slightly orange against the wall's cool blue shadow. It was the kind of light problem he'd spent years solving on canvas. He could still see it. His eyes still worked, still separating values and temperatures, still catching the warmer patch against the wall's cool blue shadow.

But when he looked at the canvas and tried to find what he would put on it — the first mark, the commitment to a particular image — the decisions arrived backward. The finished thing came to him already botched. The power lines tugged the eye too hard left. The amber on the BART track had to agree with the wall's shadow temperature or the light source would break. The orange bounce off the parked car had to stay quiet enough to keep the whole scene from going synthetic. He could see the errors in advance in fine, specific detail. A painting never cohered.

He picked up the size-six filbert. The handle was worn smooth where his thumb and forefinger had pressed for years — a groove specific to his grip, the wood darkened and slightly concave, the handle itself a physical record of ten thousand hours of painting. He held it high, near the ferrule, as his instructor at the California College of the Arts had taught him, saying a brush held high gave more range and less control, and less control was usually what you wanted.

He dipped the brush in turpentine. Touched it to the cadmium yellow. Lifted it toward the lower left corner, where a shape usually declared itself first.

The yellow gathered at the tip, trembled once, and slid back toward the ferrule. Solvent cooled his knuckles while the brush hovered an inch short of the gesso.

He set the brush down. The bristle left a crescent of cadmium yellow on the palette — a warm, saturated comma of pigment he would not use.

His laptop was on the kitchen counter, screen dark, lid still open from that morning. The Canopy evaluation portal was bookmarked, password saved. The interface was more familiar to him than the easel or the window. He knew which queues timed out first, which comment boxes clipped long notes, which tabs opened the archive without throwing a permissions warning.

The company-wide Slack notification had gone out that afternoon, as Marcus had said it would. The CEO's message, subject line: *Investing in Our Human Layer.* Five hundred new evaluators. Three new offices. "The events of this past quarter," the CEO had written, in the particular cadence of executive communication that sounded both personal and legally reviewed, "have demonstrated more clearly than ever that human judgment is the irreplaceable foundation of trustworthy AI. We are making the largest investment in human evaluation in Canopy's history, because this work, your work, is what makes everything else possible."

Nick had read it at his station on the floor, surrounded by what the message meant. Bao's station wiped down and waiting for whoever came next. Dani sitting a little differently in her chair, as if she didn't trust it to stay hers. Tommy finishing probation ratings with stripped-down care, his pen sitting still beside the keyboard.

Before he left, Marcus had sent the calibration team the first recovery queue. *FAILURE MODES. PRIORITY REVIEW. Anchor candidates for new-office onboarding.*

Under it sat Marcus's follow-up with the spreadsheet attached and Priya's calendar invites eating through next week in solid blocks of blue.

Nick stood up from the stool. He walked to the counter and woke the screen.

His phone lit again beside the keyboard. Priya had moved one of Monday's labs earlier. Marcus had added a note to the spreadsheet: START WITH LIGHTING CLUSTERS. The notifications stacked until the lock screen went blue. Nick turned the phone facedown and laid his paint rag over it. The rag smelled like old solvent and dust.

He logged in.

The portal opened on a different queue than the one he used every day. RECOVERY REVIEW. Q3-Q4 FAILURE MODES. 247 images awaiting evaluation. He opened the first one.

A landscape. Green hills under late light, arranged in moves he knew too well: low horizon, road tugging the eye out of frame, sky worked past weather into texture. The vanishing point drifted. The grass held one dead green from the front edge to the hill's shadow.

His thumb left a faint streak of cadmium yellow on the trackpad.

He clicked the rating fields. He gave it a 3 for compositional balance, a 2 for lighting consistency, a 4 for stylistic coherence. In the comment field he typed, *vanishing point drift, overworked sky, single-value grass*. He submitted the note.

The next recovery image loaded. A hand with six fingers.

Behind him, the canvas stayed white. A skin formed on the cadmium yellow. He clicked the anatomical-accuracy field.
